Productive Moods in Disjunctive Conditionals
Author explains the productive moods for the three types of disjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of disjunctive conditionals and apply their respective valid inference rules.

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals have different rules: māni'at al-jam' only yields from affirming one part, māni'at al-khuluw only from denying one part, and the real disjunctive yields all four.

Disjunctive conditionals (al-munfaṣila) come in three types: preventive of conjunction, preventive of void, and the real disjunctive. Each type permits different valid inferences. The author explains that māni'at al-jam' only produces a conclusion when one part is affirmed, māni'at al-khuluw only when one part is denied, and the real disjunctive produces conclusions in all four cases.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the three types of disjunction
The three types are: māni'at al-jam' (prevents conjunction of the two parts), māni'at al-khuluw (prevents the absence of both), and ḥaqīqiyya (prevents both conjunction and void).
The author notes that the disjunctive here is exclusive (inadīya), meaning the parts are truly opposed.
2
Determine the inference pattern for māni'at al-jam'
Since the two parts cannot both be true, affirming one part necessarily denies the other. However, denying one part does not affirm the other because both could be false.
Example: either this is a tree or a stone (cannot be both). If it is a tree, it is not a stone. But if it is not a tree, it could be a stone or something else.
3
Determine the inference pattern for māni'at al-khuluw
Since the two parts cannot both be false, denying one part affirms the other. However, affirming one part does not deny the other because both could be true.
Example: either this is not a stone or not a tree (cannot be neither). If it is not not a tree, then it is not a stone. But if it is not a stone, it could also be not a tree? Actually careful: the example uses double negation.
4
Determine the inference pattern for the real disjunctive
Since it prevents both conjunction and void, it permits all four logical operations: affirming one denies the other, and denying one affirms the other.
Example: either this number is even or odd. If it is even, it is not odd; if it is not even, it is odd; etc.

Source Text

فصل: الاستثنائى ينتج مع المتصلة وضع المقدم ورفع التالي ومع الحقيقية. قوله «الاستثنائى» اى القياس الاستثنائى و هو الذى تكون النتيجة مذكورة فيه بمادته و هيئته ابداً يتركب من مقدمة شرطية و مقدمة حملية يستثنى فيها عين احد جزئى الشرطية او نقيضه لينتج عين الآخر او نقيضه فالاحتمالات المتصورة فى انتاج كل استثنائى اربعة : وضع كل و رفع كل لكن المنتج فى كل قسم شيء. وتفصيله ما افاده المصنف: من ان الشرطية ان كانت متصلة ينتج منها احتمالان لان وضع المقدم ينتج وضع التالى لاستلزام تحقق الملزوم تحقق اللازم ورفع التالى ينتج رفع المقدم لاستلزام انتفاء اللازم انتفاء الملزوم واما وضع التالى فلا ينتج وضع المقدم ولا رفع المقدم ينتج رفع التالي الجوازان يكون اللازم اعم فلا يلزم من تحققه تحقق الملزوم ولامن انتفاء الملزوم انتفائه . وقد عرفت من .. :هذا ان المراد بالمتصلة فى هذا الباب اللزومية واعلم ايضاً: ان المراد بالمنفصلة هيهنا العنادية و ان كانت الشرطية منفصلة. فمانعة الجمع تنتج من وضع كل جزء رفع الاخر لامتناع اجتماعهما ولا تنتج من رفع كل جزء وضع الآخر لعدم امتناع الخلو بينها ومانعة الخلو بالعكس و اما الحقيقية فلما اشتملت على منع الجمع و الخلومعاً تنتج فى الصور الاربع النتائج الاربع. قوله «وضع المقدم و رفع التالى»: نحو: ان كان هذا انساناً كان حيواناً لكنه انسان فهو حيوان لكنه ليس بحيوان فهو ليس بانسان قوله « والحقيقية»: كقولنا : اما ان يكون هذا العدد زوجاً او فرداً لكنه زوج فليس بفرد لكنه فرد فليس بزوج لكنه ليس بفرد فهو زوج لكنه ليس بزوج فهو فرد. قوله « كمانعة الجمع»: نحو: اما ان يكون هذا شجراً او حجراً لكنه شجر فليس بحجر لكنه حجر فليس بشجر. قوله « كمانعة الخلو»: نحو هذا اما لاحجر او لا شجر لكنه ليس بلا شجر فهو لاحجر لكنه ليس بلا حجر فهولا شجر
Section: The conditional [syllogism] produces, with the conjunctive [conditional], the affirmation of the antecedent and the denial of the consequent, and with the real [disjunctive, all four combinations]. His statement: 'al-istithnā'ī' meaning the conditional syllogism, which is that in which the conclusion is mentioned in its matter and form always, composed of a conditional premise and a categorical premise in which is excluded exactly one of the two parts of the conditional or its contradictory, so as to produce exactly the other or its contradictory. The conceivable possibilities in the production of every conditional syllogism are four: the affirmation of each and the denial of each, but the productive in each division is something. Its detail is what the author expressed: that if the conditional is conjunctive, two possibilities produce from it, because affirming the antecedent produces affirming the consequent due to the implication that realization of the antecedent necessitates realization of the consequent, and denying the consequent produces denying the antecedent due to the implication that the non-existence of the consequent necessitates the non-existence of the antecedent. As for affirming the consequent, it does not produce affirming the antecedent, nor does denying the antecedent produce denying the consequent, because the consequent may be more general, so its realization does not necessitate the realization of the antecedent, nor does the denial of the antecedent necessitate its denial. And you have known from this that what is meant by 'conjunctive' in this chapter is the implicative. And know also that what is meant by 'disjunctive' here is the exclusive. And if the conditional is disjunctive, then the 'preventive of conjunction' produces from affirming each part the denial of the other, due to the impossibility of their conjunction, and does not produce from denying each part the affirmation of the other, due to the possibility of the void between them. And the 'preventive of void' is the opposite. As for the 'real', since it includes the prevention of both conjunction and void together, it produces in the four forms the four conclusions. His statement: 'the affirmation of the antecedent and the denial of the consequent': like: if this is a human, it is an animal; but it is a human, therefore it is an animal; but it is not an animal, therefore it is not a human. His statement: 'and the real': like our saying: either this number is even or odd; but it is even, therefore it is not odd; but it is odd, therefore it is not even; but it is not odd, therefore it is even; but it is not even, therefore it is odd. His statement: 'like the preventive of conjunction': like: either this is a tree or a stone; but it is a tree, therefore it is not a stone; but it is a stone, therefore it is not a tree. His statement: 'like the preventive of void': like: this is either not a stone or not a tree; but it is not 'not a tree', therefore it is 'not a stone'; but it is not 'not a stone', therefore it is 'not a tree'.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional syllogism (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī) and explains its productive moods. The author clarifies that a conditional syllogism consists of a conditional premise (sharṭiyya) and a categorical premise (ḥamliyya) in which either one part of the conditional or its contradictory is excluded, yielding the other part or its contradictory. There are four possible combinations: affirming the antecedent, affirming the consequent, denying the antecedent, denying the consequent. For a conjunctive conditional (muttasila), only two are productive: affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, and denying the consequent yields the denial of the antecedent. The other two are not productive because the consequent may be more general than the antecedent, as in 'if it is a human, it is an animal' — affirming 'it is an animal' does not imply 'it is a human', and denying 'it is a human' does not imply 'it is not an animal'. The author then notes that the conjunctive in this chapter is the implicative (luzūmiyya), not merely accidental. For disjunctive conditionals (munfaṣila), the productive moods depend on the type: 'preventive of conjunction' (māni'at al-jam') yields only from affirming one part the denial of the other; 'preventive of void' (māni'at al-khuluw) yields only from denying one part the affirmation of the other; the 'real' (ḥaqīqiyya), which prevents both, yields all four combinations. Examples are given for each: for conjunctive, a standard implication from 'human' to 'animal'; for real disjunctive, the even-odd example showing all four possibilities; for preventive of conjunction, 'tree or stone' where affirming one gives denial of the other; for preventive of void, a double negation example. This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn, explaining the conditions under which each type of conditional syllogism produces a valid conclusion.

Key Terms

مانعة الخلو
الحقيقية
العنادية

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive conditionals have distinct inference rules: affirming one part denies the other (māni'at al-jam'), denying one part affirms the other (māni'at al-khuluw), and both are valid for the real disjunctive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Qiyas al-Khalf: Reductio ad Absurdum Structured
Author explains the nature and structure of qiyas al-khalf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a qiyas al-khalf argument by combining a conditional premise with an exceptive syllogism, and identify the two component syllogisms.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf resolves into two syllogisms: one conditional categorical and one exceptive conditional.

Qiyas al-khalf is a powerful form of argument that proves a claim by showing its negation leads to an impossibility. In classical Islamic logic, it is not a single syllogism but a compound structure that resolves into two distinct syllogisms. Understanding this resolution is crucial for analyzing arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh where reductio arguments are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim to prove (al-matlub)
Start with the proposition you want to establish, e.g., 'The world is created' (huduth al-'alam).
2
Assume the contradictory (naqīḍ)
Formulate the opposite: 'The world is not created' (i.e., pre-eternal). This assumption is the starting point of the reductio.
3
Derive an impossibility from the assumption
Construct a conditional syllogism: 'If the world is not created (i.e., pre-eternal), then certain consequences follow that lead to an impossibility (e.g., infinite regress).' This is the first sub-syllogism (iqtirānī sharṭī).
The conditional may itself require proof.
4
Apply the exceptive syllogism (istithnā'ī)
Use the conjunctive conditional: 'If the assumption leads to impossibility, but impossibility is false, therefore the assumption is false.' This yields the denial of the contradictory, affirming the original claim.
The second sub-syllogism is the exceptive (istithnā'ī muttasil) that excludes the consequent.
5
Combine into the full reductio
The two syllogisms together form a compound argument: from 'if not P then Q' and 'if Q then impossible' we get 'if not P then impossible'; then by denying the consequent (impossible) we affirm P.
The text emphasizes that qiyas al-khalf is not one but two syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله « وقد يختص » الخ: اعلم انه قد يستدل على اثبات المدعى بانه لولاه لصدق نقيضه لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين لكن نقيضه غير واقع فيكون هذا واقعاً كمامر غير مرة فى مباحث العكوس والاقيسة و هذا القسم من الاستدلال بالخلف اما يسمى لأنه ينجر الى الخلف اى المحال على تقدير صدق نقيض المطلوب او لانه ينتقل منه الى المطلوب من خلفه اى من ورائه الذي هو نقيضه وليس هذا قياساً واحداً بل ينحل الى قياسين احدهما اقترانی شرطى والاخر استثنائى متصل يستثنى فيه نقيض التالى هكذا لو لم يثبت المطلوب لثبت نقيضه و كلما ثبت نقيضه ثبت المحال ينتج لو لم يثبت المطلوب لثبت المحال لكن المحال ليس بثابت فيلزم ثبوت المطلوب لكونه نفيض المقدم ثم قد يفتقر بيان الشرطية يعنى قولنا : كلما ثبت نقيضه ثبت المحال الى دليل آخر فتكثر القياسات كذا قال المصنف في شرح الاصول . فقوله «و مرجعه الى استثنائى و اقترانی» معناه ان هذا القدر مما لابد منه في كل قياس خلف و قديزيد عليه فافهم. (*) قوله: (وقد يختص باسم قياس الخلف»: اى وقد يختص الاستثنائى باسم قياس الخلف فيسمونه «قياس خلف» لا قياس استثناء. (التقريب ص ١٢٦ - ١٢٧)
His statement: 'And it may be specifically named qiyās al-khalf': Know that sometimes one seeks to prove the claim by [arguing] that if it were not so, its contradictory would be true, because the simultaneous non-existence of two contradictories is impossible, but its contradictory does not obtain, so this [the original claim] obtains, as has passed many times in the topics of conversion and syllogisms. This type of reasoning is called 'by khalf' either because it leads to a khalf (i.e., an impossibility) given the assumption of the truth of the contradictory of the desired, or because one moves from it to the desired from behind it (i.e., from its opposite which is its contradictory). And this is not a single syllogism; rather it resolves into two syllogisms: one categorical conditional, and the other a conjunctive conditional syllogism in which the contradictory of the consequent is excluded. Thus: if the desired is not established, then its contradictory is established; and whenever its contradictory is established, an impossibility is established; this yields: if the desired is not established, an impossibility is established. But the impossibility is not established; therefore the establishment of the desired follows, because it is the contradictory of the antecedent. Then it may be that the demonstration of the conditional (i.e., our statement: whenever its contradictory is established, an impossibility is established) requires another proof, so the syllogisms multiply. This is what the author said in Sharḥ al-Uṣūl. So his statement 'and its reference is to a conditional and a categorical' means that this amount is necessary in every qiyās khalf, and it may be increased, so understand. (*) His statement: 'And it may be specifically named qiyās al-khalf': i.e., the conditional [syllogism] may be specifically named qiyās al-khalf, so they call it 'qiyās khalf' not 'qiyās istithnā'. (al-Taqrīb pp. 126-127)
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of qiyās al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum). The author clarifies that this mode of reasoning is used to prove a claim by showing that its negation leads to an absurdity (khalf = impossible). The name comes either from the fact that it leads to an impossible state or from moving from behind (i.e., from the opposite). Importantly, qiyās al-khalf is not a single syllogism but resolves into two: a categorical conditional (sharṭī iqtirānī) and a conjunctive conditional syllogism (istithnā'ī muttaṣil) where the contradictory of the consequent is excluded. The author outlines the logical structure: assume the denial of the desired claim (the premise 'if not P then not P?' Actually: 'if the desired is not established, its contradictory is established'). Then from that contradictory, an impossibility follows. So we have: if not P, then Q (contradictory of P); and if Q, then impossible. Therefore, if not P, then impossible. But impossible is false, so not P is false, hence P is true. This is the standard reductio structure. The author also notes that the conditional premise (that the contradictory leads to impossibility) may itself require proof, resulting in multiple linked syllogisms. Finally, the author comments on the matn's statement that its reference is to a conditional and a categorical syllogism, meaning that these two are indispensable in every khalf argument. The footnote adds that the conditional syllogism may be specifically named qiyās khalf, distinguishing it from ordinary conditional syllogisms.

Key Terms
قياس الخلف
الخلف
نقيض
اقتراني شرطي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In qiyas al-khalf, what is the logical structure used to prove the desired claim?
A) A single categorical syllogism.
B) A disjunctive syllogism with two premises.
C) A combination of a conditional and a conjunctive conditional syllogism.
D) An immediate inference from the contradiction of the claim.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-khalf is a compound syllogism that proves a claim by showing the impossibility of its negation, resolving into a conditional and an exceptive syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a qiyas al-khalf argument to prove that the Quran is uncreated (ghayr makhluq) in the Ash'ari kalam tradition: assume it is created leads to impossibility (e.g., temporal priority of speech over God). Show the two syllogisms.
?
Identify the two component syllogisms in the following argument: 'If nabidh is not intoxicating, then it would not be forbidden; but every intoxicant is forbidden, so if nabidh is not intoxicating, it would be forbidden? Wait: Use a proper reductio structure.'

The Syllogism

Qiyas al-Khalf: The Reductio as a Compound Syllogism
Author defines qiyas al-khalf and reduces it to a conditional and a categorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decompose a qiyas al-khalf argument into its component conditional and categorical syllogisms and verify its validity.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf is not a primitive syllogism; it is a compound of conditional and categorical reasoning.

In this passage, the author clarifies the structure of qiyas al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum), commonly known as proof by contradiction. While it appears as a single argument, it actually resolves into two syllogisms: a conditional categorical and a connected conditional. Understanding this decomposition helps us see why qiyas al-khalf is valid and how it relates to the standard syllogistic forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired proposition and its contradictory
Locate the matlub (desired proposition) and its naqid (contradictory). For example, if the matlub is 'the world is created', its contradictory is 'the world is not created'.
2
Form the first conditional: if not matlub then contradictory
Express that if the desired proposition is false, its contradictory is true. This is a conditional premise: 'If not P then Q', where P is matlub and Q is naqid.
3
Form the second conditional: if contradictory then absurdity
Show that the contradictory leads to an absurdity (muhal). This is another conditional: 'If Q then R', where R is an absurd conclusion.
4
Combine into a compound syllogism
From the two conditionals, derive 'If not P then R' (hypothetical syllogism). Then, since R is false (absurd), the antecedent 'not P' must be false, so P is true. This is a modus tollens in the conditional, but the author describes it as a compound of conditional and categorical.
The first part (if not P then Q) is a categorical conditional? Actually, the author says one is a conditional categorical (iqtirani sharti) and the other a connected conditional with exception. The compound yields the conclusion.
5
Verify the logical structure
Check that each step is valid: the hypothetical syllogism and the denial of the consequent. The overall argument is valid if both conditionals are true and the absurdity is indeed impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضع كل كمانعة الجمع و رفعه كمانعة الخلو. و قد يختص باسم قياس الخلف وهو ما يقصد به اثبات المطلوب بابطال نقيضه و مرجعه الى استثنائى و اقتراني. فليس بفرد لكنه فرد فليس بزوج لكنه ليس بفرد فهو زوج لكنه ليس بزوج فهو فرد. قوله « كمانعة الجمع»: نحو: اما ان يكون هذا شجراً او حجراً لكنه شجر لكنه حجر فليس بشجر. فليس بحجر قوله « كمانعة الخلو»: نحو هذا اما لاحجر او لا شجر لكنه ليس بلا شجر فهو لاحجر لكنه ليس بلا حجر فلا شجر. قوله « وقد يختص » الخ: اعلم انه قد يستدل على اثبات المدعى بانه لولاه لصدق نقيضه لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين لكن نقيضه غير واقع فيكون هذا واقعاً كمامر غير مرة فى مباحث العكوس والاقيسة و هذا القسم من الاستدلال بالخلف اما يسمى لأنه ينجر الى الخلف اى المحال على تقدير صدق نقيض المطلوب او لانه ينتقل منه الى المطلوب من خلفه اى من ورائه الذي هو نقيضه وليس هذا قياساً واحداً بل ينحل الى قياسين احدهما اقترانی شرطى والاخر استثنائى متصل يستثنى فيه نقيض التالى هكذا لو لم يثبت المطلوب لثبت نقيضه و كلما ثبت نقيضه ثبت المحال ينتج لو لم يثبت المطلوب لثبت المحال لكن المحال ليس بثابت فيلزم ثبوت المطلوب لكونه نفيض المقدم ثم قد يفتقر بيان الشرطية يعنى قولنا : كلما ثبت نقيضه ثبت المحال الى دليل آخر فتكثر القياسات كذا قال المصنف في شرح الاصول . فقوله «و مرجعه الى استثنائى و اقترانی» معناه ان هذا القدر مما لابد منه في كل قياس خلف و قديزيد عليه فافهم.
Each is a prohibit combination [maniat al-jam'] and its opposite is a prohibit emptiness [maniat al-khuluw]. And it may be specifically named 'qiyas al-khalf' (reductio ad absurdum), which is that by which one intends to prove the desired proposition by invalidating its contradictory, and its reduction is to a conditional and a categorical. So it is not an individual but it is an individual, so it is not a pair but it is not an individual, so it is a pair but it is not a pair, so it is an individual. His statement: 'like maniat al-jam'': e.g., 'Either this is a tree or a stone, but it is a tree' or 'but it is a stone, so it is not a tree' or 'so it is not a stone.' His statement: 'like maniat al-khuluw': e.g., 'This is either not a stone or not a tree, but it is not not a tree, so it is not a stone' or 'but it is not not a stone, so it is not a tree.' His statement: 'And it may be specifically named' etc.: Know that one may argue for the proof of the claim by saying that if it were not so, its contradictory would be true because it is impossible for the two contradictories to be false, but its contradictory is not actual, so this one is actual, as has passed many times in the discussions of converses and syllogisms. This kind of argument by reductio is called so either because it leads to the absurd i.e. the impossible on the assumption of the truth of the contradictory of the desired proposition, or because one moves from it to the desired proposition from behind it i.e. from beyond it which is its contradictory. And this is not a single syllogism but it resolves into two syllogisms: one conditional categorical and the other a connected conditional in which the contradictory of the consequent is excluded. Thus: if the desired proposition is not established, its contradictory is established; and whenever its contradictory is established, the absurd is established; this yields: if the desired proposition is not established, the absurd is established; but the absurd is not established, so the establishment of the desired proposition follows, since it is the contradictory of the antecedent. Then the clarification of the conditional (meaning our statement: 'whenever its contradictory is established, the absurd is established') may require another proof, so the syllogisms multiply. Thus said the author in Sharh al-Usul. So his statement: 'and its reduction is to a conditional and a categorical' means that this amount is necessary in every qiyas al-khalf, and it may be increased, so understand.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on qiyas al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum), a type of argument commonly used in logic. The author begins by defining two types of disjunctive premises: maniat al-jam' (prohibit combination, i.e., exclusive disjunction) and maniat al-khuluw (prohibit emptiness, i.e., exhaustive disjunction). He then states that qiyas al-khalf is specifically named for the method where one proves a proposition by invalidating its contradictory. The essence of qiyas al-khalf is that it reduces to a combination of a conditional (sharti) and a categorical (iqtirani) syllogism. The author provides an example with 'individual' and 'pair' to illustrate the logical structure: if something is not an individual, it is a pair; but it is an individual, so it is not a pair etc. This is a convoluted example showing the transformation. Then the author clarifies the two types of disjunction with concrete examples from the text: for maniat al-jam', 'Either this is a tree or a stone' where both cannot be true; for maniat al-khuluw, 'Either not a stone or not a tree' where both cannot be false. Then the author delves into the explanation of qiyas al-khalf: the argument proceeds by assuming the contradictory of the desired proposition, showing that it leads to an absurdity, and therefore the original proposition must be true. This is not a single syllogism but decomposes into two: a conditional categorical (if not P then Q) and a connected conditional (if Q then R), yielding 'if not P then R'. Then the absurdity (R) is negated, so P follows. The author notes that the conditional premise itself may require further proof, leading to multiple syllogisms. The core idea is that qiyas al-khalf is a compound argument, not a primitive form.

Key Terms
قياس الخلف
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
استثنائي
اقتراني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, qiyas al-khalf resolves into how many syllogisms?
A) One syllogism
B) Two syllogisms
C) Three syllogisms
D) Four syllogisms

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-khalf is a compound argument that proves a proposition by showing its contradictory leads to an absurdity, and it reduces to a conditional and a categorical syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Classifying Syllogisms by Premise Material: The Five Arts
Classification of syllogisms into five arts by premise material

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any syllogism into one of the five arts (burhan, jadal, khitaba, shi'r, mughalata) based on the epistemic status of its premises.

Core Idea
The material of premises determines the art of the syllogism.

In this lesson, we learn that syllogisms are not only distinguished by their outward form (conditional vs. conjunctive), but also by the kind of premises they contain. The author presents a classification into five 'arts' that are fundamental in Islamic logic: demonstration, dialectic, rhetoric, poetry, and fallacy. This will help you understand why some arguments are considered certain and others merely persuasive or deceptive.

Guided Path
1
1. Distinguish between form and material
The author first notes that syllogisms are divided by form (sura) into conjunctive and conditional. But here we focus on material (madda): the type of premises.
2
2. Identify the two primary categories
Premises either produce assent (tasdiq) or imaginative representation (takhayyul). Poetry (shi'r) produces takhayyul. All other arts produce tasdiq.
3
3. Subdivide the assent-producing premises
Among those producing tasdiq, some yield opinion (zann) and some certainty (jazzm). Rhetoric (khitaba) yields opinion. Certainty-producing ones are further divided.
4
4. Map the five arts onto the tree
Certainty that is certain (yaqini) gives demonstration (burhan). Certainty based on general acceptance (taslim) gives dialectic (jadal). If deceptive, it is fallacy (mughalata). Now you have all five.
Refer to the flowchart in the commentary for visual learners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: القياس اما برهاني يتألف کار مان اسدی
اقسام القياس باعتبار المادة
قوله « القياس» الخ : القياس كما ينقسم باعتبار الهيئة والصورة إلى استثنائى واقتراني باقسامها، فكذلك ينقسم باعتبار المادة الى الصناعات الخمس (۱) اعنی «البرهان» و الجدل» و «الخطاية» و «شعر» و «المغالطة» و قد تسمى: «سفسطة ايضاً لان مقدماته اما ان تفيد تصديقاً (۲) او تأثيراً آخر غير التصديق اعنى التخييل» والثانى الشعر و الاول اما ان يفيد ظنّاً أو جزماً (۳) فالاول «الخطابة و الثانى ان افاد جزماً يقينياً فهو «البرهان» و الا فان اعتبر فيه عموم الاعتراف من العامة أو التسليم من الخصم فهو «الجدل» والا فـ«المغالطة».
واعلم ان «المغالطة» ان استعملت في مقابلة الحكيم سميت «سفسطة». و ان استعملت في مقابلة غير الحكيم سميت: «مشاغبة» (۴) واعلم ايضاً انه يعتبر فى البرهان ان يكون مقدماته باسرها يقينية بخلاف غيره من الاقسام مثلاً يكفى فى كون القياس مغالطة ان يكون احدى مقدمتيه وهمية و ان كانت الاخرى يقينية. نعم يجب ان لا يكون فيها ما هو ادون منها كالشعريات و الا تلحق بالادون فان المؤلف من مقدمة مشهورة و اخرى مخيلة لايسمى «جدلياً» بل شعرياً» فاعرفه .
Section: The syllogism is either demonstrative...
Divisions of the syllogism with respect to matter
His statement 'the syllogism' etc.: Just as the syllogism is divided with respect to form and figure into conditional and conjunctive with their subdivisions, so too it is divided with respect to matter into the five arts (1), namely 'demonstration', 'dialectic', 'rhetoric', 'poetry', and 'fallacy'. It is also called 'sophistry' because its premises either yield assent (2) or another effect other than assent, namely imaginative representation. The second is poetry. The first either yields opinion or certainty (3). The first (generating opinion) is 'rhetoric'. The second (generating certainty): if it yields certain certainty, it is 'demonstration'. Otherwise, if general acceptance from the public or submission from the opponent is considered, it is 'dialectic'. Otherwise, it is 'fallacy'.
And know that 'fallacy' if used against the sage is called 'sophistry', and if used against the non-sage is called 'disputation' (4). Know also that in demonstration it is required that all its premises be certain, unlike other divisions. For example, it suffices for a syllogism to be a fallacy that one of its two premises be imaginary even if the other is certain. Indeed, it must not contain something lower than that, like poetical premises; otherwise it would be attached to the lower. For if a syllogism is composed of a widely accepted premise and another imaginary premise, it is not called 'dialectical' but 'poetic'. So know this.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage classifies the syllogism (qiyas) according to its material, i.e., the type of premises it employs. The author begins by noting that just as the syllogism is divided by form (hay'a/sura) into conditional (istithna'i) and conjunctive (iqtirani), similarly it is divided by matter into five 'arts' (sana'i'): demonstration (burhan), dialectic (jadal), rhetoric (khitaba), poetry (shi'r), and fallacy (mughalata). The author explains the basis: premises either produce assent (tasdiq) or something else, namely imaginative representation (takhayyul). Poetry produces the latter. Among those producing assent, some yield opinion (zann) and some certainty (jazzm). Those yielding opinion are rhetoric; those yielding certainty are either demonstration if the certainty is certain (yaqini) or dialectic if it is based on general acceptance (taslim) from the opponent, or fallacy if it is merely deceptive. The author further clarifies that fallacy when used against a sage (hakim) is called sophistry (safsata), and when used against a non-sage it is called disputation (mushaghaba). An important point: for demonstration, all premises must be certain; a single imaginary premise makes it fallacious (unless the other is even lower, making it poetic). A mix of widely accepted (mashhura) and imaginary (mukhayyala) yields a poetic syllogism, not dialectical.

البرهان
الجدل
الخطابة
الشعر

Key Takeaway
A syllogism's art is determined by the epistemic quality of its premises: whether they produce certainty, opinion, or imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider a syllogism: 'Most people agree that honesty is good; Zayd is honest; so Zayd is good.' The first premise is widely accepted (mashhura). Which art is this? Answer: jadal (dialectic).

Mixing Premise Types: When Does a Syllogism Become Fallacious or Poetic?
Rules for determining the art when premises mix

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the art of a syllogism when its premises are of different epistemic kinds, applying the rule that the weakest premise dictates the classification.

Core Idea
A single non-certain premise ruins demonstration; the lowest premise determines the art.

This lesson expands on the classification by examining what happens when premises of different types are combined. The author gives specific examples: a mixture of a certain and an imaginary premise yields a fallacy; a mixture of a widely accepted and an imaginary premise yields poetry. Understanding this rule is crucial for evaluating real arguments, which often contain mixed premises.

Guided Path
1
1. Recognize the strict condition for demonstration
The author states that for a syllogism to be burhan, all premises must be certain (yaqiniyya). If even one is less than certain, it cannot be demonstrative.
2
2. Analyze the example of fallacy
If one premise is imaginary (wahmiyya) and the other is certain, the syllogism is a fallacy (mughalata). This shows that a single weak premise corrupts the whole.
3
3. Consider the mixing of lower types
If one premise is widely accepted (mashhura) and another is imaginary (mukhayyala), the syllogism becomes poetic, not dialectical. Why? Because the imaginary premise is lower than the widely accepted one.
The rule: the art follows the weakest premise.
4
4. Apply the rule to other combinations
For instance, a mix of rhetorical (zanni) and certain premises: would it be burhan? No, because the rhetorical premise is not certain. It could be mughalata if the rhetorical premise is deceptive, or possibly jadal if both are widely accepted? The author does not specify all, but the principle is clear: avoid mixing non-certain premises if you aim for demonstration.

Source Text

Divisions of Qiyas by Material: Overview
Author defines the types of qiyas by material and their premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main categories of qiyas by material and their corresponding premises.

Core Idea
The material of a syllogism determines its field: dialectical, poetic, or sophistical.

This lesson introduces the division of qiyas by its matter (maddah), which classifies syllogisms into three types: dialectical (jadalī), poetic (shiʿrī), and sophistical (sūfisṭāʾī). Understanding this division helps you evaluate the epistemic weight of arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the first part of the passage where the author says: 'wa ammā shiʿriyyan yataʾallafu min al-mukhayyalāt, wa ammā sūfisṭāʾiyyan yataʾallafu min al-wahmiyyāt wa al-mushabbihāt.' Notice that there are three categories corresponding to three different materials.
2
Associate each type with its function
The dialectical uses maznūnāt (probable premises), poetic uses mukhayyalāt (imaginative premises), sophistical uses wahmiyyāt and mushabbihāt (fallacious premises).
3
Note the key term shift
The author contrasts maznūnāt with maqbūlāt as general to particular. This is a nuance: maznūnāt here is broader than expected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اقسام القياس باعتبار المادة
وا المظنونات. و اما شعرى يتألف من المخيلات. و اما سفسطى يتألف من الوهميات والمشبهات.
قوله «والمظنونات»: هي قضاياء يحكم بها العقل حكماً راجحاً (۲۸) غير جازم و مقابلته (۲۹) بالمقبولات من قبيل مقابلة العام بالخاص (۳۰) فالمراد به ما سوی الخاص. (۳۱)
قوله من انخيلات» هى قضاياء لا تذعن بها النفس و لكن تتأثر منها ترغيباً وترهيباً (۳۲) (كما اذا قيل: الخمر ياقوتية سيالة تنشط النفس و ترغب بشربها و اذا قيل: العسل مرة مهوعة، انقبضت و تنفرت منه خل و اذا قرن بها سجع او وزن كما هو المتعارف الأن (۳۳) ازداد تأثيراً.

In the divisions of qiyas with respect to matter: And the maznunat. And as for the poetic, it is composed of mukhayyilat. And as for the sophistical, it is composed of wahmiyat and mushabbihat. His statement: 'and the maznunat': They are propositions about which the intellect judges with a preponderant judgment, not decisive. And its contrast with the maqbulat is a case of contrasting the general with the particular, so what is intended by it is other than the particular. His statement: 'of the mukhayyilat': They are propositions which the soul does not assent to, but is affected by them, inciting desire or repulsion (as when it is said: wine is a flowing ruby that enlivens the soul and makes it desire to drink it, and when it is said: honey is bitter and nauseating, it contracts and feels repulsed from it). And if it is accompanied by rhyme or meter, as is customary nowadays, its effect increases. 'Sufista' His statement: 'and as for the sophistical, it is attributed to sufsata, which is derived and Arabized from the Greek word 'sufasṭa', meaning 'specious, deceptive wisdom'. His statement: 'from wahmiyat': They are propositions about which the estimative faculty judges regarding non-sensible things, by analogy with sensible things, as it is said: every existent is spatial. His statement: 'and the mushabbihat': They are false propositions that resemble primary or widely-known true propositions, due to verbal or conceptual confusion. And know that what the later scholars mentioned regarding the five arts is a limited treatment; they summarized and neglected it, despite it being among the important matters, and they lengthened discussion on conditional conjunctions and their implications with little benefit. And upon you is to study the books of the ancients, for in them is the cure for the ill and deliverance from distress.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on a matn that divides qiyas by its material. The author first enumerates three types: maznunat (likely part of dialectical), shi'ri (poetic) composed of mukhayyilat, and sufsati (sophistical) composed of wahmiyat and mushabbihat. Then he comments on each term. The maznunat are propositions with preponderant but non-decisive judgment, contrasted with 'maqbulat' (accepted propositions) as general to particular. The mukhayyilat are propositions that do not gain assent but affect the soul emotionally, e.g., describing wine as a flowing ruby to entice, or honey as bitter to repulse; rhyme and meter enhance the effect. The sophistical type is attributed to the Greek word meaning deceptive wisdom. The wahmiyat are propositions judged by estimative faculty on non-sensibles via analogy to sensibles, e.g., 'every existent is spatial'. The mushabbihat are false propositions that resemble true primary or widely-known propositions due to verbal or conceptual ambiguity. Finally, the author criticizes later scholars for neglecting the five arts and over-focusing on conditional syllogisms, recommending study of classical works. This passage is definitional, introducing key terms for the material of syllogisms, while also containing critical remarks.

المخيلات
الوهميات
المشبهات

Al-Maznunat: Premises for Dialectical Syllogisms
Author defines al-maznunat as probable propositions and contrasts them with maqbulat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-maznunat and distinguish them from al-maqbulat.

Core Idea
Maznunat are probable premises – the intellect's non-decisive judgment.

In this lesson, we examine al-maznunat, the material of dialectical syllogisms. The author defines them as propositions judged with preponderance but without certainty, and contrasts them with maqbulat as general to particular.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition
Locate the phrase: 'هي قضايا يحكم بها العقل حكماً راجحاً غير جازم' – propositions judged with preponderant but non-decisive judgment.
2
Understand the contrast with maqbulat
The passage says مقابلة بالمقبولات من قبيل مقابلة العام بالخاص. That means maznunat is a broader category that includes maqbulat and others.
3
Apply to Islamic reasoning
How would a jurist use maznunat? For example, a qiyas based on probable 'illah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في اقسام القياس باعتبار المادة
وا المظنونات. و اما شعرى يتألف من المخيلات. و اما سفسطى يتألف من الوهميات والمشبهات.
قوله «والمظنونات»: هي قضاياء يحكم بها العقل حكماً راجحاً (۲۸) غير جازم و مقابلته (۲۹) بالمقبولات من قبيل مقابلة العام بالخاص (۳۰) فالمراد به ما سوی الخاص. (۳۱)
قوله من انخيلات» هى قضاياء لا تذعن بها النفس و لكن تتأثر منها ترغيباً وترهيباً (۳۲) (كما اذا قيل: الخمر ياقوتية سيالة تنشط النفس و ترغب بشربها و اذا قيل: العسل مرة مهوعة، انقبضت و تنفرت منه خل و اذا قرن بها سجع او وزن كما هو المتعارف الأن (۳۳) ازداد تأثيراً.
«سوفسطا»
قوله «و اما سفسطى منسوب الى سفسطة و هي مشتقة معرب «سوفا اسطا لغة يونانية يعنى: الحكمة المموهة المدلسة (٣٤) قوله «من الوهميات»: هي القضاياء (۳۵) التي يحكم بها الوهم في غير المحسوس (۳۶) قياساً على المحسوس كما يقال: كل موجود فهو متحيز. قوله « والمشبهات»: هي القضاياء الكاذبة الشبيهة بالصادقة الاولية (۳۷) او المشهورة لاشتباه لفظی او معنوی (۳۸)
واعلم ان ما ذكره المتأخرون فى الصناعات الخمس اقتصار محل قد اجملوه واهملوه مع كونه من المهمات وطوّلوا فى الاقترانات الشرطيات ولوازم الشرطيات مع قلة الجدوى وعليك بمطالعة كتب القدماء فان فيها شفاء العليل ونجاة الغليل.
In the divisions of qiyas with respect to matter: And the maznunat. And as for the poetic, it is composed of mukhayyilat. And as for the sophistical, it is composed of wahmiyat and mushabbihat. His statement: 'and the maznunat': They are propositions about which the intellect judges with a preponderant judgment, not decisive. And its contrast with the maqbulat is a case of contrasting the general with the particular, so what is intended by it is other than the particular. His statement: 'of the mukhayyilat': They are propositions which the soul does not assent to, but is affected by them, inciting desire or repulsion (as when it is said: wine is a flowing ruby that enlivens the soul and makes it desire to drink it, and when it is said: honey is bitter and nauseating, it contracts and feels repulsed from it). And if it is accompanied by rhyme or meter, as is customary nowadays, its effect increases. 'Sufista' His statement: 'and as for the sophistical, it is attributed to sufsata, which is derived and Arabized from the Greek word 'sufasṭa', meaning 'specious, deceptive wisdom'. His statement: 'from wahmiyat': They are propositions about which the estimative faculty judges regarding non-sensible things, by analogy with sensible things, as it is said: every existent is spatial. His statement: 'and the mushabbihat': They are false propositions that resemble primary or widely-known true propositions, due to verbal or conceptual confusion. And know that what the later scholars mentioned regarding the five arts is a limited treatment; they summarized and neglected it, despite it being among the important matters, and they lengthened discussion on conditional conjunctions and their implications with little benefit. And upon you is to study the books of the ancients, for in them is the cure for the ill and deliverance from distress.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on a matn that divides qiyas by its material. The author first enumerates three types: maznunat (likely part of dialectical), shi'ri (poetic) composed of mukhayyilat, and sufsati (sophistical) composed of wahmiyat and mushabbihat. Then he comments on each term. The maznunat are propositions with preponderant but non-decisive judgment, contrasted with 'maqbulat' (accepted propositions) as general to particular. The mukhayyilat are propositions that do not gain assent but affect the soul emotionally, e.g., describing wine as a flowing ruby to entice, or honey as bitter to repulse; rhyme and meter enhance the effect. The sophistical type is attributed to the Greek word meaning deceptive wisdom. The wahmiyat are propositions judged by estimative faculty on non-sensibles via analogy to sensibles, e.g., 'every existent is spatial'. The mushabbihat are false propositions that resemble true primary or widely-known propositions due to verbal or conceptual ambiguity. Finally, the author criticizes later scholars for neglecting the five arts and over-focusing on conditional syllogisms, recommending study of classical works. This passage is definitional, introducing key terms for the material of syllogisms, while also containing critical remarks.

D) They are unrelated

Key Takeaway
Al-maznunat are probable propositions, broader than maqbulat.

Al-Mukhayyilat: Premises for Poetic Syllogisms
Author defines al-mukhayyilat as emotionally evocative propositions used in poetic syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the nature and function of al-mukhayyilat in poetic syllogisms.

Core Idea
Mukhayyilat affect the soul without requiring assent.

Poetic syllogisms use mukhayyilat – propositions that stir emotions rather than produce belief. This lesson unpacks their definition and examples, including the role of rhyme and meter.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
'هي قضايا لا تذعن بها النفس و لكن تتأثر منها ترغيباً وترهيباً' – propositions the soul does not assent to but is affected by.
2
Analyze the examples
Wine described as a flowing ruby (رغبة), honey as bitter (نفور). Notice these are not factual but evocative.
3
Note the role of poetic devices
Rhyme and meter increase impact. The author mentions that nowadays it's customary.

Al-Wahmiyat and Al-Mushabbihat: Premises for Sophistical Syllogisms
Author defines al-wahmiyat and al-mushabbihat as false premises used in sophistical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-wahmiyat and al-mushabbihat as the material of sophistical syllogisms and explain why they are fallacious.

Core Idea
Wahmiyat misapply sense-based analogies; mushabbihat deceive by resemblance to truth.

Sophistical syllogisms use false premises that appear true. The author distinguishes two subtypes: wahmiyat (judgments of the estimative faculty based on analogy to sensibles) and mushabbihat (false propositions that mimic primary or widely-known truths). This lesson examines both.

Guided Path
1
Define wahmiyat
'هي القضايا التي يحكم بها الوهم في غير المحسوس قياساً على المحسوس' – judgments about non-sensibles by analogy to sensibles. Example: 'كل موجود فهو متحيز' (false).
2
Define mushabbihat
'هي القضايا الكاذبة الشبيهة بالصادقة الأولية أو المشهورة لاشتباه لفظي أو معنوي' – false propositions resembling true ones due to verbal or conceptual confusion.
3
Contrast with mukhayyilat
Whereas mukhayyilat don't seek assent, wahmiyat and mushabbihat are presented as true but are false.

Source Text

His statement: 'and the maznunat': They are propositions about which the intellect judges with a preponderant judgment, not decisive. And its contrast with the maqbulat is a case of contrasting the general with the particular, so what is intended by it is other than the particular. His statement: 'of the mukhayyilat': They are propositions which the soul does not assent to, but is affected by them, inciting desire or repulsion (as when it is said: wine is a flowing ruby that enlivens the soul and makes it desire to drink it, and when it is said: honey is bitter and nauseating, it contracts and feels repulsed from it). The Author's Critique of Later Logicians: Neglect of the Five Arts and Overemphasis on Conditionals
Author criticizes later logicians' treatment of the five arts and recommends studying classical works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to summarize the author's criticism of later logicians and his recommendation to study classical works.

Core Idea
Later logicians neglected the five arts and overemphasized conditional syllogisms.

The author concludes with a sharp critique: later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirūn) have treated the five arts superficially, summarizing and neglecting important details while expanding on conditional syllogisms with little benefit. He urges students to study the works of earlier masters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target
Read: 'ما ذكره المتأخرون في الصناعات الخمس اقتصار محل قد أجملوه وأهملوه' – they limited, summarized, and neglected the five arts.
2
Identify what they overdid
'وطولوا في الاقترانات الشرطية ولوازم الشرطيات مع قلة الجدوى' – they lengthened discussion on conditional conjunctions and implications with little benefit.
3
Recognize the recommendation
'وعليك بمطالعة كتب القدماء فان فيها شفاء العليل ونجاة الغليل.' – study the ancients' books.

The author first enumerates three types: maznunat (likely part of dialectical), shi'ri (poetic) composed of mukhayyilat, and sufsati (sophistical) composed of wahmiyat and mushabbihat. Then he comments on each term. The maznunat are propositions with preponderant but non-decisive judgment, contrasted with 'maqbulat' (accepted propositions) as general to particular. The mukhayyilat are propositions that do not gain assent but affect the soul emotionally, e.g., describing wine as a flowing ruby to entice, or honey as bitter to repulse; rhyme and meter enhance the effect. The sophistical type is attributed to the Greek word meaning deceptive wisdom. The wahmiyat are propositions judged by estimative faculty on non-sensibles via analogy to sensibles, e.g., 'every existent is spatial'. The mushabbihat are false propositions that resemble true primary or widely-known propositions due to verbal or conceptual ambiguity. Finally, the author criticizes later scholars for neglecting the five arts and over-focusing on conditional syllogisms, recommending study of classical works. This passage is definitional, introducing key terms for the material of syllogisms, while also containing critical remarks.

The Syllogism

The Methods of Composition and Analysis in Syllogisms
Author explains construction and analysis of syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a categorical syllogism from a given conclusion using composition (taqsīm) and determine the figure of a given syllogism using analysis (taḥlīl).

Core Idea
Composition builds syllogisms from the conclusion; analysis reveals their figure.

This lesson introduces two complementary methods: taqsīm (composition) for constructing a syllogism from the terms of the conclusion, and taḥlīl (analysis) for identifying the figure of a syllogism when it is given in non-standard form. These tools, drawn from the commentary tradition on logic, are essential for ensuring that your arguments are both valid and properly structured according to the four figures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion and its extremes
Start with the desired conclusion (the 'demand'). Clearly define its subject (al-mawḍūʿ) and predicate (al-maḥmūl). These are the two extremes that must be connected by a middle term.
Example conclusion: 'The world is created.' Subject: al-ʿālam, predicate: ḥādith.
2
Composition: enumerate possible terms
List all subjects and predicates of each extreme, including mediate and immediate relations, and also terms negated from the extremes. This 'multiplication from above' generates candidate premises.
For 'world', consider subjects/predicates like 'changing', 'temporal', 'substance'.
3
Check relations to determine the figure
Examine the lists: if a predicate of the subject is the subject of the predicate, first figure; if a predicate of the subject is predicated on the predicate, second figure; if a subject of the subject is a subject of the predicate, third figure; if a predicate of the predicate is predicated on the predicate, fourth figure. Apply quantity and quality conditions.
The four arrangements correspond to the standard figures.
4
Analysis: given a syllogism, find its figure
First, check if any premise shares both parts of the conclusion (then it is conditional) or only one part (then it is conjunctive). Identify the minor term (subject of conclusion) and major term (predicate). Then join the other part of the conclusion to the other part of the shared premise. If they combine in one of the four arrangements, the middle term is found and the figure is identified. If not, the syllogism is compound.
This reverse process confirms the syllogism's validity and structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The gloss: And it (i.e., the method of division) is division, meaning: multiplication from above, and analysis is its opposite. His saying: 'And it is division' — what is meant by it is also called 'composition of the syllogism', and that is by saying: When you want to obtain a demand from among the assertive demands, place the two extremes of the demand and seek all the subjects of each one of them and all the predicates of each one of them, whether the attribution of the two extremes to them and their attribution to the two extremes is mediate or immediate. Likewise seek all that from which one of the two extremes is negated or that which is negated from one of them. Then look at the relation of the two extremes to the subjects and predicates. If you find among the predicates of the subject of the demand that which is the subject of its predicate, then the demand is obtained from the first figure; or what is predicated on its predicate, then from the second figure; or from among the subjects of its subject what is a subject of its predicate, then from the third figure; or a predicate of its predicate, then from the fourth figure. All that after considering the conditions according to quantity and quality. Thus in Sharḥ al-Maṭāliʿ. And the author expressed this meaning by his saying: 'meaning multiplication' — i.e., multiplication of premises, taking from above, i.e., from the conclusion because it is the ultimate aim in relation to the proof. His saying: 'And analysis' — In Sharḥ al-Maṭāliʿ, often syllogisms that produce the demands are presented in the knots (?) not in logical forms due to the composer's laxity, relying on the cleverness of one knowledgeable in the rules. So if you want to know from which figure it is, you must use analysis, which is the opposite of composition. Obtain the demand and look at the productive syllogism. If there is in it a premise that shares with the demand both its parts, then the syllogism is conditional (istithnāʾī). If it shares with the demand one of its parts, then the syllogism is conjunctive (iqtirānī). Then look at the two extremes of the demand so that the minor and major become distinguished to you, because that part — if it is subject to judgment in the conclusion, then it is the minor; or if it is judged by it, then it is the major. Then join the other part of the demand to the other part of that premise. If they combine in one of the four arrangements, then what is joined to the two parts of the demand is the middle term, and the productive figure is distinguished. If they do not combine, then the syllogism is compound. So operate on each one of them with the mentioned operation, i.e., place the other part of the demand and the other part of the premise as you placed the two extremes of the demand in division. For it is necessary that each one of them have a relation to something in the syllogism; otherwise the syllogism would not be productive for the demand. Then if you find a term...
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a detailed gloss from the hashia explaining two of the four instructional methods: division (taqsīm) and analysis (taḥlīl), specifically as applied to syllogisms. It begins by clarifying that 'division' here means 'composition of the syllogism' (tarkīb al-qiyās), which is the method of constructing a syllogism from the terms of the desired conclusion. The procedure is: take the two extremes (subject and predicate) of the conclusion, then list all the subjects and predicates of each extreme, as well as all terms from which one extreme is negated or which are negated from it. Then examine the relationship between the extremes and these lists. If you find that a predicate of the subject of the conclusion is the subject of the predicate of the conclusion, you have a first figure syllogism. If a predicate of the subject is predicated on the predicate, it is second figure. If a subject of the subject is a subject of the predicate, third figure. If a predicate of the predicate is a predicate of the predicate (i.e., predicated on the predicate), fourth figure. This is after considering the conditions of quantity and quality. The author calls this 'multiplication from above' because it multiplies premises starting from the conclusion (the 'above'). The second method, analysis, is its opposite. Given a syllogism that may not be in standard logical form, you analyze it to determine its figure. First, check if any premise shares both parts with the conclusion — if so, the syllogism is conditional (istithnāʾī). If it shares only one part, it is conjunctive (iqtirānī). Then, identify the minor and major terms in the conclusion (the subject is the minor, predicate the major). Then, take the other part of the conclusion (the one not in the shared premise) and join it to the other part of the shared premise. If they combine in one of the four valid arrangements (i.e., produce a valid syllogistic figure), then the middle term appears and the figure is identified. If not, the syllogism is compound (multiple syllogisms). This method ensures that every syllogism can be reduced to a standard figure. An Islamic example: to prove 'The world is created', let the conclusion be 'The world is created'. Subject: al-ʿālam (world), predicate: ḥādith (created). List subjects/predicates: 'everything that changes is created', 'the world changes', etc. Then find a middle term like 'changing' that links the extremes.

التحليل

القياس الاقتراني

Key Takeaway
Composition and analysis are inverse methods that allow you to construct and evaluate categorical syllogisms systematically, ensuring your arguments are logically valid.

Al-Taqsim: Constructing Syllogisms by Division
Author explains taqsim (division) method for constructing categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of taqsim to construct a valid categorical syllogism in any of the four figures given a desired conclusion.

Core Idea
Taqsim systematically searches for the middle term by exhaustively listing possible subject-predicate relations.

When you have a conclusion you want to prove, you don't just guess premises. Classical logicians devised taqsim (division) to systematically find the correct middle term and figure. This passage from al-Yazdi's Hashiya explains the method in detail, drawing on Sharh al-Matali'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms of the conclusion
Place the subject and predicate of the desired conclusion (al-matlub). These are the two extremes between which you seek a middle term.
The conclusion is an assertoric proposition (tasdiqiyya).
2
List all subjects and predicates of each term
For each term, collect everything that can be its subject or predicate, including negative relations (ma suliba 'anhu). Also consider whether the terms are predicated on others or others on them, with or without a mediator.
The text explicitly includes negations: 'wa kadhālika uṭlub jamīʻa mā suliba ʻanhu aḥadu al-ṭarafayn'.
3
Examine the relations to match a figure pattern
Look at the relations between the collected terms. Four patterns correspond to the four figures: (1) among predicates of the subject, find something that is the subject of the predicate → first figure; (2) predicate of the predicate → second figure; (3) subject of the subject that is subject of the predicate → third figure; (4) predicate of the predicate → fourth figure.
These patterns assume the conditions of quantity and quality (e.g., universal affirmative, etc.) are respected.
4
Verify the syllogism's validity
Once you have identified the figure, ensure the premises satisfy the conditions for that figure (e.g., in the first figure, the minor premise must be affirmative and the major universal). Then the syllogism is complete.
The author says 'kullu dhālika ba‘da i‘tibār al-sharā’iṭ bi-ḥasab al-kammiyya wa al-kayfiyya'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is al-taqsim, meaning: multiplication from above and analysis, and it is its opposite. His statement: 'And it is al-taqsim' — what is meant by it is what is also called 'composition of the syllogism'. And that is by saying: If you want to obtain a demand from among the assertoric demands, place the two terms of the demand and seek all subjects of each of them and all predicates of each of them, whether the two terms are predicated upon them or they are predicated upon the two terms with or without a mediator, and similarly seek everything from which one of the two terms is negated or from which it is negated by one of them. Then look at the relation of the two terms to the subjects and predicates. If you find, from among the predicates of the subject of the demand, something that is the subject of its predicate, then the demand has been obtained from the first figure; or [if it is] what is predicated upon its predicate, then from the second figure; or [if it is] from among the subjects of its subject something that is the subject of its predicate, then from the third figure; or [if it is] a predicate of its predicate, then from the fourth. And look at the productive syllogism: if it contains a premise that shares with the demand both of its parts, then the syllogism is conditional; and if it shares with the demand one of its parts, then the syllogism is categorical. Then look at the two terms of the demand to distinguish for you the minor from the major, because that part — if it is the subject of judgment in the conclusion, then it is the minor; or if it is the predicate in it, then it is the major. Then join the other part of the demand to the other part of that premise. If they are composed according to one of the four compositions, then what is attached to the two parts of the demand is the middle term, and the productive figure is distinguished. If they are not composed, then the syllogism is composite; so apply to each of them the mentioned operation, i.e., place the other part of the demand and the other part of the premise just as you placed the two terms of the demand in division. For each of them must have a relation to something in the syllogism, otherwise the syllogism would not be productive of the demand. If you find a term common between them, then the syllogism is complete and the premises, figures, and conclusion are made clear to you. So his statement: 'And it is its opposite' i.e., multiplication of premises to above, and it is the conclusion, as has been narrated.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of two methods related to syllogisms: al-taqsim (division) and al-tahlil (analysis). The author begins by stating that al-taqsim is also called 'composition of the syllogism' (tarkib al-qiyas). The method involves: if you seek to prove a assertoric demand (matalib tasdiqiyya), you first place the two terms (subject and predicate) of the desired conclusion. Then you seek all subjects and predicates of each term, considering both positive and negative relations. You then examine the relations to find which figure of the syllogism yields the conclusion. Specifically, if you find among the predicates of the subject of the conclusion something that is the subject of its predicate, it is the first figure. If it is a predicate of the predicate, second figure; if among subjects of the subject something that is subject of its predicate, third figure; if a predicate of the predicate, fourth figure. All this after considering the conditions of quantity and quality. This method is called 'multiplication from above' because it starts from the conclusion (the ultimate goal). Then the author discusses analysis (al-tahlil), which is the opposite of composition. It is used to identify the figure of a given syllogism when the syllogism is presented in a non-standard form. The analyst first distinguishes whether the syllogism is conditional (istithna'i) or categorical (iqtirani) based on whether a premise shares both parts of the conclusion or only one. Then, by examining the terms, the minor and major premises are identified, and the middle term is found by composing the remaining parts. If the syllogism is composite (murakkab), the procedure is applied to each component. The passage concludes by noting that al-tahlil is the reverse of al-taqsim: the latter multiplies premises upward from the conclusion, while the former breaks down a syllogism to find its structure.

Al-Tahlil: Analyzing Syllogisms to Identify Figures
Author explains tahlil (analysis) method for identifying syllogistic figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a given categorical or conditional syllogism using tahlil to determine its figure, distinguish minor from major, and identify the middle term.

Core Idea
Tahlil is the reverse of composition: it breaks a non-standard syllogism into its parts to reveal its logical structure.

In many theological texts, syllogisms are presented in abbreviated or non-standard forms due to the author's reliance on the reader's expertise. To verify their validity, you need tahlil (analysis). This passage explains how to perform tahlil: first determine if the syllogism is conditional or categorical, then find the minor and major terms, and finally locate the middle term by composing the remaining parts.

Guided Path
1
Check if the syllogism shares both parts of the conclusion
Look at the premises. If one premise contains both the subject and predicate of the conclusion, the syllogism is conditional (istithna'i). If it shares only one part, it is categorical (iqtirani).
This step is from the text: 'فان كان فيه مقدمة تشارك المطلوب بكلا جزئيه والقياس استثنائى و ان كانت مشاركة للمطلوب باحد جزئيه فالقياس اقترانى'.
2
Distinguish the minor and major premises
Identify the two terms of the conclusion: the subject (mahkum 'alayh) is the minor term, and the predicate (mahkum bih) is the major term. The premise containing the minor term is the minor premise; the one containing the major term is the major premise.
The text says: 'انظر الى طرفي المطلوب ليتميز عندك الصغرى عن الكبرى لأن ذلك الجزء ان كان محكوم عليه فى النتيجة فهي الصغرى أو محكوماً به فيها فهى الكبرى'.
3
Join the other parts to find the middle term
Take the other part of the conclusion and join it with the other part of the premise. If they form one of the four standard compositions (patterns of first, second, third, or fourth figure), then the term common to both is the middle term, and the figure is identified.
If they do not form a standard pattern, the syllogism is composite (murakkab), and you must apply the procedure to each component.
4
For composite syllogisms, repeat the analysis
If the syllogism is composite, treat each part as a separate syllogism and apply the same steps: place the other part of the demand and the other part of the premise as you did in taqsim. Look for a common term between them.
The text: 'و ان لم يتألفا كان القياس مركباً فاعمل بكل واحد منهما العمل المذكور'.

وهي التقسيم اعنى: التكثير من فوق والتحليل و هو عكسه. قوله وهى التقسيم كان المراد به ما يسمى «تركيب القياس» ايضا و ذلك بان يقال: اذا اردت تحصيل مطلب من المطالب التصديقية ضع طرفى المطلوب و أطلب جميع موضوعات كل واحد منهما و جميع محمولات كل واحد منهما سواء كان حمل الطرفين عليها وحملها على الطرفين بواسطة او بغير واسطة وكذلك اطلب جميع ما سلب عنه احد الطرفين او سلب هو عن احدهما ثم انظر الى نسبة الطرفين الى الموضوعات و المحمولات فان وجدت من محمولات موضوع المطلوب ما هو موضوع المحموله فقد حصل المطلوب من الشكل الأول أو ما هو محمول على محموله فمن الشكل الثانى او من موضوعات موضوعه ما هو موضوع محموله فمن الشكل الثالث او محمول لمحموله فمن الرابع كل ذلك بعد اعتبار الشرايط بحسب الكمية و الكيفية كذا فى شرح المطالع . وقد عبر المصنف عن هذا المعنى بقوله: اعنى التكثير اى تكثير المقدمات اخذاً من فوق اى من النتيجة لانها المقصد الاقصى بالنسبة الى الدليل. قوله « والتحليل»: في شرح المطالع كثيراً ما يورد في العنود قياسات منتجة للمطالب لا على الهيئات المنطقية لتساهل المركب اعتماداً على الفطن العالم بالقواعد فإن اردت ان تعرف انه على اى شكل من الاشكال فعليك بالتحليل و هو عكس التركيب حصل المطلوب وانظر أن القياس المنتج ، فان كان فيه مقدمة تشارك المطلوب بكلا جزئيه والقياس استثنائى و ان كانت مشاركة للمطلوب باحد جزئيه فالقياس اقترانى ثم انظر الى طرفي المطلوب ليتميز عندك الصغرى عن الكبرى لأن ذلك الجزء ان كان محكوم عليه فى النتيجة فهي الصغرى أو محكوماً به فيها فهى الكبرى ثم ضم الجزء الآخر من المطلوب الى الجزء الاخر من تلك المقدمة فإن تألفا على احد التأليفات الاربع فما انضم الى جزئى المطلوب هو الحد الاوسط ويتميز الشكل المنتج و ان لم يتألفا كان القياس مركباً فاعمل بكل واحد منهما العمل المذكور اى ضع الجزء الاخر من المطلوب والجزء الآخر من المقدمة كما وضعت طرفى المطلوب فى التقسيم فلابد ان يكون لكل واحد منهما نسبة الى شيء مما في القياس والا لم يكن القياس منتجاً للمطلوب فان وجدت حداً مشتركاً بينهما فقد تم القياس وتبين لك المقدمات والاشكال والنتيجة فقوله: «و هو عكسه» اى تكثير المقدمات الى فوق و هو النتيجة كما مروجهه.
And it is al-taqsim, meaning: multiplication from above and analysis, and it is its opposite. His statement: 'And it is al-taqsim' — what is meant by it is what is also called 'composition of the syllogism'. And that is by saying: If you want to obtain a demand from among the assertoric demands, place the two terms of the demand and seek all subjects of each of them and all predicates of each of them, whether the two terms are predicated upon them or they are predicated upon the two terms with or without a mediator, and similarly seek everything from which one of the two terms is negated or from which it is negated by one of them. Then look at the relation of the two terms to the subjects and predicates. If you find, from among the predicates of the subject of the demand, something that is the subject of its predicate, then the demand has been obtained from the first figure; or [if it is] what is predicated upon its predicate, then from the second figure; or [if it is] from among the subjects of its subject something that is the subject of its predicate, then from the third figure; or [if it is] a predicate of its predicate, then from the fourth. All that after considering the conditions regarding quantity and quality. Thus in Sharh al-Matali'. And the author expressed this meaning by his statement: 'meaning multiplication' i.e., multiplication of premises taken from above, i.e., from the conclusion, because it is the ultimate goal relative to the proof. His statement: 'And analysis' — in Sharh al-Matali': Often in [the discussion of] contumacy, syllogisms productive of demands are presented not in logical forms due to the laxity of the composer, relying on the clever person knowledgeable of the rules. If you want to know upon which figure it is, you must perform analysis, which is the opposite of composition. The demand has been obtained. And look at the productive syllogism: if it contains a premise that shares with the demand both of its parts, then the syllogism is conditional; and if it shares with the demand one of its parts, then the syllogism is categorical. Then look at the two terms of the demand to distinguish for you the minor from the major, because that part — if it is the subject of judgment in the conclusion, then it is the minor; or if it is the predicate in it, then it is the major. Then join the other part of the demand to the other part of that premise. If they are composed according to one of the four compositions, then what is attached to the two parts of the demand is the middle term, and the productive figure is distinguished. If they are not composed, then the syllogism is composite; so apply to each of them the mentioned operation, i.e., place the other part of the demand and the other part of the premise just as you placed the two terms of the demand in division. For each of them must have a relation to something in the syllogism, otherwise the syllogism would not be productive of the demand. If you find a term common between them, then the syllogism is complete and the premises, figures, and conclusion are made clear to you. So his statement: 'And it is its opposite' i.e., multiplication of premises to above, and it is the conclusion, as has been narrated.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of two methods related to syllogisms: al-taqsim (division) and al-tahlil (analysis). The author begins by stating that al-taqsim is also called 'composition of the syllogism' (tarkib al-qiyas). The method involves: if you seek to prove a assertoric demand (matalib tasdiqiyya), you first place the two terms (subject and predicate) of the desired conclusion. Then you seek all subjects and predicates of each term, considering both positive and negative relations. You then examine the relations to find which figure of the syllogism yields the conclusion. Specifically, if you find among the predicates of the subject of the conclusion something that is the subject of its predicate, it is the first figure. If it is a predicate of the predicate, second figure; if among subjects of the subject something that is subject of its predicate, third figure; if a predicate of the predicate, fourth figure. All this after considering the conditions of quantity and quality. This method is called 'multiplication from above' because it starts from the conclusion (the ultimate goal). Then the author discusses analysis (al-tahlil), which is the opposite of composition. It is used to identify the figure of a given syllogism when the syllogism is presented in a non-standard form. The analyst first distinguishes whether the syllogism is conditional (istithna'i) or categorical (iqtirani) based on whether a premise shares both parts of the conclusion or only one. Then, by examining the terms, the minor and major premises are identified, and the middle term is found by composing the remaining parts. If the syllogism is composite (murakkab), the procedure is applied to each component. The passage concludes by noting that al-tahlil is the reverse of al-taqsim: the latter multiplies premises upward from the conclusion, while the former breaks down a syllogism to find its structure.

Key Terms
التحليل
الحد الأوسط
القياس الاستثنائي
القياس الاقتراني
الصغرى
الكبرى

Key Takeaway
Tahlil is an essential tool for uncovering the logical structure of non-standard syllogisms, enabling the evaluation of their validity.

Applying a Universal Qanun to Derive Particular Rulings
Author explains the mechanism by which a qanun yields rulings for its instances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a categorical syllogism that applies a universal qanun as a major premise to derive a specific ruling for a particular instance.

Core Idea
A qanun is a living premise that, when matched with a minor premise, yields a verdict.

In this passage, the commentator explains the practical mechanism of a qanun: how it generates rulings for its particulars. He uses the grammatical rule 'Every fa'il is marfu' as an example, showing how we derive 'Zayd is marfu' by forming a syllogism. He then addresses an important subtlety: not every derived ruling must be new theoretical knowledge; some may be self-evident, yet the qanun still qualifies as a source of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal proposition (qanun)
Look for the universal claim that will serve as the major premise, e.g., 'Every fa'il is marfu' in the grammar example.
2
Form the minor premise by applying the subject to a particular
Take a specific instance and state that it falls under the subject of the universal, e.g., 'Zayd is fa'il'. This is easily obtained by resolving the subject onto the particular.
3
Construct the categorical syllogism
Combine the premises in a first-figure syllogism: Major premise (Every fa'il is marfu'), Minor premise (Zayd is fa'il), Conclusion (Therefore, Zayd is marfu'). This yields the specific ruling.
This is a valid Barbara syllogism (AAA-1).
4
Assess the epistemological status of the conclusion
The commentator notes that the derived ruling may be self-evident (badihi) rather than theoretical (nazari). For example, instances of 'The first figure is productive' are self-evident. The qanun's function is suitability for generating knowledge, even if that knowledge was already known.
This prevents excluding such self-evident qawanin from the definition.

His statement: 'from which the rulings of the particulars of its subject are known' - and in some manuscripts 'through which one knows' and the meaning is one. Then that is by making that proposition the major premise for a minor premise that is easily obtained, from the minor premise resulting from resolving its subject onto each of the particulars subsumed under that subject, so that the particular rulings that are potentially contained in that proposition are brought out into actuality. For example, in the example the commentator mentioned: we want to know the status of Zayd in 'Zayd struck', for instance, which is a particular of the subject, namely 'fa'il'. So we predicate that subject on him, yielding our statement 'Zayd is fa'il'. We make this the minor premise for that universal proposition and say: 'Zayd is fa'il, and every fa'il is marfu'', thus we get 'Zayd is marfu', and likewise all qawanin of the sciences—apply this and do not be deficient. Then it is not hidden that it is not necessary that the branches subsumed under the qanun be theoretical, as is apparent from his statement 'from which the rulings of the particulars of its subject are known'; otherwise there would be excluded from it their statement 'The first figure is productive', for it is evident that the rulings subsumed under it are self-evident (badihiyya), as was indicated earlier in refuting the famous objection, yet they agreed that it is among the issues of logic. Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage explains the practical application of a qanun. A qanun (universal premise) is used as the major premise of a syllogism. The minor premise is formed by applying the subject of the qanun to a particular instance (e.g., 'Zayd is fa'il'). The conclusion then becomes the specific ruling for that instance (e.g., 'Zayd is marfu'). The author gives the grammatical example: from the qanun 'Every fa'il is marfu' we derive that Zayd is marfu' when we know he is a fa'il. This is the standard operation of a qanun in any science. Then the commentator addresses a nuance: it is not required that the particular rulings be theoretical (nazari); they could be self-evident (badihi). He mentions that the ruling 'The first figure is productive' is a qanun in logic, yet its particular instances (e.g., moods) are self-evident, not theoretical. So the term 'knowing rulings from it' means the qanun is suitable for generating knowledge even if the derived rulings are sometimes known prior to it. This prevents excluding such qawanin from the definition.
