When a Universal Rule Contradicts Itself
Author explains that a qanun may terminate in its contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a universal logical rule (qanun) leads to a contradictory conclusion and explain why this occurs.

Core Idea
A universal rule can yield its own opposite under specific conditions.

The author introduces a subtle point: even the most universal logical principles may, under certain conditions, produce a conclusion that contradicts the principle itself. This is not a sign of invalidity but a recognition of the complexity of logical relationships. In this lesson, we explore what it means for a qanun to end in its opposite.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'qanun' as a universal rule
In Islamic logic, a qanun is a universal proposition or principle that is used as a premise in reasoning. For example, 'every temporally originated thing has a cause' is a qanun. Recognize that such rules are meant to apply broadly.
2
Recognize that applying a qanun may lead to a contradiction
Consider cases where the conclusion derived from the qanun, possibly in combination with another premise, negates the qanun itself. This is what the author means by 'ending in its opposite' (naqid).
The contradiction may be general (fi al-jumla), not necessarily in every instance.
3
Analyze the nature of the contradiction
The author states that the qanun is contradictory to itself 'in general' (fi al-jumla). This suggests the contradiction is not necessarily actual but potential or conditional. Reflect on how this nuance preserves the utility of the qanun.
The commentator does not treat this as a fatal flaw.
4
Apply this concept to an Islamic theology example
In the kalam proof for God's existence, the qanun 'every temporally originated thing has a cause' is used. If one tries to apply it to the eternal Creator, it yields a contradiction. The mutakallimun resolve this by limiting the scope of the qanun to temporal beings.
This shows how a qanun can appear to end in its opposite when misapplied.

The commentator states that this is a 'general contradiction' (munafatun fi al-jumla). The example or further explanation is not included because the page ends. This highlights the subtlety that even universal rules can yield contradictory results under specific conditions, though such cases must be handled carefully.

Contradiction in Syllogistic Reasoning and Certainty of Error
Author explains contradictory conclusions and certainty levels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of opposition that constitute contradiction (naqid) and evaluate the certainty of contradictory claims made by one thinker vs. multiple thinkers.

Core Idea
Contradiction is certain only in one mind; across minds, words may not reflect true beliefs.

When a syllogism ends in a contradictory conclusion, we must understand what counts as contradiction. The author explains that contradiction includes not only affirmation and negation but also privation/possession, real opposition, and correlation. More importantly, the certainty of contradiction depends on whether it arises within one rational being or between different beings. This lesson unpacks these distinctions and their implications for logical error.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the broad definition of naqid
The author says contradiction includes any kind of conflict, such as affirmation/negation, privation/possession, real opposition, or correlation. Understand that 'opposite' is a general term.
2
Distinguish two sources of contradiction
Contradiction can occur between two thoughts from the same person at different times, or between thoughts from different persons. Both are included.
3
Assess certainty in single-person contradiction
When one person holds two contradictory thoughts, the conflict is known intuitively (ilm wijdani) and impossible to doubt. This is the strongest certainty.
4
Assess uncertainty in cross-person contradiction
When different persons contradict, we only infer from their words. They may not genuinely believe what they say; they could be arguing for show or other reasons. Thus, actual contradiction is less certain.
5
Apply to logical error
Error occurs in both conceptualization (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Even definitions (hadd and rasm) are subject to error, as shown by scholarly disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۶۰) قوله و قدینتهى الى نقيضها اراد به ان يكون منافياً له فى الجملة سواء تحقق ذلك التنافي في ضمن الايجاب والسلب او العدم والملكة او التضاد الحقيقى او التضايف او ما اشبه شيئاً من ذلك. (۶۲) ولا يخفى ان هذا اعم من ان يكون احد الفكرين ناشئاً من شخص والاخر من آخر لظهور ان العقلاء يناقض بعضهم بعضاً بمقتضى افكارهم و من ان يكون كلاهما ناشئاً من واحد بحسب اوقات متعددة، ضرورة ان العاقل المفكر اذا رجع وجدانه ربما يفكر ويعتقد حكماً ثم يفكر آخر ويعتقد حكماً آخر مناقضاً للحكم الاول الاثرى ان العلماء المتبحرين ربما تغير مذهب و انتقال رأى بحسب حالات متعددة و اوقات مختلفة؟ . (محمد علی) (۶۳) قوله والالزم اجتماع النقيضين هذا فى غاية الظهور اذا كان الفكر الاخير ناشئاً مما نشأ الفكر الاول لان علمه بان فكره هذا يناقض فكره ذاك علم وجدانى لا يمكن ان يشك فيه بخلاف مالوكان ناشناً من غيره لان حال الغير ليس بهذه المثابة لاحتمال ان يكون فكره المناقض للفكر الاول لغرض من اظهار الفضل والكمال وغيره مما لا يحتمله فكر الشخص الواحد، ولان مناقضة بعض العقلاء بعضاً انما يعلم من الفاظهم الدالة على ان مقتضيات افكارهم متناقضة و يحتمل انهم لم يعتقدوا ما يدل عليه الفاظهم وعباراتهم فلا يكون في افكار هم مناقضة. منه : و اعلم ان الخطاء كما يقع فى الافكار الكاسبة للتصديقات كذلك يقع في التصورات ولذلك يخالف العقلاء في تعريف الاشياء حداً ورسماً و لذا تركه اعتماداً على المقايسة أو لعدم ظهور ذلك في التصورات (عبد الرحيم)
(60) His saying: 'and it may end in its opposite': he meant by it that it is conflicting with it in general, whether that conflict occurs in affirmation and negation, or privation and possession, or real opposition, or correlation, or anything similar to that. (62) And it is not hidden that this is more general than that one of the two thoughts arises from one person and the other from another, because it is apparent that rational beings contradict one another according to their thoughts, and than that both arise from one person at multiple times, by necessity, for the rational thinker when he returns to his inner consciousness, perhaps thinks and believes a judgment, then later thinks and believes another judgment conflicting with the first judgment. Indeed, profound scholars sometimes change their school and transfer their opinion according to multiple states and different times. (Muhammad Ali) (63) His saying: 'and the necessary consequence is the meeting of the two contradictories': this is extremely apparent when the later thought arises from what the first thought arose from, because his knowledge that this thought contradicts that thought is intuitive knowledge that cannot be doubted. Unlike if it arises from another person, because the state of another is not of this status, due to the possibility that his thought conflicting with the first thought is for a purpose of showing excellence or perfection or something else that a single person's thought does not entail. And because the contradiction of some rational beings with others is only known from their words indicating that the implications of their thoughts are contradictory, and it is possible that they do not believe what their words and expressions indicate, so there is no contradiction in their thoughts. From him [the author]: And know that error occurs in the concepts that produce assent just as it occurs in concepts, and therefore rational beings differ in the definition of things by hadd and rasm. And thus he [the commentator] left it, relying on analogy or because that is not apparent in concepts. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of a syllogism ending in a contradictory conclusion (naqid). The author first defines what 'opposite' means here: it includes any kind of conflict—affirmation/negation, privation/possession, genuine opposition, correlation, etc. Then he expands that this conflict can occur between two thoughts from different persons or from the same person at different times. He notes that rational beings do contradict each other, and also scholars change their opinions. The crucial point is made in the next gloss: when the two contradictory thoughts come from the same person, the impossibility of them both being true is intuitively known (ilm wijdani) because the person directly experiences the conflict. However, when the thoughts come from different persons, we only infer contradiction from their words, and it is possible that they do not actually hold those beliefs (e.g., they might be arguing for show). Therefore, the certainty of actual contradiction is weaker across persons. The passage ends with an important note: error occurs not only in conceptualizations (tasawwurat) that lead to assent (tasdiqat), but also in tasawwurat themselves. Hence scholars differ in definitions by hadd and rasm. The commentator (sharh?) left this topic perhaps relying on analogy or because the error is not obvious in tasawwurat.

Key Terms
الخطاء
النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is contradiction less certain when it occurs between two different rational beings?
A) Because different persons are less rational.
B) Because we only infer contradiction from their words, which may not reflect actual belief.
C) Because the syllogism cannot produce contradiction across persons.
D) Because the author does not consider cross-person contradiction as real.

Key Takeaway
Contradiction is certain only when experienced within a single mind; across minds, linguistic expression may deceive, so the existence of true contradiction is only probable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Two jurists disagree on whether qiyas (analogy) is valid in all cases. One says it is always valid, the other says it is never valid. Can we be sure their beliefs are contradictory? What additional information would increase certainty?

Analyzing the Philosophers' Syllogism for Infinite Souls
Author presents and refines the philosophers' syllogism for infinite souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the categorical syllogism from the philosophers' argument for infinite rational souls and distinguish between actual and potential infinity.

Core Idea
A valid categorical syllogism about the world's eternity yields a potentially infinite series of souls.

The philosophers' claim that rational souls are infinite in number is not a loose statement but is grounded in a precise syllogism about the world's eternity. This passage presents that syllogism and then clarifies a crucial distinction to preempt an obvious objection. Understanding this argument will sharpen your ability to analyze theological arguments that rely on infinite regress or series.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Find the statement that the philosophers are trying to prove. It is that rational souls are infinite in number.
The conclusion appears after the word 'فيكون'.
2
Extract the premises
The argument uses two premises: (1) The world is eternal, having no beginning. (2) Everything that has no beginning has no end. These lead to the intermediate conclusion that the world has no end.
The premises are linked by 'لأن' and 'وكل'.
3
Formulate the categorical syllogism
Write the syllogism in standard form. Major premise: 'Everything that has no beginning has no end.' Minor premise: 'The world has no beginning.' Conclusion: 'Therefore, the world has no end.' Then from this they infer that rational souls (as part of the world) are infinite in number.
This is a first-figure, AAA (Barbara) syllogism.
4
Analyze the notion of infinity
The passage explicitly clarifies that the infinity is not actual (all souls existing at once) but potential: each soul is followed by another without end, like the number series. Note that this avoids the objection that an actual infinite cannot exist.
This distinction is crucial in Islamic philosophy and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الناطقة غير معدودة على ما يقول الحكماء، لأنها أزلية أبدية كما يقولون وتحقيق ذلك في مبحث النفس من الفلسفة. (التقريب ص ٢٥) (١١) فإنهم قالوا: إن العالم قديم لا أول له وكل ما لا أول له لا آخر له فيكون العالم عندهم لا أول له ولا آخر له، فيكون النفوس الناطقة غير متناهية العدد عندهم لا بمعنى أنها داخلة تحت الوجود مرة واحدة غير متناهية حتى يرد أن ما انضده الوجود لابد وأن يكون متناهياً بل بمعنى أنها لا تصل إلى حد لا يوجد بعده نفس ناطقة بل كلما وجدت نفس ناطقة وجدت أيضاً بعدها نفس ناطقة إلى مالا نهاية له كالأعداد فإنها لا تصل إلى حد لا يوجد بعده عدد بل كل مرتبة يصل إليها يمكن وصولها إلى ما بعدها إلى غير النهاية وهكذا الحال في معلومات البارى تعالى.
The rational (souls) are not counted according to what the philosophers say, because they are eternal and everlasting as they say, and its investigation is in the topic of the soul from philosophy. (Approximation p. 25) (11) For they said: The world is eternal, having no beginning, and everything that has no beginning has no end; thus the world according to them has no beginning and no end; thus the rational souls according to them are infinite in number, not in the sense that they enter into existence at once as an infinite (multitude) such that it would be objected that what is opposite to existence must be finite, but in the sense that they do not reach a limit beyond which no rational soul exists; rather, whenever a rational soul exists, there also exists after it a rational soul indefinitely, like numbers: they do not reach a limit beyond which no number exists; rather, every rank reached can be surpassed further, without end. And such is the case with the known objects of the Exalted Creator.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a gloss on the text, presenting the philosophers' argument that rational souls are infinite in number. It begins by stating that according to the philosophers, rational souls are not countable because they are eternal. Then it gives the syllogism: the world is eternal (no beginning), and whatever has no beginning has no end, so the world has no end. Consequently, rational souls (which are part of the world) are infinite. The gloss clarifies that this infinity is not actual (all existing at once) but potential: each soul is followed by another without bound, like the number series. This is an important distinction in Islamic philosophy between actual and potential infinity. The author then mentions that the same applies to the objects of God's knowledge. The passage is a critical refinement because it elaborates on the philosophers' reasoning to avoid misunderstanding.

Proof that Contradictories of Equal Terms Are Equal: Objection and Resolution
Proof of equality of contradictories with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in inferring from a negative modified predicate to a positive affirmative, and explain when the equality of contradictories holds.

Core Idea
Equality of contradictories fails when subjects are non-existent; existence is key.

This passage presents a proof that if two universals are equal, their negations are also equal, followed by a subtle objection. The proof relies on the impossibility of both a term and its negation being false together. However, the objector shows that this inference fails for negative modified predicates—e.g., "not not-A" does not imply "A" if the subject does not exist. This lesson dissects the logical distinction and the resolution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
The claim is: If every A is B and every B is A (A and B are equal), then every not-A is not-B and every not-B is not-A (their contradictories are also equal). This is the equality of contradictories.
The example used is man (insan) and rational (natiq).
2
Examine the proof
Assume the contradictories are not equal. Then we would have, say, some not-man is not not-rational. By the impossibility of both contradictories being false (i.e., the law of excluded middle applied to terms), this would imply that some not-man is rational. Similarly, some not-rational is man. This contradicts the original equality because we would have man without rational or vice versa.
The proof uses immediate inferences and the principle of contradiction.
3
Identify the objection
The objector points out that the step from 'some not-man is not not-rational' to 'some not-man is rational' is invalid. A negative modified predicate (saliba ma'dula al-mahmul) is true even when the subject does not exist, but the positive affirmative requires existence. Example: 'Zaid is not not-a-writer' is true if Zaid does not exist, but 'Zaid is a writer' is false.
This is a key distinction between modified and simple propositions.
4
Analyze the resolution
The responder tries to salvage the proof by noting that subjects like not-man and not-rational are real existents (trees, stones). But the objector counters with terms like 'thing' and 'possible' that cover all concepts; their negations 'not-thing' and 'not-possible' have no existent subjects. The answer restricts the discussion to cases where the equal terms do not cover everything, ensuring their negations have an existent subject (external or mental).
The commentator further suggests generalizing to hypothetical (mental) existence, which eliminates the objection.
5
Reflect on the generalization
If we accept that negative modified predicates can be true of unreal subjects only in a real sense, then the objection stands. But if we allow truth for hypothetical (mental) existence as well, then the distinction between modified and affirmative collapses, and the original proof becomes universally valid.
This shows how logical rules are adjusted based on the domain of discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢١) مثل : كل لا انسان لا ناطق وكل لا ناطق لا انسان (٢٢) قوله : اى كل ما صدق عليه أحد النقيضين حاصله انه اذا ثبت أن بين الانسان و الناطق مثلاً تساوياً فلابد وان يكون بين نقيضيهما وهما اللاانسان واللاناطق ايضاً تساو بمعنى ان يكون كل لا انسان لا ناطقاً وكل لا ناطق لا انساناً اذ لولم يصدق نصدق نقيضاهما وهما بعض اللاانسان ليس بلا ناطق وبعض اللاتناطق ليس بلا انسان فحينئذ يصدق بعض اللا انسان ناطق و بعض اللاناطق انسان ضرورة استحالة ارتفاع النقيضين، فيصدق عين احدهما بدون عين الآخر لامتناع اجتماع النقيضين وهو خلاف الفرض، هذا. واعترض عليه بأن صدق :قولنا بعض اللاانسان ليس بلا ناطق و بعض اللاناطق ليس بلا انسان لا يستلزم صدق قولنا بعض اللا انسان ناطق و بعض اللاناطق انسان لما سيأتى من ان صدق السالبة : المعدولة المحمول لا يستلزم صدق الموجبة المحصلة الاترى ان صدق قولنا زيد ليس بلا كاتب لا يوجب صدق قولنا زيد كاتب الجواز ان يكون زيد معدوماً فلا يكون كاتباً ولا لا كاتباً. لا يقال ان الموضوع فيا نحن فيه موجود فان اللاانسان واللاناطق صادقان على موجودات محققة كالشجر والحجر وغيرهما و السالبة المعدولة و الموجبة المحصلة مثلا زمان حين وجود الموضوع كما سيأتى. لانا نقول : ليس الكلام في خصوص اللا انسان و اللاناطق ونحوهما بل في نقيضى المتساو بين مطلقا فما تقول في مثل الشيء والممكن العام فانها متساويان لصدقهما على جميع المفهومات و لا يصدق نقيضاهما و هما اللاشيء واللاممكن على شيء اصلاً كما تقدم فلايتم البرهان المذكورح البتة، فاذا قلت: لو لم يصدق كل لا شيء لا ممكن لصدق بعض اللاشيء ليس بلا ممكن فيصدق بعض اللاشيء ممكن اتجه المنع المذكور فان الموضوع ليس بموجود قطعاً . واجيب بانا تخص البحث بما اذا لم يكن المتساويان شاملين لجميع الاشياء ذهن و خارجاً فلابد ان يصدق نقيضاهما على موجود اما خارجى او ذهني فيتم حالدليل و ينسد السبيل وتعميم القواعد انما يجب بحسب الاغراض المقصودة والمقاصد المطلوبة من الفن كما سبق. ولا يخفى اخترناه من تعميمه الى الحقيقى والفرضي ،فلا لعدم ورود الاعتراض حتى يحتاج الى الجواب وقولهم : ان السالبة المعدولة المحمول اعم من الموجبة المحصلة، انما هو على تقدير اختصاص الصدق و الوجود بالحقيقى واما على تقدير تعميمها اليه والى الفرضى فالظاهر عدم الفرق بينهما، فتأمل . (21) Example: Every not-man is not-rational and every not-rational is not-man. (22) His saying: i.e. everything that is true of one of the two contradictories. The summary: If it is established that there is equality between e.g. man and rational, then necessarily there is also equality between their contradictories, i.e. not-man and not-rational, meaning that every not-man is not-rational and every not-rational is not-man. For if it were not true, then their contradictories would be true: 'Some not-man is not not-rational' and 'Some not-rational is not not-man'. Then it would be true that 'Some not-man is rational' and 'Some not-rational is man' by necessity, because the raising of the two opposites (naqidayn) is impossible. So one of them would be true without the other due to the impossibility of their conjunction, which is contrary to the assumption. This is it. And it was objected that the truth of our statement 'Some not-man is not not-rational' and 'Some not-rational is not not-man' does not entail the truth of 'Some not-man is rational' and 'Some not-rational is man' because, as will come, the truth of the negative with a modified predicate does not entail the truth of the affirmative with a positive predicate. Do you not see that the truth of our statement 'Zaid is not not-a-writer' does not necessitate that 'Zaid is a writer', since it is possible that Zaid does not exist, so he is neither a writer nor not-a-writer. Do not say that the subject in our case exists, because 'not-man' and 'not-rational' are true of real existents like tree, stone, etc., and the modified negative and positive affirmative are simultaneous with the existence of the subject, as will come. For we say: The discussion is not specifically about 'not-man' and 'not-rational' and the like, but about the contradictories of equal terms absolutely. So what do you say about something like 'thing' and 'general possible'? They are equal because they apply to all concepts, but their contradictories 'not-thing' and 'not-possible' do not apply to anything at all, as previously mentioned. So the said proof does not hold at all. For if you say: If it were not true that every not-thing is not-possible, then some not-thing would be not not-possible, so some not-thing would be possible. The mentioned objection applies because the subject certainly does not exist. And it was answered: We restrict the discussion to when the two equals do not include all things in mind and external. Then necessarily their contradictories apply to some existent, either external or mental, so the proof holds and the path is blocked. Generalizing rules is only required according to the intended objectives and desired purposes of the art, as before. And it is not hidden that we chose to generalize it to the real and the hypothetical, so there is no need for the objection that requires an answer. And their statement that the negative with modified predicate is more general than the positive affirmative is only on the assumption that truth and existence are restricted to the real. But on the assumption of generalizing them to the real and the hypothetical, apparently there is no difference between them. So reflect. (Muhammad Ali) (Muhammad) (23) They are, for example, not-rational and rational, because nothing is devoid of being rational or not-rational. (Note:) This is only needed based on what preceded regarding restricting truth to real truth. But as for...
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that if two universals are equal, then their contradictories (negations) are also equal, along with an objection and resolution. The proof begins with an example: 'Every not-man is not-rational and every not-rational is not-man' (which is the equality of not-man and not-rational). The argument is by contraposition: assume equality of man and rational; if the contradictories were not equal, then we would have a situation where, for instance, some not-man is not not-rational, implying (by impossibility of both negation and affirmation being false together) that some not-man is rational, and similarly some not-rational is man. That would mean one of the original equal terms (man) applies without the other (rational), contradicting equality. The objection raises a subtle logical point: the truth of a negative with a modified predicate (e.g., 'Some not-man is not not-rational') does not necessarily imply the truth of a positive affirmative ('Some not-man is rational') because the subject might be non-existent. For example, 'Zaid is not not-a-writer' is true if Zaid does not exist, but 'Zaid is a writer' is false. The responder tries to counter by noting that the subjects (not-man, not-rational) are real existents, so the implication holds. However, the objector responds with a more general case: consider 'thing' and 'general possible' – they are equal and apply to all concepts, but their contradictories 'not-thing' and 'not-possible' apply to nothing, so the proof fails because we cannot assume the subjects exist. The answer restricts the discussion to cases where the equal terms do not cover everything (both mentally and externally), ensuring that their contradictories apply to some existent (external or mental). Finally, the commentator notes that they have chosen to generalize the discussion to both real and hypothetical existence, thus avoiding the objection. The passage ends with a note on the special case of 'rational' and 'not-rational' covering all existents, requiring the restriction to real truth.

السالة المعدولة المحمول

Complete Refutation of an Objection in Logical Debate
Author explains that the objection is fully refuted by both approaches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a refutation in a logical debate is complete or partial based on the implications addressed.

Core Idea
A refutation is only complete if it repels all implications of the objection.

This passage from the hashiya emphasizes that when an objection is raised, the answer must address it fully. The commentator insists that both proposed answers completely refute the objection, not just one of its aspects. Understanding what makes a refutation complete is crucial for rigorous debate in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Locate what the objection (ishkāl) is about: the occurrence of 'hadd' in answer to 'what is it?' and the truth of the definition on the hadd and the genus.
The objection challenges the definition's applicability.
2
Identify the two answers
Find references to the 'first answer' and the 'second answer' in the passage. The first answer is from al-Muhakamat, the second from Muhaqqiq Tusi.
The passage argues both are sufficient.
3
Analyze completeness of each answer
Check if each answer addresses all parts of the objection. The commentator says both repelled the objection 'bi asrihi' (in its entirety).
Do not assume only one part is refuted.
4
Understand the commentator's correction
Note that the gloss resolves confusion about which answer fully refutes and clarifies that no restriction is needed.
This prevents a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۱۰)ای باسره و تمامه اى اشكال وقوع الحد في جواب اي شيء وصدق التعريف على الحد و الجنس. ثم لاوجه لتخصيص دفع الاشكال بحذافيره بهذا الجواب كما هو ظاهر كلامه قانه بالجواب الأول أيضاً يندفع الاشكال بحذافيره كما بينا آنفاً. ويمكن ان يكون قوله: «فحينئذ» اشارة الى جميع ما تقدم من كلام صاحب المحاكمات وكلام المحقق الطوسي او يكون قوله فحينئذ» الى آخره من كلام المحقق الطوسي (ره) لا من كلام المحشى او ان يكون تو هم المحشى في جواب صاحب المحاكمات انه انما يندفع به اللزوم الثاني فقط لا الاول كما توهمه بعض المحققين من المحشين وقدمر. (الشيخ محمد على)
(110) That is, in its entirety and completeness: the objection of the occurrence of the definition (hadd) in answer to 'what is it?' and the truth of the definition on the definition (hadd) and the genus. Then there is no reason to restrict the repulsion of the objection completely to this answer, as is apparent from his statement. Because with the first answer also the objection is completely repelled, as we made clear earlier. And it is possible that his statement 'then (fahina'idhin)' refers to all the preceding speech of the author of al-Muhakamat and the speech of Muhaqqiq Tusi, or that his statement 'then' to its end is from the speech of Muhaqqiq Tusi (may Allah have mercy on him), not from the speech of the commentator. Or that the illusion of the commentator in the response of the author of al-Muhakamat is that only the second implication (luzum) is repelled by it, not the first, as some of the investigating commentators imagined, and it has passed. (Shaykh Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage discusses the refutation of an objection (ishkal) concerning the definition (hadd) and its applicability. The objection is about the occurrence of 'hadd' in answer to 'what is it?' and the truth of the definition on both the hadd and the genus. The commentator, Muhammad Ali, clarifies that there is no need to restrict the complete refutation to the particular answer (mentioned earlier), because the first answer also repels the objection entirely, as previously shown. He then explores several possibilities regarding who said what: perhaps the phrase 'fahina'idhin' refers to earlier statements from al-Muhakamat and Muhaqqiq Tusi, or it is a continuation of Tusi's own words, not the commentator's. Another interpretation is that the commentator had the illusion that the objection's second implication (luzum) alone is repelled, not the first, but that is incorrect. This passage shows the precision of argumentation in the margins.

Key Takeaway
A complete refutation must eliminate all implications of the objection, not just some.

Categorical Syllogism: Constitutive of Higher Is Part of Lower
Author presents a categorical syllogism showing that the constitutive of the higher is part of the lower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism showing that the constitutive of the higher is part of the lower, and explain the genus-species hierarchy.

Core Idea
The constitutive of the higher is its differentia, which is part of the higher; and the higher (genus) is part of the lower (species).

In logic, the relationship between genus and species is hierarchical. The muqawwim (constitutive) of a higher category is its differentia, and the differentia is a part of the species. Moreover, the higher category (genus) is itself a part of the lower category (species). This lesson walks through the syllogism that proves these relationships.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise (sughrā)
The author states: 'the constitutive is the differentia, and the differentia is a part of the reality of its individuals.' Therefore, the constitutive of the higher (its differentia) is a part of the higher.
The minor premise connects the constitutive to the higher.
2
Identify the major premise (kubrā)
The author states: 'the higher is the genus of the lower, and the genus of everything is a part of it.' Therefore, the higher (genus) is a part of the lower (species).
The major premise connects the higher to the lower.
3
Combine to form the conclusion
From the minor: constitutive of higher is part of higher. From the major: higher is part of lower. By transitivity, constitutive of higher is part of lower. This is the desired conclusion.
The passage states: 'it follows that the higher is part of the lower' – implied transitivity.
4
Verify the categorical syllogism form
This is a first-figure categorical syllogism (Barbara): All A are B (constitutive is part of higher), All B are C (higher is part of lower), therefore All A are C (constitutive is part of lower). The middle term is 'higher'.
Check validity: subject of conclusion is constitutive, predicate is lower.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١١٤) اما الصغرى فلان المقوم هو عبارة عن الفصل وقد تقدم أن الفصل جزء حقيقة افراده تحته في الكليات الخمس فحينئذ نقول : مقوم العالى فصله وفصل كل شيء جزئه فتقوم العالى جزئه واما الكبرى، فلان العالى جنس السافل وجنس كل شيء جزئه فينتج ان الحالي جزء للسائل و هو المطلوب (ميرزا محمد علی)
(114) As for the minor (premise): because the constitutive (muqawwim) is the differentia (fasl), and it has preceded that the differentia is a part of the reality of its individuals under it in the five universals. Then we say: the constitutive of the higher is its differentia, and the differentia of every thing is its part. So the constitutive of the higher is its part. As for the major (premise): because the higher is the genus of the lower, and the genus of every thing is its part. So it follows that the higher is a part of the lower. And that is the desired conclusion. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage constructs a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) to prove that the constitutive (muqawwim) of the higher (al-'ali) is a part of the lower (al-safil). The commentator, Mirza Muhammad Ali, breaks it into a minor premise (sughra) and a major premise (kubra). The minor: the constitutive is the differentia (fasl), and the differentia is a part of its individuals (i.e., of the species). Therefore, the constitutive of the higher (its differentia) is a part of the higher. The major: the higher is the genus of the lower, and the genus of everything is a part of it. Therefore, the higher (genus) is a part of the lower (species). The conclusion: the higher (as constitutive?) Actually, the conclusion states: 'the higher is part of the lower' (al-'ali juz' li'l-safil). But the desired result seems to be that the constitutive of the higher is part of the lower, which follows from transitivity: if the constitutive of the higher is part of the higher, and the higher is part of the lower, then the constitutive of the higher is part of the lower. The passage says 'it follows that the higher is part of the lower' which then implies that the constitutive of the higher is part of the lower. This argument illustrates how logical hierarchy works.

Key Terms
مقوم (muqawwim): The constitutive part of an essence; here it is equated with the differentia that makes a species what it is. [Note: Stable; derived from taqwim.]
صغرى (sughrā): Minor premise in a categorical syllogism. [Note: Standard logical term.]
كبرى (kubrā): Major premise in a categorical syllogism. [Note: Standard.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minor premise (sughrā) in the argument?
A) The muqawwim is the fasl, and fasl is part of the reality of its individuals.
B) The higher is the genus of the lower.
C) The genus is part of the species.
D) The conclusion is that the higher is part of the lower.

Key Takeaway
The constitutive of the higher is part of the lower because the differentia (constitutive) is part of the higher, and the higher (genus) is part of the lower.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the same syllogistic form, prove that the constitutive of 'ibadat (acts of worship) is part of salah. Use the premises: 'The constitutive of 'ibadat is its being an action with intention of nearness to Allah.' and ''Ibadat is the genus of salah.'

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Categorical Syllogism: The Higher is Part of the Lower
Author proves that the higher universal is a part of the lower universal via categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism that proves why the higher universal is a part of the lower universal, and validate its form.

Core Idea
The higher is part of the lower because it is its genus, and genus is part of its species.

This passage from a hashiya on the five universals presents a concise categorical syllogism that establishes a fundamental relationship in the hierarchy of universals. The author argues that the higher universal (such as genus) is a part of the lower universal (such as species). By breaking down the syllogism, you will see how logical reasoning from clear premises yields a necessary conclusion that is central to understanding the structure of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the conclusion of the syllogism
The text states 'فينتج ان الحالي جزء للسائل' – 'it results that the higher is a part of the lower'. This is the proposition to be proved.
It matches the lesson's title.
2
Identify the two premises
The premises are: (1) 'The genus of everything is its part' (جنس كل شيء جزئه) – a universal rule. (2) 'The higher is the genus of the lower' (العالي جنس السافل) – a specific application of that rule.
In the text, the second premise is introduced with 'واما الكبرى' which here means 'as for the major premise', but logically it is the minor premise. This is a known usage in commentarial style.
3
Classify the syllogistic form
This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure (shakl awwal). The middle term is 'genus of the lower'. The mood is Barbara (all universal affirmative).
First figure requires the middle term to be subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor premise.
4
Confirm validity
Since all premises are universal affirmatives and the figure is valid, the conclusion follows necessarily. No conversion or other operation is required.
This illustrates how a syllogism can prove a relation between universals in a simple and direct way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحته ٢٤٥ في الكليات الخمس فحينئذ نقول : مقوم العالى فصله وفصل كل شيء جزئه فتقوم العالى جزئه واما الكبرى، فلان العالى جنس السافل وجنس كل شيء جزئه فينتج ان الحالي جزء للسائل و هو المطلوب
Under it 245: On the five universals, then we say: The constitutive of the higher is its differentia, and the differentia of everything is its part, so the constitutive of the higher is its part. As for the major premise, because the higher is the genus of the lower, and the genus of everything is its part, so it results that the higher is a part of the lower, which is what was sought.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents a categorical syllogism from the commentary on the five universals. The argument proceeds: The constitutive of the higher (the 'ali) is its differentia (fasl). The differentia of any thing is a part (juz') of that thing. Therefore, the constitutive of the higher is a part of the higher. Then the major premise: The higher is the genus (jins) of the lower (safil). The genus of any thing is a part of that thing. Therefore, the higher is a part of the lower. So the conclusion is that the higher is a part of the lower, which is the desired result. This establishes an important relation between the higher and lower universals in the hierarchy of the five universals.

Key Terms
مقوم
فصل
جنس
عالى
سافل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The higher is a part of the lower
C) The lower is a part of the higher
D) The constitutive is the species

Key Takeaway
The higher universal is a part of the lower universal because the higher is the genus of the lower, and the genus is a part of its species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Use the same syllogistic form (Barbara) to prove that 'the differentia (fasl) is a part of the species (naw').' Provide the two premises and conclusion using an example from the five universals (e.g., differentia 'rational' for 'human').

Therefore, 'human' is composed of genus and differentia.' Identify the major premise, minor premise, middle term, and conclusion. Is this syllogism valid? Explain why or why not.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Every Divider of the Lower is a Divider of the Higher: A Syllogistic Proof
Commentary on 'every divider of the lower' with a two-syllogism proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two-step categorical syllogism proving that every divider of the lower is a divider of the higher, and identify its figure and mood.

Core Idea
The divider of the lower is a part of the higher, and every part is a divider – a chain of two syllogisms.

In this composite passage, the commentator Muhammad 'Ali provides a rigorous syllogistic proof that every divider (muqassim) of the lower universal is a divider of the higher universal. He also critiques a previous commentator who restricted the concept too narrowly. We will walk through the logical steps and see how categorical syllogisms are used in the Islamic logical tradition to establish relationships between universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being proved
The thesis is 'Every divider of the lower is a divider of the higher.' Note that the converse (every divider of the higher is a divider of the lower) is false, as stated in note 120.
2
Extract the first syllogism
Premises: (1) The divider of the lower is a part of the lower. (2) The lower is a part of the higher. Conclusion: The divider of the lower is a part of the higher. The middle term is 'the lower'.
3
Extract the second syllogism
Premises: (1) The divider of the lower is a part of the higher (from first conclusion). (2) Every part of a thing is a divider of that thing. Conclusion: The divider of the lower is a divider of the higher. The middle term is 'part of the higher'.
4
Determine figure and mood of each syllogism
Both are in the first figure (middle term subject of major and predicate of minor) and mood Barbara (all A are B, all B are C, therefore all A are C).
5
Reflect on the commentator's critique
The author rejects an earlier commentator's definition of 'divider of the lower' as applying only to the lower genus. The criticism holds because 'lower' here means any lower universal, not just the lowest species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١١٩) قوله اى كل مقسم للسائل: قال بعض المحققين من المحشين: «اى للجنس السافل فان النوع السافل يمتنع ان يكون له فصل مقسم انتهى . ولا يخفى ما فيه، لأنه مبنى على ما مر اولاً من ان النوع السافل مالا يكون تحته نوع و اما على ما سبق آنفاً من معنى السافل وهو المراد هنا فلاوجه لهذا الكلام كما لا يخفى لذوى الافهام . (محمد على) (۱۲۰) ای : ليس كل مقسم للعالي مقسماً للسافل . ( محمد على) (۱۲۱) تقريره ان مقسم السائل قسم من السائل و السافل قسم من العالى وقسم القسم قسم فينتج مقسم السافل قسم من العالى فحينئذ نقول : مقسم السافل قسم من العالى وكل قسم من الشيء مقسم له فينتج : كل مقسم السائل مقسم العالي و هو المطلوب. (محمد علی) (۱۲۲) و ايضاً : العالى جزء للسافل وقد ثبت آنفاً ان ليس كل ما هو جزء للشيء جزء الجزء فتذكر. (محمد علی)
(119) His statement: 'i.e., every divider of the lower': Some verifying commentators among the marginalists said: 'i.e., for the lower genus, for the lower species is impossible to have a dividing differentia.' End. And it is not hidden what is in it, because it is based on what was earlier that the lower species is what has no species under it. But according to what was mentioned just now of the meaning of the lower, which is intended here, there is no basis for this statement, as is clear to those of understanding. (Muhammad Ali) (120) i.e., Not every divider of the higher is a divider of the lower. (Muhammad Ali) (121) Its exposition: The divider of the lower is a part of the lower, and the lower is a part of the higher, and a part of a part is a part, so it results that the divider of the lower is a part of the higher. Then we say: The divider of the lower is a part of the higher, and every part of a thing is a divider of it, so it results: Every divider of the lower is a divider of the higher, which is what was sought. (Muhammad Ali) (122) And also: The higher is a part of the lower, and it has been established that not everything that is a part of a thing is a part of its part, so remember.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This composite section (notes 119-122) deals with the relationship between dividers (muqassim) of the higher and lower universals. Note 119 quotes another commentator who defined 'divider of the lower' as specifically applying to the lower genus, arguing that the lower species cannot have a dividing differentia. The present commentator (Muhammad Ali) criticizes this, saying it relies on an earlier narrower definition of 'lower' (as the lowest species), but the current context uses the broader meaning of any lower universal. Therefore, that restriction is unjustified. Note 120 asserts that not every divider of the higher is a divider of the lower (a caution against universalizing the relationship). Note 121 provides a syllogism proving the converse: 'Every divider of the lower is a divider of the higher'. The proof: The divider of the lower is a part of the lower (since a divider is a part). The lower is a part of the higher (by definition). Thus the divider of the lower is a part of the higher (first syllogism). Then: Every part of a thing is a divider of it. So the divider of the lower is a divider of the higher (second syllogism). This is the desired conclusion. Note 122 adds a reminder: the higher is a part of the lower? Wait, earlier it was said the higher is a part of the lower? Actually, the text says 'the higher is a part of the lower' (العالى جزء للسافل). This seems reversed from the earlier lesson where the higher was a part of the lower? Actually, in the initial syllogism, the higher was shown to be a part of the lower. So this note reminds that not every part of a thing is a part of its part (i.e., the higher is a part of the lower, but a part of the higher (like its differentia) is not necessarily a part of the lower? This connects back to the earlier discussion on conversion. It warns against a hasty generalization.

Key Terms
مقسم
قسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the syllogism in note 121?
A) Every divider of the higher is a divider of the lower
B) Every divider of the lower is a divider of the higher
C) The divider of the lower is not a part of the higher
D) The higher is not a part of the lower

Key Takeaway
The divider of the lower is a part of the lower, the lower is a part of the higher, and a part of a part is a part; therefore the divider of the lower is a divider of the higher.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
The Imam's Syllogism for the Mental Universal
The Imam's syllogism proving the mental universal exists only in the mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Imam's compound syllogism for the mental universal and identify its main inference type.

Core Idea
The Imam proves the mental universal exists only in the mind using a disjunctive syllogism supported by a categorical sub-argument.

The Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a rigorous argument that the universal, as described by philosophers, must exist in the mind alone. His reasoning combines a disjunctive syllogism with a categorical sub-argument about external existents. Let's walk through this argument step by step and see how it holds together.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main disjunction
The Imam starts by stating that the thing described by universality exists either in the mind or in the exterior. This is the first premise of a disjunctive syllogism.
A disjunctive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i munfasil) eliminates one option to conclude the other.
2
Eliminate the external existence option
The Imam argues that external existence is impossible because every external existent is a particularized individual (shakhs mu'ayyan). No particularized individual can be shared among many, but universals are shared. Therefore, nothing external is universal.
This is a categorical syllogism: All external existents are particularized; no particularized is shared; all universals are shared; so no external existent is universal.
3
Combine to conclude mental existence
Since the disjunction has only two options and the external option is eliminated, the conclusion follows that the universal (as described) must exist in the mind alone.
This is valid modus tollendo ponens for a disjunctive syllogism.
4
Note the structure as compound
The argument is compound: a disjunctive syllogism with a categorical sub-syllogism as support. Recognizing this helps in analyzing objections.
Compound syllogisms combine multiple inference steps.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «فإن انتفاء الجزء»: وهو الكلي المنطقي، يستلزم انتفاء الكل»: وهو الكلي العقلي. قال المحشي في الحاشية: فإن الكلي العقلي إنما يحصل من الكلي المنطقي والكلي الطبيعي، وإذا قررنا أن الكلي المنطقي ليس بموجود في الخارج والعقلي أيضاً كذلك، فإن انتفاء الجزئي... ولا يخفى عليك أن هذا مبني على أن الكلي الطبيعي موجود في الخارج، ولو قلنا إنه غير موجود فيه فالوجه أن يقال لأن الأجزاء إذا كانت منتفية بجميعها فلا يتحقق الكل، لأن الكل لا يكون إلا هذه الأجزاء، فإذا لم يتحقق هذه لم يتحقق هذا. ثم اعلم أن الإمام استدل على وجود الكلي العقلي في الذهن فقط بما قاله الحكماء في الأمر الموصوف بالكلية، إنه موجود إما في الذهن أو في الخارج، وإلا كان عدماً صرفاً، ولو كان كذلك لاستحال أن يكون مشتركاً بين كثيرين، ومحال أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج لأن كل موجود في الخارج فهو مشخص معين، ولا شيء من المشخص المعين بمشترك بين كثيرين، وكل كلي مشترك بين كثيرين، فلا شيء من الموجود في الخارج بكلي، ولما بطل كونه موجوداً في الخارج، تعين كونه موجوداً في الذهن. وفيه: أنه إن عنى بالشخص في قوله: «ولا شيء من المشخص المعين بمشترك بين كثيرين» الماهية مع المشخصات، فلا شك أن الأمر كذلك، وإن عنى به الماهية المعروضة لتلك المشخصات ممنوع، لأنها إذا انتزعت من المشخصات وحصلت في العقل صلح لأن يعرض لها الكلية، فكانت مشتركة بين كثيرين. (عبد الرحيم) أي مع قطع النظر عن عروض الكلية، وإلا لم يكن موجوداً في الخارج اتفاقاً. (عبد الرحيم) وقد عرفت أن عبارة المصنف كما يحتمل ذلك يحتمل القول الأول أيضاً، فلا يظهر من المصنف ميل إلى أحدهما، ولو كان الحق عنده هو الثاني كما زعم لكان حق العبارة أن يقول: «والحق أن لا وجود للطبيعي في الخارج»، فإنه نص في المقصود من الاختصار المطلوب. (ميرزا محمد علي) قبل أن أراد أنه يلزم اتصاف الشيء الواحد بالشخص بالصفات المتضادة ووجوده في الأمكنة المتعددة، سلمنا الاستحالة لكن نمنع الملازمة. وإن أريد أنه يلزم اتصافه ووجوده لا بالشخص بالصفات المتضادة وفي الأمكنة المتعددة، فالملازمة مسلمة ولا يضر، فإنا لا نسلم امتناع وجود الواحد النوعي والجنسي في الأمكنة المتعددة واتصافهما بالصفات المتعددة، بل هو أول المسألة. انتهى. لا يقال: إن المراد هو الأول وبيان الملازمة أن كل موجود خارجي فهو في حد ذاته متميز عن غيره بحيث إذا لاحظ العقل خصوصيته الممتازة لم يكن له أن يفرض اشتراكها، فلو وجدت الطبيعة في الخارج لكان كذلك مع أنها مشتركة بين أفراد متمكنة في أماكن مختلفة ومتصفة بصفات متضادة، فحينئذ يلزم المحال المذكور. فإنا نقول: لا نسلم أن كل موجود خارجي لا يمكن فرض اشتراكه إذا لاحظ العقل خصوصيته، بل هو أول المرحلة في تلك المسألة. وربما استدل على أصل الدعوى بأنه لو وجد الكلي الطبيعي في الخارج فلا يخلو إما أن يكون خارجاً عن الجزئيات أو لا، وعلى الثاني إما أن لا يكون نفسها أو جزء منها، والأقسام كلها باطلة. أما الأول فبديهي وإلا فلا يصح الحمل، وكذا الثاني وإلا لزم أن يكون كل واحد من الجزئيات عين الآخر، وبيان الملازمة أن كل واحد عين الطبيعي والطبيعي عين الجزئي الآخر، وعين العين عين، وبطلانه واضح. وكذا الثالث وإلا فلا يصح الحمل أيضاً لتغاير الجزء للكل، فتأمل.
His statement: 'For the non-existence of the part' — which is the logical universal — 'implies the non-existence of the whole' — which is the mental universal. The commentator said in the gloss: For the mental universal is only obtained from the logical universal and the natural universal, and if we have established that the logical universal does not exist in the exterior and the mental likewise, then the non-existence of the partial... And it is not hidden from you that this is based on the natural universal existing in the exterior, and if we say it does not exist in it, then the proper way is to say: because if all the parts are non-existent, then the whole does not occur, because the whole is nothing but these parts, so if these do not occur, this does not occur. Then know that the Imam argued for the existence of the mental universal in the mind only by what the philosophers said about the thing described by universality: that it exists either in the mind or in the exterior, otherwise it would be pure non-existence, and if that were so, it would be impossible for it to be shared among many. And it is impossible for it to exist in the exterior because every existent in the exterior is a particularized determinate, and nothing of the particularized determinate is shared among many, and every universal is shared among many, so nothing of the existent in the exterior is a universal. And when it is invalid that it exists in the exterior, it is determined that it exists in the mind. And there is in it: that if by 'individual' in his statement 'and nothing of the particularized determinate is shared among many' he means the essence together with the particularizations, then there is no doubt that it is so; and if he means the essence that is the substrate for those particularizations, it is denied, because when it is abstracted from the particularizations and occurs in the mind, it becomes fit for universality to attach to it, so it becomes shared among many. (Abd al-Rahim) i.e., disregarding the attachment of universality, otherwise it would not exist in the exterior by agreement. (Abd al-Rahim) And you have known that the author's expression, as it admits that, also admits the first statement, so no inclination toward one of them appears from the author. And if the truth according to him were the second as claimed, the proper expression would be for him to say: 'The truth is that there is no existence for the natural in the exterior,' for it is a clear text regarding the intended brevity. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) Before, if he intended that it necessitates the characterization of the single thing as an individual with contradictory attributes and its existence in multiple places, we concede the impossibility but we deny the implication. And if it is intended that it necessitates its characterization and existence not as an individual with contradictory attributes and in multiple places, the implication is conceded and does no harm, for we do not concede the impossibility of the existence of the specific and generic one in multiple places and their characterization with multiple attributes; rather that is the starting point of the issue. End. It may not be said: the intended is the first, and the explanation of the implication is that every external existent is in itself distinct from others such that if the intellect notes its distinguished particularity, it cannot suppose it to be shared. So if nature existed in the exterior, it would be thus while it is shared among individuals located in different places and characterized by contradictory attributes, then the mentioned impossibility would follow. For we say: we do not concede that every external existent cannot be supposed to be shared if the intellect notes its particularity; rather that is the first stage in that issue. And perhaps one argued for the original claim: that if the natural universal existed in the exterior, it would either be outside the particulars or not; and on the second, either it is not identical to them or a part of them; and all divisions are invalid. As for the first, it is obvious, otherwise predication would not be valid. Likewise the second, otherwise it would necessitate that each particular be identical to the other. And the explanation of the implication is that each one is identical to the natural, and the natural is identical to the other particular, and the identical of the identical is identical, and its invalidity is clear. And likewise the third, otherwise predication would not be valid either, because of the difference of the part from the whole, so reflect.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques the Imam's (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) syllogism for the existence of the mental universal (al-kulli al-'aqli) only in the mind. The structure is as follows: First, a commentator notes that the whole (mental universal) depends on the part (logical universal) and the natural universal; if the logical universal lacks external existence, the whole also lacks it, assuming the natural universal exists externally. Then the Imam's argument: the thing described by universality must exist either in the mind or externally; if neither, it would be nothing and could not be shared. External existence is impossible because every external existent is a particularized individual (shakhs mu'ayyan), and no such individual can be shared among many, while the universal is shared. Therefore it must exist in the mind. The objection (by 'Abd al-Rahim) distinguishes two meanings of 'individual' (shakhs): if it means the essence plus particularizations, then indeed it cannot be shared; but if it means the essence that is the substrate for particularizations, then once abstracted in the mind, it can become universal and shared. The commentator adds that the author's phrase is ambiguous. Then Mirza Muhammad Ali provides a further objection: if the impossibility is said to be that a single thing characterized by contradictory attributes and in multiple places is impossible, we concede the impossibility but deny the implication that the natural universal would require that. If it is meant without individual characterization, then the impossibility is not conceded. Finally, an argument based on division: if the natural universal existed externally, it would either be outside the particulars (invalid for predication), or not identical to them (invalid because they would not be the same), or a part of them (invalid because a part cannot be predicated of the whole).

Critique: The Ambiguity of 'Individual' (Shakhs)
Critique of the Imam's argument by clarifying the meaning of 'shakhs'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the critical ambiguity in the term 'shakhs' (individual) in the Imam's argument and explain how it undermines the conclusion.

Core Idea
The term 'shakhs' can mean either essence+particularizations or essence alone; if the latter, the universal can be shared after abstraction.

The Imam's argument relies on the premise that no particularized individual can be shared. However, the critic 'Abd al-Rahim points out that 'individual' (shakhs) is ambiguous. When the essence is considered apart from its particularizations, it can become universal in the mind. This critique shows the importance of univocal terminology in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the ambiguous term
In the Imam's argument, the key premise is 'nothing of the particularized determinate (shakhs mu'ayyan) is shared among many.' The critic asks what 'shakhs' refers to.
Shakhs mu'ayyan is a technical term for a specific individual with all its accidents.
2
Distinguish two meanings
If 'shakhs' means the essence together with its particularizations (mushakhkhasat), then indeed it cannot be shared. But if it means the essence that is the substrate for those particularizations, then once abstracted in the mind, it can become universal and shared.
This distinction is crucial: the essence itself (e.g., 'humanity') is not particularized; only the concrete individual is.
3
Apply the distinction to the argument
The Imam's premise only works for the first meaning. If he used the second meaning, the premise is false. Since the argument does not specify, it is equivocal.
Equivocation (ishtirak lafzi) is a fallacy that invalidates the syllogism.
4
Assess the impact on the conclusion
The conclusion that the universal cannot exist in the exterior is not proven, because the natural universal (the common essence) might exist in external individuals as a shared reality, even if not as a particularized individual.
This opens the door for the realist position that universals exist in re.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «فإن انتفاء الجزء»: وهو الكلي المنطقي، يستلزم انتفاء الكل»: وهو الكلي العقلي. قال المحشي في الحاشية: فإن الكلي العقلي إنما يحصل من الكلي المنطقي والكلي الطبيعي، وإذا قررنا أن الكلي المنطقي ليس بموجود في الخارج والعقلي أيضاً كذلك، فإن انتفاء الجزئي... ولا يخفى عليك أن هذا مبني على أن الكلي الطبيعي موجود في الخارج، ولو قلنا إنه غير موجود فيه فالوجه أن يقال لأن الأجزاء إذا كانت منتفية بجميعها فلا يتحقق الكل، لأن الكل لا يكون إلا هذه الأجزاء، فإذا لم يتحقق هذه لم يتحقق هذا. ثم اعلم أن الإمام استدل على وجود الكلي العقلي في الذهن فقط بما قاله الحكماء في الأمر الموصوف بالكلية، إنه موجود إما في الذهن أو في الخارج، وإلا كان عدماً صرفاً، ولو كان كذلك لاستحال أن يكون مشتركاً بين كثيرين، ومحال أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج لأن كل موجود في الخارج فهو مشخص معين، ولا شيء من المشخص المعين بمشترك بين كثيرين، وكل كلي مشترك بين كثيرين، فلا شيء من الموجود في الخارج بكلي، ولما بطل كونه موجوداً في الخارج، تعين كونه موجوداً في الذهن. وفيه: أنه إن عنى بالشخص في قوله: «ولا شيء من المشخص المعين بمشترك بين كثيرين» الماهية مع المشخصات، فلا شك أن الأمر كذلك، وإن عنى به الماهية المعروضة لتلك المشخصات ممنوع، لأنها إذا انتزعت من المشخصات وحصلت في العقل صلح لأن يعرض لها الكلية، فكانت مشتركة بين كثيرين. (عبد الرحيم) أي مع قطع النظر عن عروض الكلية، وإلا لم يكن موجوداً في الخارج اتفاقاً. (عبد الرحيم) وقد عرفت أن عبارة المصنف كما يحتمل ذلك يحتمل القول الأول أيضاً، فلا يظهر من المصنف ميل إلى أحدهما، ولو كان الحق عنده هو الثاني كما زعم لكان حق العبارة أن يقول: «والحق أن لا وجود للطبيعي في الخارج»، فإنه نص في المقصود من الاختصار المطلوب. (ميرزا محمد علي) قبل أن أراد أنه يلزم اتصاف الشيء الواحد بالشخص بالصفات المتضادة ووجوده في الأمكنة المتعددة، سلمنا الاستحالة لكن نمنع الملازمة. وإن أريد أنه يلزم اتصافه ووجوده لا بالشخص بالصفات المتضادة وفي الأمكنة المتعددة، فالملازمة مسلمة ولا يضر، فإنا لا نسلم امتناع وجود الواحد النوعي والجنسي في الأمكنة المتعددة واتصافهما بالصفات المتعددة، بل هو أول المسألة. انتهى. لا يقال: إن المراد هو الأول وبيان الملازمة أن كل موجود خارجي فهو في حد ذاته متميز عن غيره بحيث إذا لاحظ العقل خصوصيته الممتازة لم يكن له أن يفرض اشتراكها، فلو وجدت الطبيعة في الخارج لكان كذلك مع أنها مشتركة بين أفراد متمكنة في أماكن مختلفة ومتصفة بصفات متضادة، فحينئذ يلزم المحال المذكور. فإنا نقول: لا نسلم أن كل موجود خارجي لا يمكن فرض اشتراكه إذا لاحظ العقل خصوصيته، بل هو أول المرحلة في تلك المسألة. وربما استدل على أصل الدعوى بأنه لو وجد الكلي الطبيعي في الخارج فلا يخلو إما أن يكون خارجاً عن الجزئيات أو لا، وعلى الثاني إما أن لا يكون نفسها أو جزء منها، والأقسام كلها باطلة. أما الأول فبديهي وإلا فلا يصح الحمل، وكذا الثاني وإلا لزم أن يكون كل واحد من الجزئيات عين الآخر، وبيان الملازمة أن كل واحد عين الطبيعي والطبيعي عين الجزئي الآخر، وعين العين عين، وبطلانه واضح. وكذا الثالث وإلا فلا يصح الحمل أيضاً لتغاير الجزء للكل، فتأمل.

Quantifiers and the Status of Singular Propositions in Syllogisms
Author clarifies quantifier usage and refutes singular major premise objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish quantifiers for individuals vs parts and evaluate whether a singular proposition can serve as a major premise in the first figure.

Core Idea
Quantifiers count individuals, not parts; singular propositions are not true majors.

In logic, quantifiers like 'كل' and 'بعض' are used to indicate how many individuals a predicate applies to. But they can also be used for parts of a single object, as in 'كل هذا الصندوق ساج'. However, logical propositions that enter syllogisms must have a universal subject. This lesson also addresses an objection: can a singular proposition like 'زيد إنسان' be a major premise? The author shows why it cannot, and what counts as a 'considered proposition'.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two uses of quantifiers
Quantifiers can indicate quantity of individuals (e.g., كل إنسان) or quantity of parts (e.g., كل هذا الصندوق). Note the difference in the subject term.
The text gives examples for both.
2
Understand why only individuals matter in logic
The discussion of propositions is for syllogisms, and syllogisms only use quantified propositions with universal subjects. So parts-quantifiers are irrelevant.
The author cites the Shaykh and the definition of quantified propositions.
3
Analyze the objection about singular major premises
An objector says a singular proposition like 'زيد إنسان' can be a major premise. Identify the structure: 'هذا زيد' (minor), 'زيد إنسان' (major), conclusion 'هذا إنسان'.
4
Evaluate the author's response
The author argues that the major is not truly singular because the predicate 'إنسان' is universal, not singular. Moreover, the universality of the judgment can be disputed (not all named Zayd are human).
Also note: the considered proposition is defined as that which can be a major premise in the first figure.
5
Contrast with natural propositions
Natural propositions like 'الإنسان نوع' can be minor premises but are not considered because they cannot be majors. This reinforces the definition.
The alternative definition: considered propositions are those suitable for investigation in the demonstrative sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم ان هذه الاسوار الاربعة كما تذكر لبيان كمية الجزئيات كقولنا كل انسان حیوان و بعض الانسان كاتب ولاشيء من الانسان بحجر، كذلك تذكر لبيان كمية الاجزاء كقولنا كل هذا الصندوق ساج و بعض هذا الصندوق رطب ولاشيء من هذا الصندوق بحديد والمراد بها في هذا الفن ما يبين كمية الجزئيات لا كمية الاجزاء على ما صرح به الشيخ و يشهد به جعلهم المحصورات قسماً مما يكون الموضوع فيه كلياً كما سبق. (ميرزا محمد على) (۳۵) و ذلك لان الكلام فى القضاياء انما هو لاجل تألف القياس منها والقياس لا يتألف الامن المحصورات الاربع. فان قبل القضية الشخصية قد يكون كبرى للشكل الأول كما في قولنا : هذا زيد وزيد انسان فهذا انسان فينبغي ان يكون معتبرة. قلنا: المحمول بحسب الحقيقة في المثال المذكور انما هو مسمى زيد فليس شخصياً فلا يقع محمولاً فكذا الموضوع في الكبرى فليست الكبرى شخصية. فان قيل : يكفى فى اعتبارها صلاحيتها لكبروية الشكل الاول ظاهراً كما في المثال المذكور. قلنا: صلاحيتها لكبروية الشكل الاول خفاء، لامكان المناقشة فى كليتها اذ يجوز ان : الانسان فلا يصح الحكم بان کل مسمی بزید انسان فاذا قيل : الشخصية قد تقع صغرى للشكل الاول فينبغي ان تعتبر کر کاشت کارو برداری ساری قلنا : القضية المعتبرة هى التى تقع كبرى للشكل الاول فلهذا لم يعدوا الطبيعية من القضاياء المعتبرة مع انها تقع صغرى للشكل الاول كما في قولنا: الانسان نوع وكل نوع متفقة الافراد فالانسان متفقة الافراد يسمى بزيد غير و قد يفسر القضية المعتبرة بانها التي يصلح لان يبحث في العلوم الحكمية عنها وعلى هذا بناء اقوال المحشى.
Then know that these four quantifiers, just as they are mentioned to indicate the quantity of individuals, as we say 'Every human is an animal', 'Some human is a writer', 'Nothing from human is a stone', likewise they are mentioned to indicate the quantity of parts, as we say 'All of this box is teak', 'Some of this box is moist', 'Nothing of this box is iron'. But the intended meaning in this art is what clarifies the quantity of individuals, not the quantity of parts, as the Shaykh has explicitly stated, and it is attested by their making the quantified propositions a division of what has a universal subject, as previously. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (35) And that is because the discussion of propositions is only for the sake of composing the syllogism from them, and the syllogism is composed only from the four quantified propositions. If it is said: the singular proposition may be a major in the first figure, as in our saying 'This is Zayd, and Zayd is a human, so this is a human'; then it should be considered. We say: the predicate in reality in the mentioned example is only the named 'Zayd', so it is not singular, so it does not occur as a predicate; similarly the subject in the major, so the major is not singular. If it is said: its suitability for being a major of the first figure is sufficient for considering it apparently, as in the mentioned example. We say: its suitability for being a major of the first figure is obscure, because it is possible to dispute its universality, since it is possible that ... human, so it is not correct to judge that every named Zayd is human. If it is said: the singular may occur as a minor in the first figure, so it should be considered ... We say: the considered proposition is that which occurs as a major in the first figure, and for this reason they did not count the natural proposition among the considered propositions, even though it occurs as a minor in the first figure, as in our saying 'Human is a species, and every species has coincident individuals, so human has coincident individuals.' It is called 'by Zayd other'? And the considered proposition has been interpreted as that which is suitable for investigation in the demonstrative sciences, and upon this is based the statements of the commentator.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of which propositions are considered (المعتبرة) in logic. The author begins by noting that the four quantifiers (كل, بعض, لا شيء, and implicitly not كل) can be used for quantity of individuals (جزئيات) or quantity of parts (اجزاء). For example, 'كل إنسان حيوان' refers to individuals, while 'كل هذا الصندوق ساج' refers to parts of a single object. The intended use in this art (علم المنطق) is for individuals, not parts, as stated by the Shaykh and evinced by the fact that quantified propositions have a universal subject. The reason is that propositions are studied for composing syllogisms, and syllogisms only compose from the four quantified (محصورات) propositions. An objection is raised: singular propositions (الشخصية) can serve as major premises in the first figure, e.g., 'هذا زيد' (This is Zayd), 'زيد إنسان' (Zayd is human), therefore 'هذا إنسان'. The response is that in the major premise 'زيد إنسان', the subject 'زيد' is a proper name, but the predicate 'إنسان' is universal; however, the major premise is not singular because the predicate 'إنسان' is not a singular. Moreover, the universality of the judgment can be challenged because not every individual named 'Zayd' (if there are many) is necessarily human. The considered proposition is defined as that which can be a major premise in the first figure. Therefore, natural propositions (الطبيعية), like 'الإنسان نوع' (Human is a species), are not considered even though they can be minor premises. An alternative interpretation is offered: considered propositions are those suitable for investigation in the demonstrative sciences.

Key Terms
القضية المعتبرة
الشخصية
الطبيعية
كمية الجزئيات

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that a singular proposition like 'زيد إنسان' can be a major premise in the first figure?
A) Because the subject 'زيد' is not universal
B) Because the predicate 'إنسان' is not singular and the judgment may not be universally true for all named Zayd
C) Because singular propositions cannot be quantified
D) Because the first figure requires affirmative premises only

Key Takeaway
Quantifiers in logic refer to individuals, not parts; singular propositions cannot serve as major premises because they lack true universality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objection in the passage argues that 'زيد إنسان' is not a valid major premise. Restate the objection in your own words and then write the author's refutation, identifying the logical flaw in the objection.

Singular Propositions Excluded as Major Premises
Author argues against singular propositions as major premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a proposition can serve as a major premise in a first-figure categorical syllogism by checking the universality of its predicate.

Core Idea
The major premise must be universal; a singular predicate destroys syllogistic validity.

In this passage, the author refutes the idea that singular propositions can serve as major premises in the first figure. Through a dialectical exchange, he shows that the predicate in a singular proposition is a proper name, not a universal, and thus cannot sustain the logical form required for a valid syllogism. Understanding this criterion is essential for distinguishing considered propositions (mu'tabara) in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's example
The objector offers: 'This is Zayd, and Zayd is human, so this is human.' Locate this example in the Arabic. Note that the major premise is 'Zayd is human'—a singular proposition (subject is a proper name).
The objector claims this shows singular propositions can be major premises.
2
Analyze the author's refutation
The author argues that the predicate in the minor premise ('Zayd') is actually a singular name, not a universal. Therefore, the major premise 'Zayd is human' is singular, and the syllogism fails to meet the first-figure condition that the major premise must be universal.
The response hinges on the nature of the term 'Zayd' as a singular name.
3
Extract the criterion for considered propositions
The author states: 'The proposition that is considered is that which occurs as a major premise of the first figure.' This means a proposition must be able to function as a universal major premise to be considered (mu'tabara). Singular propositions, even if true, are excluded.
This is a key principle for evaluating propositions in syllogistic logic.
4
Apply the criterion to a new example
Consider the natural proposition 'Human is a species'. Although it can be a minor premise, it is not considered because it cannot be a major premise (its subject is not a term that can be predicated universally). This reinforces the rule.
The example of the natural proposition is given in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان قبل القضية الشخصية قد يكون كبرى للشكل الأول كما في قولنا : هذا زيد وزيد انسان فهذا انسان فينبغي ان يكون معتبرة. قلنا: المحمول بحسب الحقيقة في المثال المذكور انما هو مسمى زيد فليس شخصياً فلا يقع محمولاً فكذا الموضوع في الكبرى فليست الكبرى شخصية. فان قيل : يكفى فى اعتبارها صلاحيتها لكبروية الشكل الاول ظاهراً كما في المثال المذكور. قلنا: صلاحيتها لكبروية الشكل الاول خفاء، لامكان المناقشة فى كليتها اذ يجوز ان : الانسان فلا يصح الحكم بان کل مسمی بزید انسان فاذا قيل : الشخصية قد تقع صغرى للشكل الاول فينبغي ان تعتبر کر کاشت کارو برداری ساری قلنا : القضية المعتبرة هى التى تقع كبرى للشكل الاول فلهذا لم يعدوا الطبيعية من القضاياء المعتبرة مع انها تقع صغرى للشكل الاول كما في قولنا: الانسان نوع وكل نوع متفقة الافراد فالانسان متفقة الافراد
For if it is said: The singular proposition can be a major premise of the first figure, as in our saying: 'This is Zayd, and Zayd is a human, so this is a human'; so it ought to be considered (mu'tabara). We say: The predicate in reality in the mentioned example is the name 'Zayd', so it is not singular (shakhsi), so it does not occur as a predicate; likewise the subject in the major premise, so the major premise is not singular. If it is said: Its apparent suitability for being a major premise of the first figure is sufficient for its consideration, as in the example. We say: Its suitability for being a major premise of the first figure is hidden, because it is possible to object to its universality, for it is possible that... So if it is said: The singular proposition can occur as a minor premise of the first figure, so it should be considered... We say: The proposition that is considered is that which occurs as a major premise of the first figure; that is why they did not count the natural proposition among the considered propositions, even though it occurs as a minor premise of the first figure, as in our saying: 'Human is a species, and every species has individuals that are alike, so human has individuals that are alike'.

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical objection and response about whether singular propositions should be considered (mu'tabara) in logic. The objection claims that a singular proposition like 'This is Zayd' can serve as a major premise in a first-figure syllogism. The response argues that in such an example, the predicate 'Zayd' is actually a singular name, not a universal, so the proposition is not really singular in logical terms. Further, the suitability for being a major premise is not apparent because one can question whether every individual named 'Zayd' is human. The discussion then shifts to the natural proposition, which is not considered even though it can be a minor premise. The key criterion is that a considered proposition must be capable of being a major premise in the first figure.

A proposition is 'considered' in syllogistic logic only if it can serve as a universal major premise in the first figure; singular propositions fail this test.

Reconciling Natural Propositions in Logic and Philosophy
Commentator discusses conflicting interpretations of why natural propositions are not studied in sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the commentator's two attempted reconciliations of the statement that natural propositions are not studied in sciences, and evaluate their consistency with the original author's intent.

Core Idea
Natural propositions are studied in logic but not in philosophy; domain confusion creates false contradictions.

The author of the text claimed that natural propositions are not researched in the sciences, yet logicians clearly do study them (e.g., they examine their convertibility). The commentator faces an apparent contradiction. In this lesson, you will dissect his two proposed solutions and judge their fit with the original text. This interpretive skill is essential for working with dense logical commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
The author says natural propositions are not researched in the sciences, but logicians treat them as a subject (e.g., they note that natural propositions do not serve as major premises in the first figure and do not convert). What is the tension?
The contradiction hinges on what 'sciences' means.
2
Extract the first reconciliation
The commentator suggests that 'sciences' might mean only the philosophical sciences (like metaphysics, physics), not logic. In that case, natural propositions being studied in logic does not violate the claim.
This restricts the domain of 'sciences' to philosophy.
3
Extract the second reconciliation
Alternatively, the commentator proposes that by 'considered propositions' he meant those that are subjects in philosophy, while unconsidered ones are not subjects there, regardless of status in logic.
This also limits exclusion to philosophy.
4
Evaluate the writer's hesitation
The writer (Mirza Muhammad Ali) states that these interpretations, though considered, do not accord with the author's words. Why might this be? Possibly because the author's original term 'sciences' is broader, encompassing both logic and philosophy.
The writer commands reflection, indicating the reconciliations are insufficient.
5
Reflect on the interpretive challenge
This passage shows how logicians carefully delimit the scope of claims. Recognizing that a claim holds in one domain but not another is crucial for avoiding false contradictions in Islamic sciences.
Similar skills apply in usul al-fiqh when reconciling apparently conflicting texts.
