The Absolute Necessary Proposition Cannot Be Restricted
Author explains why the absolute necessary proposition cannot accept any restrictions of non-necessity or non-permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine why the absolute necessary proposition cannot be restricted by essential or attributive non-necessity or non-permanence.

Core Idea
Absolute necessity implies permanence over all times of the essence, so any negation of necessity or permanence contradicts it.

The absolute necessary proposition (الضرورية المطلقة) asserts that the predicate holds necessarily for the subject at all times of its essence. This lesson examines why such a proposition cannot be paired with any modal that negates necessity or permanence, using the logical principle that opposite modalities cannot coexist in the same proposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the absolute necessary proposition
Find the proposition where the predicate is attributed to the subject with necessity at every moment the subject's essence exists. An example from Islamic sciences: 'Every human is an animal necessarily'.
This is the strongest modal form.
2
Understand the meaning of essential non-necessity
Essential non-necessity (اللاضرورة الذاتية) claims that the predicate is not necessary for the subject as long as the essence exists. This directly contradicts the absolute necessary proposition, which claims necessity for all essence times.
3
Check attributive non-necessity
Attributive non-necessity (اللاضرورة الوصفية) negates necessity with respect to a particular attribute. However, if the predicate is necessary at all essence times, it is also necessary for every attribute's time, since attribute times are subsets of essence times. Hence, attributive non-necessity also contradicts.
The attribute's times do not exceed the essence's times.
4
Test non-permanence restrictions
Non-permanence (اللادوام) means the predicate does not persist. But absolute necessity implies permanence (استحالة انفكاك), so any non-permanence restriction is impossible.
Permanence is a necessary consequence of necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فانه بمنزلة قولك بالامكان العام ملحوظة فى بيان ما يصح تقييده باللادوام الذاتي والوصفى واللاضرورة الذاتية و الوصفية وما لا يصح تقييده بها جميعاً أو ببعضها من القضايا البسائط السابقة الذكر. الضرورية المطلقة لا يصح تقييد الضرورية المطلقة باللاضرورة الذاتية لان قيدها المذكور يناقض الاصل المقيد به، فان الضرورة الذاتية واللاضرورة الذاتية تتمانعان وهكذا لا يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الوصفية، لان المحمول اذا ثبت انتسابه للموضوع بالضرورة فى بيع اوقات الذات، فهو ثابت له مع جميع أوصافه لان اوقات أوصافه من اوقات ذاته لا تبارحها و هكذا لا يصح تقييدها باللادوام الذاتى، والوصفى لان ضرورة الانتساب دوام مع استحالة انفكاك. ٢- المشروطة العامة لا يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الوصفية لتنافى الضرورة بحسب الوصف واللاضرورة بحسب الوصف، ولكن يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الذاتية فان الضرورة الوصفية تحكم باستحالة انفكاك المحمول عن الموضوع مادام الوصف و هذا الوصف قديكون وصفاً مفارقاً يزول و يثبت مكانه غيره وذلك كوصف الكتابة والقيام و القعود و نظائرها، واللاضرورة الذاتية تقول : ان انتساب المحمول المذكور ليس ضرورياً للموضوع مادامت ذاته و قد عرفت ان وقت الوصف بعض من وقت الذات، فاللاضرورة الذاتية تشير الى مازاد من اوقات الذات عن اوقات الوصف، واذاصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الذاتية صح تقييدها باللادوام الذاتى فان اللادوام الذاتى فيها معناه ان انتساب المحمول فيها للموضوع ليس دائماً مادامت ذات الموضوع موجودة، فان اوقات الذات تزيد على اوقات الوصف العنوانى المفارق كالكتابة ونحوها فالضرورة بحسب الوصف انما تتناول وقتاً محدوداً من اوقات الذات والأوصاف اللازمة للذات كالانسانية للانسان والحيوانية للحيوان ونظائر ذلك، لا يقال لها: اوصاف عنوانية الابضرب من التكلف، وانما هي عناوين انتزاعية من مقام المعنون ولذلك تدوم بدوامه و تنهدم با نهدامه. ولكن لا يصح تقييدها باللادوام الوصفى لانه يناقض الضرورة الوصفية بوضوح. ٣- الوقتية المطلقة يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الذاتية لان الضرورة فيها في وقت معين و اوقات ذات الموضوع تزيد على هذه القطعة اعنى الوقت المعين، فقيد اللاضرورة الذاتية يشير الى مازاد من اوقات الذات عن ذلك الوقت المعين وهكذا يصح تقييدها باللادوام الذاتى بالملاك المذكور وهكذا باللاضرورة و اللادوام الوصفيين، لان الوصف الذى يراعى فى الوقتية المطلقة وصف لازم منتزع عن ذات الموضوع كالقمرية من القمر و الانسانية من الانسان و هذه الاوصاف كما قرأت باقية ببقاء الذات، فعنى لاضرورتها ولا دوامها هو عين معنى لا ضرورة الذات ولا دوامها بلا تفاوت اصلا. -- المنتشرة المطلقة - وهي كالوقتية المطلقة ولكن وقت الضرورة فيها مردد في جملة اوقات الذات فكل ما قيل هناك ، يقال هنا بلا أدنى ،تفاوت فتتقيد المنتشرة المطلقة باللاضرورتين الذاتية و الوصفية
It is like saying 'with general possibility' observed in explaining what can be restricted by essential and attributive non-permanence and essential and attributive non-necessity, and what cannot be restricted by all or some of them from the simple propositions mentioned previously. Absolute necessary proposition: it is not valid to restrict the absolute necessary proposition by essential non-necessity, because the mentioned restriction contradicts the original being restricted; for essential necessity and essential non-necessity are mutually exclusive. Likewise, it is not valid to restrict it by attributive non-necessity, because if the predicate is proven to be attributed to the subject necessarily in all times of the essence, then it is established for it with all its attributes, since the times of its attributes are from the times of its essence and do not leave it. And likewise, it is not valid to restrict it by essential and attributive non-permanence, because the necessity of attribution is permanence with impossibility of separation. 2. General conditional proposition: it is not valid to restrict it by attributive non-necessity, due to the contradiction between necessity according to the attribute and non-necessity according to the attribute. But it is valid to restrict it by essential non-necessity, for the attributive necessity judges the impossibility of separation of the predicate from the subject as long as the attribute exists, and this attribute may be a separable attribute that disappears and is replaced by another, such as the attribute of writing, standing, sitting, and the like. And essential non-necessity says: the attribution of the mentioned predicate is not necessary for the subject as long as its essence exists. And you have known that the time of the attribute is part of the time of the essence. So essential non-necessity refers to the extra times of the essence beyond the times of the attribute. And if it is valid to restrict it by essential non-necessity, it is valid to restrict it by essential non-permanence, for essential non-permanence in it means that the attribution of the predicate in it is not permanent as long as the subject's essence exists. For the times of the essence exceed the times of the distinguishing separable attribute like writing and the like. So necessity according to the attribute only covers a limited time of the times of the essence. And the necessary attributes of the essence, like humanity for man and animality for animal and the like, are not called distinguishing attributes except with a stretch; rather they are abstracted titles from the level of the designated thing, and therefore they persist with its persistence and cease with its cessation. But it is not valid to restrict it by attributive non-permanence because it obviously contradicts attributive necessity. 3. Absolute temporal proposition: it is valid to restrict it by essential non-necessity, because the necessity in it is at a specific time, and the times of the subject's essence exceed this segment, i.e., the specific time. So the restriction of essential non-necessity refers to the extra times of the essence beyond that specific time. And likewise it is valid to restrict it by essential non-permanence according to the mentioned criterion, and likewise by attributive non-necessity and non-permanence, because the attribute considered in the absolute temporal proposition is a necessary attribute abstracted from the subject's essence, like lunar-ness from the moon and humanity from man. And these attributes, as you have read, remain with the persistence of the essence. So the meaning of their non-necessity and non-permanence is exactly the meaning of essential non-necessity and non-permanence without any difference at all. -- Absolute widespread proposition: it is like the absolute temporal proposition, but the time of necessity in it is ambiguous among the times of the essence. So everything said there is said here without the slightest difference. So the absolute widespread proposition is restricted by both essential and attributive non-necessities.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of four types of simple propositions (القضايا البسائط) and the modal restrictions they can or cannot accept. The author lists: 1) Absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة), 2) General conditional (المشروطة العامة), 3) Absolute temporal (الوقتية المطلقة), and 4) Absolute widespread (المنتشرة المطلقة). For each, he explains whether it can be restricted by essential non-necessity (اللاضرورة الذاتية), attributive non-necessity (اللاضرورة الوصفية), essential non-permanence (اللادوام الذاتي), or attributive non-permanence (اللادوام الوصفي). The reasoning hinges on the relationship between the subject's essence, its attributes, and time. For the absolute necessary, the necessity is permanent over all times of the essence, so adding any restriction that negates permanence or necessity contradicts the original. For the general conditional, the necessity is tied to a separable attribute (e.g., writing), so its times are a subset of the essence's times; thus essential non-necessity (which refers to the remainder of times) is compatible, but attributive non-necessity is not because it directly contradicts the attribute-based necessity. For the absolute temporal, the necessity is at a specific time; the essence's other times are unrestricted, so both essential and attributive non-necessity/permanence are valid because the attribute in this proposition is a necessary attribute (e.g., humanity), which is coextensive with the essence. The absolute widespread is similar but with an ambiguous time, so the same restrictions apply. The passage uses examples from the Islamic intellectual tradition: writing, standing, sitting (separable attributes) and humanity, animality, lunar-ness (necessary attributes). This classification is crucial for understanding how modal qualifiers interact with propositional content in logic.

Key Terms
الضرورية المطلقة
اللاضرورة الذاتية
اللاضرورة الوصفية
اللادوام الذاتي
اللادوام الوصفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The attribute considered is separable.

D) The predicate is not permanent.

Key Takeaway
The absolute necessary proposition is incompatible with any restriction that negates necessity or permanence because its modality covers all times and attributes of the essence.

General Conditional and Its Modal Restrictions
Author discusses which restrictions apply to the general conditional proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which modal restrictions are permissible for the general conditional proposition and explain why attributive non-necessity is excluded but essential non-necessity is allowed.

Core Idea
The general conditional proposition's necessity is tied to a separable attribute, so essential non-necessity (which refers to other times) is compatible, but attributive non-necessity contradicts the attribute-based necessity.

The general conditional proposition (المشروطة العامة) asserts that the predicate holds necessarily only as long as a certain separable attribute exists (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily while writing'). This lesson explores how such a proposition can be restricted by essential non-necessity and essential non-permanence but not by attributive non-necessity or attributive non-permanence, based on the relationship between the attribute's time and the essence's time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general conditional proposition
Find a proposition where the predicate is necessary for the subject only during the presence of a separable attribute, such as writing, standing, or sitting. For example: 'Every writer is a finger-mover necessarily while writing'.
The attribute is separable, meaning it can cease and be replaced.
2
Understand attributive non-necessity
Attributive non-necessity (اللاضرورة الوصفية) negates necessity with respect to the same attribute. Since the proposition's necessity is precisely due to that attribute, adding attributive non-necessity would directly contradict the proposition's condition.
The passage says it is invalid because of 'تنافى' (mutual exclusion).
3
Consider essential non-necessity
Essential non-necessity (اللاضرورة الذاتية) says the predicate is not necessary for the subject as long as the essence exists. The attribute's time is only a part of the essence's time; the essence exists even when the attribute is absent. Thus, essential non-necessity refers to the times when the attribute is absent, which do not conflict with the necessary times under the attribute.
The author says: 'فان الضرورة الوصفية تحكم باستحالة انفكاك المحمول عن الموضوع مادام الوصف و هذا الوصف قديكون وصفاً مفارقاً يزول و يثبت مكانه غيره'.
4
Check essential non-permanence
Essential non-permanence (اللادوام الذاتي) also targets the essence's entire duration. Since the predicate is not required to hold outside the attribute's time, essential non-permanence is valid, asserting that the attribution is not permanent over all essence times.
The author explicitly states: 'واذاصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الذاتية صح تقييدها باللادوام الذاتى'.
5
Confirm attributive non-permanence is invalid
Attributive non-permanence would negate permanence during the attribute's time, but the proposition asserts necessity (which entails permanence) during that time. Hence, it is also contradictory.
The author says: 'ولكن لا يصح تقييدها باللادوام الوصفى لانه يناقض الضرورة الوصفية بوضوح'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الذاتى فيها معناه ان انتساب المحمول فيها للموضوع ليس دائماً مادامت ذات الموضوع موجودة، فان اوقات الذات تزيد على اوقات الوصف العنوانى المفارق كالكتابة ونحوها فالضرورة بحسب الوصف انما تتناول وقتاً محدوداً من اوقات الذات والأوصاف اللازمة للذات كالانسانية للانسان والحيوانية للحيوان ونظائر ذلك، لا يقال لها: اوصاف عنوانية الابضرب من التكلف، وانما هي عناوين انتزاعية من مقام المعنون ولذلك تدوم بدوامه و تنهدم با نهدامه. ولكن لا يصح تقييدها باللادوام الوصفى لانه يناقض الضرورة الوصفية بوضوح. ٣- الوقتية المطلقة يصح تقييدها باللاضرورة الذاتية لان الضرورة فيها في وقت معين و اوقات ذات الموضوع تزيد على هذه القطعة اعنى الوقت المعين، فقيد اللاضرورة الذاتية يشير الى مازاد من اوقات الذات عن ذلك الوقت المعين وهكذا يصح تقييدها باللادوام الذاتى بالملاك المذكور وهكذا باللاضرورة و اللادوام الوصفيين، لان الوصف الذى يراعى فى الوقتية المطلقة وصف لازم منتزع عن ذات الموضوع كالقمرية من القمر و الانسانية من الانسان و هذه الاوصاف كما قرأت باقية ببقاء الذات، فعنى لاضرورتها ولا دوامها هو عين معنى لا ضرورة الذات ولا دوامها بلا تفاوت اصلا. -- المنتشرة المطلقة - وهي كالوقتية المطلقة ولكن وقت الضرورة فيها مردد في جملة اوقات الذات فكل ما قيل هناك ، يقال هنا بلا أدنى ،تفاوت فتتقيد المنتشرة المطلقة باللاضرورتين الذاتية و الوصفية
It is like saying 'with general possibility' observed in explaining what can be restricted by essential and attributive non-permanence and essential and attributive non-necessity, and what cannot be restricted by all or some of them from the simple propositions mentioned previously. Absolute necessary proposition: it is not valid to restrict the absolute necessary proposition by essential non-necessity, because the mentioned restriction contradicts the original being restricted; for essential necessity and essential non-necessity are mutually exclusive. Likewise, it is not valid to restrict it by attributive non-necessity, because if the predicate is proven to be attributed to the subject necessarily in all times of the essence, then it is established for it with all its attributes, since the times of its attributes are from the times of its essence and do not leave it. And likewise, it is not valid to restrict it by essential and attributive non-permanence, because the necessity of attribution is permanence with impossibility of separation. 2. General conditional proposition: it is not valid to restrict it by attributive non-necessity, due to the contradiction between necessity according to the attribute and non-necessity according to the attribute. But it is valid to restrict it by essential non-necessity, for the attributive necessity judges the impossibility of separation of the predicate from the subject as long as the attribute exists, and this attribute may be a separable attribute that disappears and is replaced by another, such as the attribute of writing, standing, sitting, and the like. And essential non-necessity says: the attribution of the mentioned predicate is not necessary for the subject as long as its essence exists. And you have known that the time of the attribute is part of the time of the essence. So essential non-necessity refers to the extra times of the essence beyond the times of the attribute. And if it is valid to restrict it by essential non-necessity, it is valid to restrict it by essential non-permanence, for essential non-permanence in it means that the attribution of the predicate in it is not permanent as long as the subject's essence exists. For the times of the essence exceed the times of the distinguishing separable attribute like writing and the like. So necessity according to the attribute only covers a limited time of the times of the essence. And the necessary attributes of the essence, like humanity for man and animality for animal and the like, are not called distinguishing attributes except with a stretch; rather they are abstracted titles from the level of the designated thing, and therefore they persist with its persistence and cease with its cessation. But it is not valid to restrict it by attributive non-permanence because it obviously contradicts attributive necessity. 3. Absolute temporal proposition: it is valid to restrict it by essential non-necessity, because the necessity in it is at a specific time, and the times of the subject's essence exceed this segment, i.e., the specific time. So the restriction of essential non-necessity refers to the extra times of the essence beyond that specific time. And likewise it is valid to restrict it by essential non-permanence according to the mentioned criterion, and likewise by attributive non-necessity and non-permanence, because the attribute considered in the absolute temporal proposition is a necessary attribute abstracted from the subject's essence, like lunar-ness from the moon and humanity from man. And these attributes, as you have read, remain with the persistence of the essence. So the meaning of their non-necessity and non-permanence is exactly the meaning of essential non-necessity and non-permanence without any difference at all. -- Absolute widespread proposition: it is like the absolute temporal proposition, but the time of necessity in it is ambiguous among the times of the essence. So everything said there is said here without the slightest difference. So the absolute widespread proposition is restricted by both essential and attributive non-necessities.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of four types of simple propositions (القضايا البسائط) and the modal restrictions they can or cannot accept. The author lists: 1) Absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة), 2) General conditional (المشروطة العامة), 3) Absolute temporal (الوقتية المطلقة), and 4) Absolute widespread (المنتشرة المطلقة). For each, he explains whether it can be restricted by essential non-necessity (اللاضرورة الذاتية), attributive non-necessity (اللاضرورة الوصفية), essential non-permanence (اللادوام الذاتي), or attributive non-permanence (اللادوام الوصفي). The reasoning hinges on the relationship between the subject's essence, its attributes, and time. For the absolute necessary, the necessity is permanent over all times of the essence, so adding any restriction that negates permanence or necessity contradicts the original. For the general conditional, the necessity is tied to a separable attribute (e.g., writing), so its times are a subset of the essence's times; thus essential non-necessity (which refers to the remainder of times) is compatible, but attributive non-necessity is not because it directly contradicts the attribute-based necessity. For the absolute temporal, the necessity is at a specific time; the essence's other times are unrestricted, so both essential and attributive non-necessity/permanence are valid because the attribute in this proposition is a necessary attribute (e.g., humanity), which is coextensive with the essence. The absolute widespread is similar but with an ambiguous time, so the same restrictions apply. The passage uses examples from the Islamic intellectual tradition: writing, standing, sitting (separable attributes) and humanity, animality, lunar-ness (necessary attributes). This classification is crucial for understanding how modal qualifiers interact with propositional content in logic.

Key Terms
المشروطة العامة
اللاضرورة الوصفية
اللاضرورة الذاتية
اللادوام الذاتي
اللادوام الوصفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can the general conditional proposition be restricted by essential non-necessity but not by attributive non-necessity?
A) Attributive non-necessity contradicts the necessity based on the attribute, while essential non-necessity applies to times beyond the attribute's duration.
B) Essential non-necessity is a stronger modal.
C) The proposition's predicate is not truly necessary.
D) The attribute is inseparable from the essence.

Key Takeaway
The general conditional proposition's restriction by essential non-necessity is permissible because the attribute's time is a part of the essence's time, leaving room for negation outside that attribute's presence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition: 'Every traveler shortens the prayer necessarily while traveling.' Explain why it is valid to restrict this with essential non-necessity (i.e., the obligation is not necessary for the traveler as a human being) but not with attributive non-necessity.
?
Create a general conditional proposition from Islamic law (e.g., concerning purity conditions) and test which modal restrictions are permissible using the criteria from this lesson.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Absolute Temporal and Widespread Propositions Accept All Restrictions
Author explains that absolute temporal and widespread propositions can be restricted by all four modal conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the absolute temporal and absolute widespread propositions can be restricted by all four modal conditions (essential/attributive non-necessity and non-permanence).

Core Idea
The absolute temporal proposition's necessity is tied to a specific time or a necessary attribute, making its other times unrestricted; the widespread proposition follows the same logic.

The absolute temporal proposition (الوقتية المطلقة) asserts necessity at a specific time (e.g., 'The moon is eclipsed necessarily at opposition'), and the absolute widespread proposition (المنتشرة المطلقة) at an unspecified time within the essence's duration. Since the attribute in these propositions is a necessary attribute (e.g., lunar-ness, humanity) that is coextensive with the essence, restricting them by any non-necessity or non-permanence is equivalent to essential restrictions and thus valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the absolute temporal proposition
Find a proposition where the predicate holds necessarily at a fixed, specific time. Example from Islamic sciences: 'The moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of opposition (when the earth is between the sun and moon).'
The time is determined and unique.
2
Understand the attribute involved
The attribute in an absolute temporal proposition is a necessary attribute of the subject, such as 'lunar-ness' (القمرية) for the moon or 'humanity' (الانسانية) for humans. These attributes persist as long as the essence exists.
The passage says: 'الوصف الذى يراعى فى الوقتية المطلقة وصف لازم منتزع عن ذات الموضوع'.
3
Check essential non-necessity and non-permanence
Since the attribute is coextensive with the essence, the times of the attribute equal the times of the essence minus the specific moment. Essential non-necessity (or non-permanence) simply refers to the other times, which are unrestricted. So they are valid.
The passage: 'فعنى لاضرورتها ولا دوامها هو عين معنى لا ضرورة الذات ولا دوامها بلا تفاوت اصلا'.
4
Check attributive non-necessity and non-permanence
Because the attribute is necessary and coextensive, attributive non-necessity is equivalent to essential non-necessity. Hence, it is also valid.
The author explicitly says that for the absolute temporal, both essential and attributive non-necessities are permissible.
5
Extend to absolute widespread proposition
The absolute widespread proposition is identical in nature, except the time of necessity is ambiguous rather than fixed. All the same reasoning applies, so it also accepts all restrictions.
The passage: 'المنتشرة المطلقة كالوقتية المطلقة ولكن وقت الضرورة فيها مردد في جملة اوقات الذات فكل ما قيل هناك يقال هنا بلا أدنى تفاوت'.

Why can the absolute temporal proposition be restricted by attributive non-necessity?

The Syllogism

Resolving Contradictions through Absolute vs. Restricted Non-Perpetuity
Gloss resolves contradiction by distinguishing absolute vs. restricted non-perpetuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute non-perpetuity (ladawam mutlaq) and restricted non-perpetuity (ladawam muqayyad) and use this distinction to resolve apparent contradictions in conversion rules.

Core Idea
Absolute and restricted non-perpetuity are not the same—confusing them leads to false contradictions.

This gloss by Mirza Muhammad Ali is a model of scholarly care. It identifies an apparent contradiction between two statements about how propositions with non-perpetuity convert. But the solution lies in a precise distinction: one statement applies to absolute non-perpetuity, the other to restricted non-perpetuity. By learning this distinction, you will see how later commentators protect the internal consistency of the logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting claims
First, find the earlier claim that non-perpetuity agrees with the original proposition in quantity (universal stays universal, particular stays particular). Then find the later claim that certain special propositions convert to a universal negative with 'non-perpetuity in some,' which indicates a particular—hence a quantity change. Recognize the contradiction.
2
Distinguish absolute vs. restricted non-perpetuity
Define 'ladawam mutlaq' (absolute non-perpetuity, unrestricted) and 'ladawam muqayyad' (restricted non-perpetuity, e.g., 'in some'). The gloss says the earlier statement was about absolute non-perpetuity, while the later involves restricted non-perpetuity.
Absolute: no extra condition. Restricted: e.g., 'non-perpetuity in some'.
3
Apply the distinction to resolve the contradiction
Understand that the condition on the absolute does not transfer to the restricted. Therefore, the earlier rule only applies to absolute non-perpetuity; the later case is different, so no contradiction arises.
4
Consider the second answer (majority agreement)
Even if the distinction is not accepted, the gloss offers a second answer: agreement in quantity holds in most cases, not necessarily always. Occasional exceptions do not invalidate the rule.
This shows a nuanced approach to logical rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا كلام حق لكنه ينافى ماسيأتى فى اواخر مبحث العكس المستوى من ان الخاصتين تنعكسان الى عرفية عامة سالبة كلية مقيدة باللادوام فى البعض وهو اشارة الى مطلقة عامة موجبة جزئية فانه ظاهر فى أن اللادوام قد لا يكون موافقاً لأصل القضية في الكم ايضاً. والجواب ان كلامنا انما هو فى اللادوام المطلق اعنى غير المقيد بشيء وما سيأتى انما يدل على جواز عدم الموافقة فى المقيد ولا يلزم من اشتراط شيء فى المطلق اشتراطه في المقيد. وقد يجاب ايضاً بان المراد انه يكون موافقاً للاصل فى الكمية فى الاغلب ولا ينا في ذلك عدم الموافقة في بعض المواد. میرزا محمد علی)
This is a true statement, but it contradicts what will come at the end of the discussion of simple conversion: that the two special propositions convert to a universal negative general conventional restricted by non-perpetuity in some, which is an indication to a particular affirmative absolute general. For it is apparent that non-perpetuity may not agree with the original proposition in quantity as well. The answer: our statement is only about absolute non-perpetuity, i.e., unrestricted by something, and what will come only indicates the permissibility of disagreement in the restricted, and it is not necessary that a condition in the absolute entails the same condition in the restricted. It may also be answered that what is meant is that it agrees with the original in quantity in most cases, and disagreement in some matters does not harm that.

Commentary
This gloss by Mirza Muhammad Ali identifies a contradiction between an earlier claim and a later statement in the section on simple conversion. The earlier claim asserted that when a proposition is restricted by non-perpetuity, the resulting non-perpetuity matches the original in quantity (i.e., universal to universal, particular to particular). However, later it is said that two special propositions (e.g., the two 'special' propositions) convert to a universal negative general conventional restricted by non-perpetuity in some, which indicates a particular affirmative absolute general. This would mean the quantity changes from universal to particular. The gloss then provides two answers: first, that the earlier statement was about absolute non-perpetuity (unrestricted), while the later case involves restricted non-perpetuity (muqayyad), so the condition does not transfer. Second, that the agreement in quantity is only in most cases, not necessarily always. This is a typical example of resolving apparent contradictions in the tradition.

Apparent contradictions in the logical tradition can often be resolved by carefully distinguishing between absolute and restricted versions of a modal condition.

Proving Conversion by the Second Method (al-'aks) and the Circularity Objection
Author explains the second proof method for conversion and addresses the circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conversion method for proving the validity of conversion and explain why the circularity objection is invalid.

Core Idea
The conversion method proves a converse by deriving a contradiction from the contradictory of the converse.

In this part of the passage, the author presents the second of three methods for proving that when a proposition is true, its converse is also true. This method, called 'al-'aks' (conversion itself), works by assuming the converse is false, taking its contradictory, converting that contradictory, and showing that the result contradicts the original proposition. The author then raises a potential circularity in this method and provides a response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and the converse to be proved
In the example, the original is 'No human is a stone' and the converse is 'No stone is a human'. The goal is to show that if the original is true, the converse must be true.
2
Understand the proof structure: assume the converse is false, take its contradictory, and convert that contradictory
The method assumes the converse is false, so its contradictory ('Some stone is a human') is true. Then it converts that contradictory to 'Some human is a stone' (using the conversion rule for particular affirmatives).
3
Check that the converted contradictory contradicts the original
The original 'No human is a stone' contradicts 'Some human is a stone'. This contradiction shows the assumption that the converse is false is impossible, so the converse must be true.
4
Examine the circularity objection: why might one think the method is circular?
The objection states that proving the conversion of a particular affirmative (e.g., some A is B) requires knowing the conversion of a universal negative, and vice versa. The two examples in the text illustrate this interdependence.
5
Understand the response: the method is not circular because it is not used simultaneously for both types
The author argues that if you prove the conversion of the particular affirmative using this method, you do not at the same time prove the conversion of the universal negative. The two proofs are separate. Presenting both together in a book is not to claim they are interdependent.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

انسان حيوان او بعض الانسان حيوان صدق بعض الحيوان انسان و الا لصدق نقيضه و هو: لاشيء من الحيوان بانسان وينعكس الى قولنا : لاشيء من الانسان بحيوان وقد كان حكم الأصل كل انسان او بعضه حیوان فهذا خلف. في السوالب لم يبين الانعكاس به في الموجبة الجزئية. وما يترأى فى بعض الكتب من ذكرهما معاً فهو على سبيل منع الجمع. الثالث: الخلف وهو بضم الخاء المعجمة: ضم نقيض العكس مع الأصل لينتج من الشكل الاول سلب (میرزا محمد علی) (۱۱) وذلك لاستحالة ارتفاع النقيضين. Know that the scholars proved the conversions of propositions by three methods: The first: hypothesis (iftirad), and the commentator (al-Muhashi) will mention it at the end of the section on contraposition (aks al-naqid). The second: conversion itself, which is to convert the contradictory of the converse and it leads back to what contradicts the original. For example, it is said: if 'Every human is an animal' or 'Some human is an animal' is true, then 'Some animal is a human' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'No animal is a human', and it converts to our saying 'No human is an animal', while the original rule was 'Every human is an animal' or 'Some human is an animal' — this is a contradiction (khalf). So in our case we say: whenever 'No human is a stone' is true, then 'No stone is a human' is true; otherwise its contradictory is true, namely 'Some stone is a human', which converts to our saying 'Some human is a stone', while the original rule was 'No human is a stone' — this is a contradiction. Then if you say: the inference by conversion is invalid because it entails circularity (dawr), for knowledge of the converse of the particular affirmative depends on knowledge of the converse of the universal negative, and knowledge of the converse of the universal negative depends on knowledge of the converse of the particular affirmative, as is clear to anyone who reflects on the two mentioned examples. I say: the circularity only occurs when both inferences are combined, as is apparent, and it is not allowed. Necessarily, someone who demonstrates conversion by this method for the particular affirmative does not demonstrate conversion by it for negative propositions, and someone who demonstrates conversion by it for negative propositions does not demonstrate conversion by it for the particular affirmative. What appears in some books of mentioning both together is only by way of denying the combination. The third: reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf), which is to combine the contradictory of the converse with the original to produce from the first figure the negation of the thing of itself, as the commentator mentioned. And an objection was raised here: if by their saying 'when some B is A is true, then some A is B is true' it is meant that the truth of the latter necessarily follows from the truth of the former, i.e., it is impossible for its truth to be separate from its truth, then we do not accept that if it did not follow, its contradictory would be true, because it is possible that it is true while separation and non-necessity are possible, necessarily because a compound is negated by the negation of one of its parts also. And if what is meant is that the truth of the original is more general, whether that be by way of necessity or coincidence, then we accept it, but it does not help because the more general does not indicate the more specific. And it was answered: we choose the first and say: what is meant by the truth of the contradictory is the possibility of its truth, and that is realized by non-necessity as well, because if the converse were not necessary for the original, i.e., inseparable from it, then separation would be possible, so the truth of its contradictory with it would be possible, and it would be possible for the thing to be free of the two contradictories, but the truth of its contradictory with it is impossible, and the possibility of the impossible is impossible. In other words, what is claimed is the necessity of the truth of the converse when the original is true; otherwise, it would be possible for its contradictory to be true with it, but that is impossible because it entails an impossibility. (Mirza Muhammad 'Ali) (11) That is due to the impossibility of the two contradictories being lifted. (Muhammad 'Ali) (12) By making it a minor premise with its affirmative, and the original proposition as a major premise due to its universality. (Muhammad 'Ali) (13) It cannot be said: negation is the removal of affirmation, and affirmation is not conceivable between a thing and itself, because the discussion is about...
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents three methods for proving the validity of conversion. The first method, hypothesis (iftirad), will be discussed later. The second method uses conversion itself in a proof by contradiction: assume the converse is false, then its contradictory is true; convert that contradictory to get something contradicting the original, showing the original assumption was false. An example is given: to prove 'No human is a stone' converts to 'No stone is a human', assume the opposite: 'Some stone is a human', which converts to 'Some human is a stone', contradicting the original 'No human is a stone'. The third method is reductio (khalf): combine the contradictory of the converse with the original to produce a syllogism in the first figure that negates something of itself. The passage addresses two objections: (1) circularity (dawr) in the second method, which is refuted by noting that the method is not used simultaneously for both cases; (2) an objection against the reductio method concerning the meaning of 'truth of the contradictory', which is answered by clarifying that by 'truth of the contradictory' is meant its possibility, not actuality. The answer involves a careful analysis of modality and impossibility. The passage ends with glosses on the reductio's structure and a note that negation cannot be of a thing with itself.

Key Terms
العكس (الطريقة الثانية)
نقيض العكس
الدور
منع الجمع

Key Takeaway
The conversion method proves a converse by deriving a contradiction from the contradictory of the converse, and the circularity objection fails because the method is not applied simultaneously to interdependent conversions.

Proving Conversion by the Third Method (al-khalf) and the Modal Objection
Author presents the khalf method for proving conversion and addresses a modal objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the khalf method for proving conversion and evaluate the objection concerning the possibility of the contradictory.

Core Idea
The khalf method proves conversion by combining the contradictory of the converse with the original to produce a syllogism that negates something of itself.

This lesson covers the third method for proving conversion, called 'al-khalf' (reductio ad absurdum). In this method, the contradictory of the converse is combined with the original proposition in a first-figure syllogism to derive a contradiction, namely that a thing is negated of itself. The passage also presents an intricate objection concerning the meaning of 'truth of the contradictory' and the modal notions of necessity and possibility, along with the response.

Guided Path
1
Understand the khalf method: combine the contradictory of the converse with the original
The author says: ضم نقيض العكس مع الأصل (combine the contradictory of the converse with the original). This forms a syllogism. The middle term is arranged so that the conclusion negates the subject of itself (e.g., 'No human is human').
2
Examine the objection raised against this method
The objector questions the implication that if the converse does not necessarily follow, then its contradictory is true. The objection distinguishes between necessary truth and accidental truth. If 'some B is A' is true but not necessarily, then the fact that 'some A is B' does not necessarily follow does not mean its contradictory is true, because the original might be true without necessity.
3
Follow the response: the truth of the contradictory is interpreted as possibility, not actuality
The respondent clarifies that when they say 'the contradictory is true', they mean it is possible (ja'iz) for it to be true. If the converse were not necessary, then it is possible for its contradictory to be true with the original. But that leads to an impossibility because it would allow two contradictories to coexist or both to be negated.
4
Analyze the key modal move: the impossibility of the two contradictories being simultaneously true or both false
The response relies on the principle that two contradictories cannot both be true and cannot both be false. If it were possible for the contradictory of the converse to be true with the original, that would mean both the original and the contradictory of the converse are true simultaneously, which is impossible. Therefore, the converse must be necessarily true.
5
Relate this to the first-figure syllogism mentioned
The glosses indicate how the syllogism is formed: make the contradictory of the converse the minor premise and the original proposition the major premise, which yields a universal conclusion that negates the subject of itself. This shows that the assumption leads to a logical absurdity.

The third: reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf), which is to combine the contradictory of the converse with the original to produce from the first figure the negation of the thing of itself, as the commentator mentioned. And an objection was raised here: if by their saying 'when some B is A is true, then some A is B is true' it is meant that the truth of the latter necessarily follows from the truth of the former, i.e., it is impossible for its truth to be separate from its truth, then we do not accept that if it did not follow, its contradictory would be true, because it is possible that it is true while separation and non-necessity are possible, necessarily because a compound is negated by the negation of one of its parts also. And if what is meant is that the truth of the original is more general, whether that be by way of necessity or coincidence, then we accept it, but it does not help because the more general does not indicate the more specific. And it was answered: we choose the first and say: what is meant by the truth of the contradictory is the possibility of its truth, and that is realized by non-necessity as well, because if the converse were not necessary for the original, i.e., inseparable from it, then separation would be possible, so the truth of its contradictory with it would be possible, and it would be possible for the thing to be free of the two contradictories, but the truth of its contradictory with it is impossible, and the possibility of the impossible is impossible. In other words, what is claimed is the necessity of the truth of the converse when the original is true; otherwise, it would be possible for its contradictory to be true with it, but that is impossible because it entails an impossibility. (Mirza Muhammad 'Ali) (11) That is due to the impossibility of the two contradictories being lifted. (Muhammad 'Ali) (12) By making it a minor premise with its affirmative, and the original proposition as a major premise due to its universality. (Muhammad 'Ali) (13) It cannot be said: negation is the removal of affirmation, and affirmation is not conceivable between a thing and itself, because the discussion is about...
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents three methods for proving the validity of conversion. The first method, hypothesis (iftirad), will be discussed later. The second method uses conversion itself in a proof by contradiction: assume the converse is false, then its contradictory is true; convert that contradictory to get something contradicting the original, showing the original assumption was false. An example is given: to prove 'No human is a stone' converts to 'No stone is a human', assume the opposite: 'Some stone is a human', which converts to 'Some human is a stone', contradicting the original 'No human is a stone'. The third method is reductio (khalf): combine the contradictory of the converse with the original to produce a syllogism in the first figure that negates something of itself. The passage addresses two objections: (1) circularity (dawr) in the second method, which is refuted by noting that the method is not used simultaneously for both cases; (2) an objection against the reductio method concerning the meaning of 'truth of the contradictory', which is answered by clarifying that by 'truth of the contradictory' is meant its possibility, not actuality. The answer involves a careful analysis of modality and impossibility. The passage ends with glosses on the reductio's structure and a note that negation cannot be of a thing with itself.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Proof of Universal Negative Conversion via Reductio
Commentary explains the proof of universal negative conversion via first-figure syllogism and reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof that a universal negative proposition converts to itself, using a first-figure syllogism.

Core Idea
The universal negative converts to itself; its denial leads to self-negation, proven by reductio.

In this lesson, we study a classical proof from the logical tradition that a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No human is a stone') converts to its converse ('No stone is a human'). The proof uses a first-figure syllogism and a reductio ad absurdum argument, showing that denying the conversion leads to an impossibility. This illustrates the power of syllogistic reasoning in establishing immediate inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and its converse
Locate the universal negative (e.g., 'No A is B') and the claim that it converts to 'No B is A'.
2
Set up the reductio assumption
Assume the converse is false, so its contradictory (a particular affirmative 'Some B is A') is true.
This is the contradictory of the converse.
3
Construct the first-figure syllogism
Combine the original universal negative as major (since it is universal) and a true universal affirmative as minor (e.g., 'Every A is B') to form a first-figure syllogism.
The major must be universal; the minor must be affirmative.
4
Derive the absurd conclusion
The syllogism yields a self-negation (e.g., 'No A is A'), which is impossible because a thing cannot be denied of itself.
5
Identify the source of absurdity
Since the original proposition and the syllogistic form are valid, the absurdity must come from assuming the contradictory of the converse is true. Therefore, the contradictory is false, and the converse is true.
This completes the reductio proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في عكس المستوى
(۱۴) اي صلب الشيء عن نفسه محال لما ثبت من ان ثبوت الشيء لنفسه ضروری
لا يقال : انا لا نسلم استحالته الجواز سلب الشيء عن نفسه عند عدمه. لانا نقول : وان كان صدق السالبة قد يكون لعدم موضوعها وقد يكون لعدم المحمول مع وجود الموضوع، لكنه لا يكون هنا الالعدم المحمول، ضرورة وجود الموضوع هنا حيث فرض صدق نقيض العكس و هو الموجبة الجزئية.
لا يقال انا لا نسلم ان صدق السالبة هنا لانتفاء المحمول و ما استدل به لا ينتهض دليلاً لجواز ان يكون بعض افراد الموضوع موجوداً فيصدق الموجبة الجزئية الصغرى وبعضها غير موجود فيصدق النتيجة السالبة الجزئية.
لانا نقول: لانسلم ذلك ، لان موضوع النتيجة هو الموضوع فى الصغرى فاذا ثبت ان موضوع الصغرى [؟] القول بانتفاء الموضوع في النتيجة والا لاختلف موضوع الصغرى و موضوع [؟] يصح موجود فلا النتيجة. (ميرزا محمد علی)
(۱۵) قوله: «لان الاصل صادق يريد ان هيهنا ثلاثة اشياء أصل القضية ونقيض العكس و هيئة التأليف و هذا المجال لابد وان يكون ناشئاً عن احدها لاسبيل الى الأول لانه مفروض الصدق و لا الى الثالث لانه الشكل الاول و هو بين الانتاج فتعين ان يكون ناشئاً عن الثاني و هذا معنى قوله: منشأه هو نقيض العكس». (میرزا محمد علی)
On the conversion of the universal negative. (14) i.e., the self-negation of a thing is impossible because it is established that the affirmation of a thing for itself is necessary. It cannot be said: we do not accept its impossibility, as it is possible to negate a thing from itself when it does not exist. We say: Even though the truth of a negative may be due to the non-existence of its subject or due to the non-existence of the predicate with the existence of the subject, here it is only due to the non-existence of the predicate, necessitating the existence of the subject here where the truth of the contradictory of the converse is assumed, which is the particular affirmative. It cannot be said: we do not accept that the truth of the negative here is due to the non-existence of the predicate, and what is adduced does not serve as a proof, because it is possible that some individuals of the subject exist, so the particular affirmative minor is true, and some do not exist, so the particular negative conclusion is true. We say: we do not accept that, because the subject of the conclusion is the subject in the minor. So if it is established that the subject of the minor exists, it cannot be said that the subject is non-existent in the conclusion; otherwise the subject of the minor and the subject of the conclusion would differ. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (15) His statement: 'Because the original is true' means that here there are three things: the original proposition, the contradictory of the converse, and the syllogistic form. And this impossibility must arise from one of them. It cannot be from the first because it is assumed true; nor from the third because it is the first figure and it is evidently productive. So it must arise from the second. This is the meaning of his statement: 'Its source is the contradictory of the converse.' (Mirza Muhammad Ali) (16) That is: We combine it with the original by making it the major premise of the first figure because it is a universal negative, and the original the minor premise because it is affirmative. So we say: Every human is an animal necessarily or always, and always no animal is a human as long as it is an animal, which yields: No human is a human necessarily or always, which is false because self-negation is impossible. Then we say: This impossibility did not arise from the original which is the minor because it is assumed true; nor from the form because it is productive. So it must arise from the major which is the contradictory of the converse, because the parts are limited to these. And if the contradictory entails an impossibility, it is impossible, because that which entails an impossibility is impossible. So if it is impossible, then the converse is true, because the simultaneous denial of the two contradictories is impossible. That is what was required. The same applies to the two universal affirmatives, so we do not repeat it here. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage from the Yazdi Hashiya provides a detailed commentary on the proof that the universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No A is B') converts to itself (i.e., 'No B is A'). The method is a reductio ad absurdum using a first-figure syllogism. The commentator (Mirza Muhammad Ali) explains the proof and addresses objections. The proof proceeds as follows: Assume the original universal negative is true (e.g., 'No human is a stone'). Denying its conversion would mean that the converse 'No stone is a human' is false, so its contradictory (a particular affirmative 'Some stone is a human') is true. Now combine this particular affirmative with the original universal negative in a first-figure syllogism: the original serves as minor (affirmative) and its contradictory as major (negative). But the first figure requires the major to be universal. The commentator then gives an example with 'human' and 'animal' to show that the result is a self-negation (e.g., 'No human is human'), which is absurd. Since the absurdity cannot come from the true original or the valid syllogistic form, it must come from assuming the contradictory of the converse is true. Therefore, the contradictory must be false, so the conversion holds. The commentary also responds to potential objections about existential import and the possibility of a particular negative conclusion.

Resolving Partial Premises via Supposition (Iftirad)
Dialectical resolution of first-figure partial premises via iftirad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of supposition (iftirad) to derive a valid conclusion from a first-figure syllogism with a partial major premise.

Core Idea
When the major premise is partial, supposition of an individual saves the syllogism.

The first figure requires the major premise to be universal. But what if the original premise is partial? The logicians discuss solutions, but the author introduces a subtle method: supposing a concrete individual (iftirad). This lesson walks through the problem and the elegant resolution using an example from the text: 'writer' and 'finger-mover'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
In the first figure, if the major premise is partial (juz'iyya), the syllogism cannot produce a certain conclusion. The author addresses an objection to this rule and explores alternative arrangements of premises.
The original proposition to be proven is called 'asl'.
2
Review the failed alternatives
The objector suggests making the contradictory (naqid) the major premise, but that makes the minor negative. Another proposal swaps the roles of premise parts, but the major remains partial and the inference fails.
The goal is to avoid the drawback of a negative or partial premise.
3
Understand iftirad (supposition)
The author proposes to suppose a specific individual that satisfies the description in the original partial premise. For example, suppose 'Amr is a writer who is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer, but not always.
This individual serves as a concrete test case.
4
Apply iftirad to derive the conclusion
From the supposition, we deduce that 'Amr is a finger-mover and not actually a writer (otherwise he would be always a writer, contradicting 'not always'). Thus, some finger-mover is not actually a writer.
This conclusion is the non-permanence of the converse.
5
Recognize the advantage
The iftirad method works for both universal and partial originals, unlike the earlier alternatives. It provides a general way to validate inferences with partial premises.
This shows the elegance and power of the suppositional approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(محمد على)
It cannot be said: This is if we make the contradictory (naqid) the minor and the first or second part the major. But if we make the contradictory the major, it is valid absolutely because it is universal absolutely. We say: Then another drawback occurs, namely the minor being negative, because the second part of the original must be negative as is apparent. If it is said: We make first the first part of the original the minor and the contradictory the major, and second we make the contradictory the minor and the second part of the original the major, so no drawback occurs. We say: In the second also, the major is partial, so the drawback is not repelled, because it invalidates the inference entirely. For the basis of inference is to join the contradictory to the first part of the original, producing a result that [?] in the result joining it to the second part of the original. According to what was mentioned, the result from joining it to the first part is 'some writer is always a writer' for example, and the result from joining it to the second part is 'some finger-mover is not actually a finger-mover' for example. Where is the incompatibility between them? Thus, if the original is partial, another way is needed, namely supposition (iftirad), such that in the mentioned example we suppose the entity that is described as writer and finger-mover as long as he is a writer, not always, for example 'Amr. So 'Amr is a finger-mover—that is apparent—and is not actually a writer; otherwise he would be always a writer and hence always a finger-mover, for we ruled in the original that he is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer, and he was a finger-mover not always. And if it is true of him that he is a finger-mover and not actually a writer, then it is true that some finger-mover is not actually a writer, and this is the concept of the non-permanence of the converse as previously mentioned. And if the judgment is explained in this way, it would be better because it applies to the universal and partial original, as is not hidden. (Muhammad 'Ali)

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange about the use of partial premises in the first figure of a categorical syllogism. The author is responding to an objection to the rule that partial premises in the first figure require the major to be universal. The objection suggests that if the contradictory of the conclusion is taken as the major premise, it can be universal and thus valid. The author counters that then the minor becomes negative, which is problematic. Further proposals to swap roles are refuted, showing the major remains partial. The author then introduces the method of supposition (iftirad) using an example with 'writer' and 'finger-mover' to demonstrate how a partial original can still produce a valid conclusion. This method is preferred as it works for both universal and partial originals.

Reductio Proof for Conversion of Durative Negatives
Author constructs a reductio proof that a durative negative does not convert to a universal affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a reductio proof to test the conversion of a categorical proposition, using the example of a durative negative premise.

Core Idea
A reductio proof derives an absurdity from the denial of the desired conversion, thus confirming the conversion (or its failure).

In Islamic logic, proving that a proposition converts requires showing that its contradictory leads to an impossible conclusion. The passage presents a classic example: testing whether a durative negative (e.g., 'No still-fingered is a writer always') can convert to a universal affirmative. The reductio argument combines the original premise with the contradictory of the desired converse to force a self-negation, establishing the failure of the conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The original is a durative negative: 'لا شيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب دائماً' (No still-fingered is a writer always). This is the premise whose conversion is being tested.
2
Assume the contradictory of the desired converse
The desired converse would be a universal affirmative: 'كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع دائماً' (Every writer is always still-fingered). Its contradictory is 'ليس كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع دائماً' (Not every writer is always still-fingered), but the passage uses a different assumption: it takes the contradictory of the universal quantity, which is the original's own contradictory? Actually, the passage says 'النقيض لكليته' meaning the contradictory of its universality, i.e., the contradictory of the universal affirmative. That would be the particular negative 'ليس كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع دائماً'. But the passage instead uses 'لا شيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب دائماً' (the original) as major? This step requires careful parsing: the reductio uses the original as major and assumes an affirmative minor that is the durative of the original as an affirmative? The guided path should follow the text.
The passage constructs the minor premise as 'كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع بالفعل' (Every writer is actually still-fingered), which is the affirmative version of the original but with 'actually' instead of 'always'.
3
Combine premises in a valid syllogism
Minor: Every writer is actually still-fingered (A). Major: No still-fingered is a writer always (E). The middle term is 'still-fingered'. After removing the repeated term, the conclusion is: No writer is a writer always (E). This is a valid categorical syllogism in Figure 1, mood AEE.
4
Observe the absurd conclusion
The conclusion 'لا شيء من الكاتب بكاتب دائماً' (Nothing from the writer is a writer always) negates a thing of itself, which is impossible (محال). This absurdity shows that the premises cannot both be true.
Since the original premise is assumed true (the durative negative), the absurdity must come from the assumed contradictory of the desired converse.
5
Conclude the conversion fails
Because assuming the desired universal affirmative converse leads to absurdity, the conversion is invalid. The original durative negative does not convert to a universal affirmative.
The reductio method proves the non-conversion by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بان يجعل لادوام الاصل لايجابه صغرى و النقيض لكليته كبرى فيقال كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع بالفعل ولاشيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب دائماً ، فبعد حذف المكرر ينتج: لاشيء من الكاتب بكاتب دائماً، فيلزم سلب الشيء عن نفسه وهو محال منشأه النقيض لكون الاصل مفروض الصدق والهيئة منتجة كما سبق. (میرزا محمدعلی)
By taking the durative [premise] of the original as an affirmative minor premise, and the contradictory of its universality as a major premise, so it is said: Every writer is actually still-fingered, and nothing from the still-fingered is a writer always. Then after removing the repeated term, it yields: Nothing from the writer is a writer always, so it necessitates the negation of a thing from itself, which is absurd, whose source is the contradictory [of the converse], because the original is assumed true and the syllogism is productive, as previously. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio argument (khalf) to show that a certain proposition does not convert. The author constructs a syllogism: take the durative (dā'iman) premise of the original as an affirmative minor (e.g., 'Every writer is actually still-fingered'), and then take the contradictory of its universal major (e.g., 'Nothing from the still-fingered is a writer always'). After canceling the repeated middle term ('still-fingered'), we get 'Nothing from the writer is a writer always', which is a self-negation (salb al-shay' 'an nafsihi), an absurdity. Since the original premise is assumed true and the syllogism is valid, the absurdity must be due to the falsity of the assumed contradictory, thus proving the desired converse. This method is typical for establishing conversions by reductio.

Key Terms
لادوام الأصل (li-dawām al-aṣl): the durative [proposition] which is the original premise, i.e., asserting always.
النقيض (al-naqīḍ): the contradictory, here the contradictory of the universal quantity of the original.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the absurd conclusion in the reductio argument from the passage?
A) Some writers are not still-fingered.
B) Nothing from the writer is a writer always.
C) Every writer is a writer always.
D) The original premise is false.

Key Takeaway
A reductio proof uses the assumption of the conflicting proposition to derive a self-contradiction, thereby confirming the original claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Reductio Proof That Universal Negatives Cannot Convert to Themselves
Demonstrates that converting a universal negative proposition leads to an absurdity if taken literally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the syllogistic proof that a universal negative proposition does not convert to a universal negative, and identify the absurdity that results.

Core Idea
Converting a universal negative to itself leads to self-negation, proving the conversion invalid.

In this passage, the commentator demonstrates why a universal negative (E) proposition cannot simply be converted to another universal negative. Using a categorical syllogism, he shows that if we try to convert 'No S is P' to 'No P is S', we end up with a contradiction: something is negated of itself. This reductio proof is a powerful tool for testing the validity of immediate inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and its components
The original universal negative is 'No writer is still-fingered always'. Its non-permanence aspect (lā-dawām al-aṣl) indicates that the negative claim is not eternal; at some time, the subject may have the predicate.
The non-permanence is extracted as an affirmative minor premise.
2
Construct the minor premise
From the original proposition, take the non-permanence and form an affirmative: 'Every writer is actually still-fingered' (i.e., at some time, each writer is still-fingered). This is the minor premise.
This step converts the negative modal aspect into a positive categorical statement.
3
Construct the major premise
The major premise is the contradictory of the original's universality – essentially the original proposition itself: 'Nothing still-fingered is a writer always'.
This is the original E proposition assumed true for the proof.
4
Apply the syllogism in the first figure
Combine minor (All W are S) and major (No S is always W) in the first figure (Celarent). The conclusion is 'No writer is always a writer', i.e., self-negation.
The middle term is 'still-fingered' (S). Mood: AEE (major E, minor A, conclusion E).
5
Recognize the absurdity
The conclusion negates a thing of itself ('No S is S'), which is impossible. Since the premises derived from the assumption that the original converts, this impossibility proves that the conversion is invalid.
The reductio shows that universal negatives cannot convert to themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بان يجعل لادوام الاصل لايجابه صغرى و النقيض لكليته كبرى فيقال كل كاتب ساكن الاصابع بالفعل ولاشيء من ساكن الاصابع بكاتب دائماً ، فبعد حذف المكرر ينتج: لاشيء من الكاتب بكاتب دائماً، فيلزم سلب الشيء عن نفسه وهو محال منشأه النقيض لكون الاصل مفروض الصدق والهيئة منتجة كما سبق. (میرزا محمدعلی)
By making the la-dawām (non-permanence) of the original proposition its affirmative minor premise, and the contradictory of its universality the major premise, so it is said: Every writer is actually still-fingered, and nothing still-fingered is a writer always. Then after deleting the repeated term, it yields: Nothing of the writer is a writer always. Thus self-negation follows, which is impossible, and its source is the contradiction because the original is assumed true and the form is productive, as before. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by reductio ad absurdum to show that a universal negative proposition ('No writer is still-fingered always') does not convert to another universal negative. The proof constructs a syllogism in the first figure. The minor premise is 'Every writer is actually still-fingered' (affirmative, taken from the original proposition's non-permanence). The major premise is 'Nothing still-fingered is a writer always' (the original proposition itself). After eliminating the middle term ('still-fingered'), the conclusion is 'Nothing of the writer is a writer always', which negates itself. This self-contradiction shows that the original cannot be true if its conversion is assumed valid. Hence the original does not convert.

Key Terms
لادوام الاصل
النقيض

Key Takeaway

Deriving the Converse Through a Hypothetical Individual
Commentary on inferring the converse using an assumption about a specific individual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use a hypothetical assumption to derive the converse of a categorical proposition.

Core Idea
Assuming an individual with two descriptions bridges the gap to the converse.

In classical logic, converting a proposition often requires more than mere inversion. This passage shows a technique: assume a specific individual that satisfies both the subject and a new term D, then use that to derive the converse. The method is crucial for certain modal conversions and exemplifies how logical reasoning integrates with commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the given proposition
We start with the known proposition: 'some J is B'. This is the original statement whose converse we aim to derive.
2
Introduce the assumption about a specific individual
We assume that there is a specific individual which is J and also described as D. In logical form: 'some J is D'. This D is a new term that we choose for the inference.
The assumption is hypothetical but must be about an actual individual for the inference to hold, as the commentary clarifies.
3
Infer that the assumed individual is B
Since that individual is J, and we know that some J is B (the original proposition), the individual must also be B. Therefore, we conclude that D is B (where D refers to that individual under the description D).
This step is not a categorical syllogism but an inference based on the identity of the subject.
4
Derive the converse from the intermediate conclusion
From 'D is B' and the fact that D is also J (by assumption), we can state that some B is J (the converse of the original). This is justified because if a thing is both B and J, then B is J for that thing.
This conversion is valid in the context of actual coexistence of descriptions, as discussed in the modal nuance.
5
Reflect on the modal condition
The commentary notes that the inference relies on the assumption that the description D actually applies to the individual (not just possibly). The chosen view is that the titular description (e.g., 'as a J') is actual, not merely possible. This ensures the logical step is sound.
If the description were merely possible, the inference might fail. Understanding this modal distinction is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فانه قد حكم فيه بان بعض ج ب و قد فرض هنا بعض ج، د فيكون د، ب ايضاً وهو المطلوب (محمد علی). هذا ليس علة لصدق ج على د مقيداً بهذه الجهة على ما يسبق الى الوهم فان صدق وصف الموضوع على ذاته ضروري لا يحتاج الى برهان بل هو علة لتقييده بهذه الجهة خاصة فانه قد اختلف في ان اتصاف ذات الموضوع بالوصف العنوانى هل هو بالامكان او بالفعل؟ وقد سبق مفصلا في شرح قوله: «و لا عكس للممكنتين» ولما كان المختار هو الثانى استدل به فى اثبات المطلوب فتأمل. (میرزا محمد علی). یعنی: انه لماثبت ان د ب وج ثبت ان بعض ب ج بالفعل لان الوصفين اذا تقارنا في ذات يصح صدق كل واحد منهما على الآخر فى الجملة كما هو ظاهر. (محمد علي)
For it has been judged that some J is B, and here it is assumed that some J is D, so D is B also, which is the required. (Muhammad Ali) This is not a cause for the truth of J of D restricted by this modality, as might be supposed. For the truth of the description of the subject of itself is necessary and does not need proof, but it is a cause for its restriction by this modality specifically. For it has been disputed whether the qualification of the subject's entity with the titular description is by possibility or actuality. And it has been detailed previously in the explanation of his statement: 'And there is no conversion of the two possibility propositions.' And since the chosen view is the second, it is used in proving the required, so contemplate. (Mirza Muhammad Ali) Meaning: When it is established that D is B and J, it is established that some B is J actually, because when two descriptions coexist in an entity, it is valid for each to be predicated of the other in general, as is clear. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage combines three annotations (15, 16, 17) that explain the logical steps after assuming a specific individual. The first annotation states that from the assumption that some J is D, and since we already know that some J is B, we deduce that D is B, which is the required conclusion. The second annotation clarifies that this is not to prove that J is true of D, as that is self-evident (since D is assumed to be a J), but rather to restrict the predication to a specific modality (e.g., as long as it is J). There is a dispute about whether the qualification of the subject by the titular description (e.g., 'as a J') is by possibility or actuality. The chosen view is actuality, and this is used in proving the required. The third annotation explains that once it is established that D is both B and J, then actually some B is J, because when two descriptions coexist, each can be predicated of the other in general.

Key Terms
الوصف العنوانى (al-wasf al-'unwani): The titular description: the description by which the subject is identified (e.g., 'as a J'). [Note: Specific to modal conversion discussions.]
العكس

Assuming a specific individual with two descriptions can bridge the gap to derive a converse proposition, provided the descriptions are actual.
