Definition of Qiyas and Its Defense Against Objections
Definition of qiyas and defense against objections regarding verbal syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the definition of logical qiyas and defend its applicability to verbal syllogisms using the dialectical arguments from this passage.

Core Idea
The definition of qiyas covers both mental and verbal because the verbal is inseparable from its intended meaning.

This passage from a hashia on logic addresses a crucial ambiguity: does the definition of qiyas (syllogism) apply only to mental reasoning or also to spoken expressions? The author defends the definition by showing that verbal syllogism inherently entails the conclusion because it is defined as a meaningful composite. We will follow the dialectical exchange to understand how the author counters objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of qiyas
Find the phrase that defines qiyas as 'a statement composed of propositions that entails by itself another statement'. Note the components: genus (statement composed of propositions) and differentia (entails another statement by itself).
This definition is stated early in the passage.
2
Understand the mental vs. verbal distinction
The passage explains that 'statement' originally meant any utterance, then was restricted to meaningful utterance, then in logic to composite (mental or verbal). So the definition applies to both mental and verbal syllogisms.
The distinction hinges on whether the composite is in the mind or uttered.
3
Analyze the first objection and response
Objection: Uttering premises does not necessitate the conclusion, so verbal syllogism does not entail conclusion. Response: The verbal syllogism only exists when it indicates its meaning, and when that happens, the mental syllogism also occurs, so they are inseparable.
The key is that 'verbal syllogism' is defined by its meaningfulness.
4
Examine the second objection about ignorance of convention
Objection: For someone who does not understand the language, uttering premises does not produce a mental syllogism. Response: Such utterances are not considered a syllogism for that person, just as a sentence in Arabic is not speech for someone who doesn't know Arabic.
This reinforces that the definition applies relative to a knowledgeable person.
5
Consider the refined objection and final response
Objection: Even if the mental follows, the entailment in the definition is 'by itself', but here it seems the verbal entails only via the mental. Response: The verbal syllogism, by being intended to indicate its meaning (as part of the definition of 'statement'), entails the conclusion by itself because that intentional structure is integral.
The author insists that the entailment is intrinsic to the verbal syllogism's nature as a meaningful composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله اي مركب قد تقدم في صدر مباحث القضاياء ان القول في اصل اللغة بمعنى اللفظ مهملاً كان او موضوعاً ثم خص فى العرف العام باللفظ الموضوع مفرداً كان أو مركباً ثم خص في هذا الفن بالمركب معقولاً كان او ملفوظاً فهذا الحد يمكن ان يكون حداً لكل واحد من القياس اصطلاح المعقول والملفوظ فعلى الاول يراد بالقول والقضاياء المعقولة وعلى الثانى الملفوظة. فان قيل : ان القول الآخر الذي يسمى نتيجة لا يلزم القياس الملفوظ ضرورة ان التلفظ بالمقدمات لا يستلزم النتيجة فلا يجوز ان يكون هذا حداً للقياس الملفوظ . قلنا ممنوع لظهور ان النتيجة لازمة للقياس الملفوظ أيضاً و ذلك لان القول على هذا المركب الذي قصد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه ولا يصدق هذا على القياس الملفوظ الا اذا دل على معناه فاذا حصل القياس الملفوظ حصل القياس المعقول، فلا يتصور انفكاك القياس الملفوظ عن القياس المعقول حتى يلزم ما ذكر. و ما سبق الى بعض الاوهام من ان هذا لايصح بالنسبة الى من كان جاهلاً بالوضع وتكلم بالمقدمات القياسية فانه يتحقق ح القياس الملفوظ من دون المعقول فيلزم ما ذكر ايضاً، فهو مردود بانا لا نسلم كون تلك المقدمات الملفوظة قياساً بالنسبة الى ذلك الشخص و ان كان قياساً بالنسبة الى العالم بالوضع كما لا يعد مثل زيد قائم كلاماً بالنسبة الى الجاهل بالوضع مع انه كلام بالنسبة الى العالم. فان قلت غاية ما تحصل من الجواب ان القول الآخر يلزم القياس الملفوظ من حيث انه يلزم القياس المعقول اللازم للقياس الملفوظ و المذكور في الحدان القياس قول مؤلف من قضاياء يلزمه لذاته قول آخر، فلا يصح الحد بالنسبة الى القياس الملفوظ على هذا التقدير ايضاً. قلنا: لاتسلم ان لزوم القول الآخر للقياس الملفوظ على هذا التقدير ليس لذاته بعد ما ذكر سابقاً من ان المراد من القول هو «اللفظ المركب الذي قصد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه لان معنى الكلام ح ان القياس الملفوظ لفظ مقصود بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه يلزمه من حيث انه كذلك قول اخر اعتباراً
His saying: i.e., composite. It has already preceded at the beginning of the discussions on propositions that 'statement' (qawl) originally in language means an utterance, whether meaningless or meaningful. Then it was specified in general custom as a meaningful utterance, whether single or composite. Then it was specified in this art as a composite, whether mental or verbal. So this definition can be a definition for each of the syllogisms in the terminology of the mental and the verbal. In the first, by 'statement' and 'propositions' are meant mental, and in the second, verbal. If it is said: the other statement which is called the conclusion does not follow from the verbal syllogism, necessarily because uttering the premises does not necessitate the conclusion, so it is not permissible for this to be a definition of the verbal syllogism. We say: it is denied because it is evident that the conclusion is necessary for the verbal syllogism as well, because the statement in this composite, part of which is intended to indicate part of its meaning, and this does not apply to the verbal syllogism unless it indicates its meaning; so when the verbal syllogism occurs, the mental syllogism occurs, and separation of the verbal syllogism from the mental syllogism is inconceivable such that what was mentioned would follow. And what occurred to some imaginations that this is not valid with respect to someone who is ignorant of the convention and speaks the syllogistic premises, for then the verbal syllogism occurs without the mental, so what was mentioned would also follow, is refuted by us saying: we do not concede that those verbal premises are a syllogism with respect to that person, even if they are a syllogism with respect to the one knowing the convention, just as the utterance 'Zayd is standing' is not considered speech with respect to the one ignorant of the convention, while it is speech with respect to the knowledgeable one. If you say: the utmost obtained from the answer is that the other statement follows the verbal syllogism insofar as it follows the mental syllogism which is necessary for the verbal syllogism, but in the definition it is 'a syllogism is a statement composed of propositions that entails by itself another statement', so the definition is not valid for the verbal syllogism on this interpretation either. We say: we do not concede that the entailment of the other statement for the verbal syllogism on this interpretation is not by itself, after what was mentioned earlier that the intended meaning of 'statement' is 'the composite utterance part of which is intended to indicate part of its meaning', because the meaning of the speech here is that the verbal syllogism is an utterance part of which is intended to indicate part of its meaning, and it entails by virtue of being so another statement considered as.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This is a lengthy dialectical passage from the main text (likely a sharh or hashiya) discussing the definition of qiyas (syllogism). It begins by recalling that the term 'qawl' (statement) originally meant any utterance, then was restricted in general usage to meaningful utterance, and finally in logic to composite (whether mental or verbal). The definition of qiyas as 'a statement composed of propositions that entails by itself another statement' is thus applicable to both mental and verbal syllogisms. The objector argues that verbal syllogism does not entail the conclusion because merely uttering premises does not guarantee the conclusion; one might utter premises without drawing the conclusion. The answer denies this, arguing that the verbal syllogism only counts as such if it indicates its meaning, and when the verbal syllogism occurs, the mental syllogism also occurs because the utterance is intended to convey meaning. Thus they are inseparable. A further objection: for someone ignorant of the convention, uttering premises does not constitute a syllogism because they don't mentally infer. The response: such utterances are not considered a syllogism for that person, just as 'Zayd is standing' is not speech for someone who doesn't know Arabic. Finally, a refined objection: the entailment in the definition is 'by itself', but the answer implies the entailment is via the mental syllogism, not directly. The response: the verbal syllogism, by virtue of being intended to indicate meaning, entails the conclusion by itself because the meaning of 'statement' in the definition includes that intentional structure. This is a sophisticated defense of the definition against various criticisms.

القول
المعقول
الملفوظ
النتيجة

Defense of the Definition of Qiyas: Spoken and Conceptual Syllogism
Author defends the definition of qiyas against objections regarding spoken syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the definition of qiyas applies to both spoken and conceptual syllogisms and defend it against the objection that spoken premises do not entail the conclusion.

Core Idea
The spoken syllogism entails the conceptual, so the definition of qiyas covers both.

In this passage, the commentator defends the standard definition of qiyas by showing it applies not only to mental syllogisms but also to spoken ones. He addresses two key objections and clarifies the phrase 'by itself'. Understanding this defense is essential for correctly applying syllogistic reasoning in Islamic theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of qiyas
Locate the definition: 'qiyas is a saying composed of premises from which follows by itself another saying.' Write it down. Note the three components: saying (qawl), premises, and conclusion following by itself.
2
Understand the twofold application of 'qawl'
The commentator explains that 'qawl' in the definition includes both conceptual and spoken compounds. See how the same definition works for al-qiyas al-ma'qul (conceptual) and al-qiyas al-malfuz (spoken).
3
Reconstruct the first objection and response
The objector says: Spoken premises do not necessarily produce a conclusion in the mind. The response: Spoken qiyas, being a meaningful compound, necessarily implies the conceptual qiyas. So the conclusion is entailed indirectly.
The commentator adds that if someone utters premises without understanding, it is not a real qiyas for them.
4
Reconstruct the second objection and response
The second objector says: The conclusion follows only via the conceptual qiyas, not directly from the spoken form. The response: The spoken qiyas, as a meaningful utterance, directly entails the conceptual conclusion by itself, because the meaning is attached to the words.
The phrase 'by itself' means without an external premise, not without any mediation.
5
Interpret 'by itself' (li-dhatihi) correctly
The commentator clarifies that 'by itself' excludes the need for an extra premise (like an equality syllogism), but does not exclude the natural mediation between spoken words and their meaning.

Source Text

الدلالة على جزء معناه ولا يصدق هذا على القياس الملفوظ الا اذا دل على معناه فاذا حصل القياس الملفوظ حصل القياس المعقول، فلا يتصور انفكاك القياس الملفوظ عن القياس المعقول حتى يلزم ما ذكر. و ما سبق الى بعض الاوهام من ان هذا لايصح بالنسبة الى من كان جاهلاً بالوضع وتكلم بالمقدمات القياسية فانه يتحقق ح القياس الملفوظ من دون المعقول فيلزم ما ذكر ايضاً، فهو مردود بانا لا نسلم كون تلك المقدمات الملفوظة قياساً بالنسبة الى ذلك الشخص و ان كان قياساً بالنسبة الى العالم بالوضع كما لا يعد مثل زيد قائم كلاماً بالنسبة الى الجاهل بالوضع مع انه كلام بالنسبة الى العالم. فان قلت غاية ما تحصل من الجواب ان القول الآخر يلزم القياس الملفوظ من حيث انه يلزم القياس المعقول اللازم للقياس الملفوظ و المذكور في الحدان القياس قول مؤلف من قضاياء يلزمه لذاته قول آخر، فلا يصح الحد بالنسبة الى القياس الملفوظ على هذا التقدير ايضاً. قلنا: لاتسلم ان لزوم القول الآخر للقياس الملفوظ على هذا التقدير ليس لذاته بعد ما ذكر سابقاً من ان المراد من القول هو «اللفظ المركب الذي قصد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه لان معنى الكلام ح ان القياس الملفوظ لفظ مقصود بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه يلزمه من حيث انه كذلك قول اخر اعتباراً لمعنى الوصف في تعليق الحكم كما هو المشهور في الحدود والرسوم ولاريب في ان لزوم القول الآخر للقياس الملفوظ من هذه الحيثية انما هذه الحيثية انما هو لذاته من دون ملاحظة شيء آخر معه ولو سلم فنقول : ان المقصود من قوله: «لذاته» ان لا يكون استلزامه للقول الآخر بواسطة مقدمة خارجية كما في قياس المساواة على ها سيجيء، لا ان يكون استلزامه له بنفسه ومن دون ملاحظة شيء من الاشياء كما توهم و معلوم ان ما ذكر انما يأتى على هذا التقدير دون التقدير الاول. ثم لا يذهب عليك : ان ليس المراد من القول الآخر اللازم للقياس الا المعقول سواء جعل الحد للقياس المعقول أو الملفوظ او الاعم منهما لظهور ان واحداً منهما لا يستلزم التلفظ بالنتيجة بل تعلقها والاذعان بها في الذهن فلا تغفل (میرزا محمد علی)
It has already been stated at the beginning of the chapter on propositions that 'qawl' in the original language means utterance, whether meaningless or meaningful, then in common usage it became specific to meaningful utterance, whether simple or compound, then in this art it became specific to the compound, whether conceptual or spoken. So this definition can be a definition for each of the qiyas in the terminology of the conceptual and the spoken. On the first, by 'qawl' and 'qadaya' are meant the conceptual, and on the second, the spoken. If it is said: 'The other saying called the conclusion does not follow from the spoken qiyas necessarily, because uttering the premises does not entail the conclusion, so this cannot be a definition of the spoken qiyas.' We say: It is prevented because it is clear that the conclusion is necessary for the spoken qiyas as well, and that is because the qawl here is the compound whose part is intended to indicate part of its meaning, and this does not apply to the spoken qiyas unless it indicates its meaning. So when the spoken qiyas occurs, the conceptual qiyas occurs, so separation of the spoken qiyas from the conceptual is inconceivable such that what was mentioned would follow. And what came to some minds that this does not hold for someone ignorant of the convention who speaks the syllogistic premises, for then the spoken qiyas occurs without the conceptual, so what was mentioned would also follow, it is refuted by our saying: We do not concede that those spoken premises are a qiyas for that person, even if they are a qiyas for one knowledgeable of the convention, just as 'Zayd is standing' is not considered speech for one ignorant of the convention, though it is speech for one knowledgeable. If you say: The utmost gained from the answer is that the other saying follows the spoken qiyas insofar as it follows the conceptual qiyas which is necessary for the spoken qiyas, and what is mentioned in the definition is that 'qiyas is a saying composed of premises from which follows by itself another saying', so the definition is not valid for the spoken qiyas on this estimation either. We say: We do not concede that the following of the other saying from the spoken qiyas on this estimation is not by itself after what was mentioned earlier that what is meant by 'saying' is 'the compound utterance whose part is intended to indicate part of its meaning', for the meaning of the speech now is that the spoken qiyas is an utterance whose part is intended to indicate part of its meaning, from which follows, insofar as it is like that, another saying in consideration of the meaning of the description in the attribution of the judgment, as is well-known in definitions and descriptions. And there is no doubt that the following of the other saying from the spoken qiyas from this regard is by itself without observing anything else with it. And even if conceded, we say: What is meant by 'by itself' is that its entailing the other saying is not by means of an external premise, as in the syllogism of equality as will come, not that it entails it by itself without observing any of the things, as was imagined. And it is known that what was mentioned only applies on this estimation, not on the first estimation. Then let it not escape you that what is meant by the other saying necessary for the qiyas is only the conceptual, whether the definition is made for the conceptual qiyas or the spoken or the common between them, because it is clear that neither of them entails uttering the conclusion, but rather its mental attachment and assent. So do not be heedless. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a hashiya commentary defending the standard definition of qiyas as 'a saying composed of premises from which follows by itself another saying'. The author first clarifies that the term 'qawl' in the definition applies to both conceptual and spoken compounds, so the definition covers both types of syllogism. An objection is raised: for spoken qiyas, merely uttering premises does not guarantee the conclusion follows, so the definition fails. The author responds by explaining that spoken qiyas necessarily entails the conceptual qiyas because the spoken utterance indicates its meaning. If someone ignorant of the convention utters premises, it is not a real qiyas for them. A second objection claims the conclusion follows only via the conceptual, not directly. The author counters by arguing that the spoken qiyas, by virtue of being a meaningful compound, directly entails the conceptual conclusion by itself, without external premises. The phrase 'by itself' means without an extra premise, not without any mediation. Finally, the author stresses that the 'other saying' that follows is always the conceptual conclusion, even for spoken qiyas. This reflects the Islamic logical principle that syllogistic inference is a mental act, while spoken utterance is merely its expression. For example, in kalam, one says 'The world is originated; everything originated has a creator; therefore the world has a creator.' The spoken premises lead to a conceptual conviction.

Key Terms

القول
لذاته
القياس الملفوظ
القياس المعقول

A) That the conclusion follows without any mediation whatsoever.
B) That the conclusion follows without an external premise, not without any mediation.
C) That the conclusion follows only if the premises are uttered aloud.
D) That the conclusion follows regardless of the form of the syllogism.

Key Takeaway
The definition of qiyas covers both spoken and conceptual syllogisms because spoken qiyas necessarily implies the conceptual, and 'by itself' means without an external premise, not without any mediation at all.

The Exclusion of Poetic Syllogism from the Definition of Qiyas
Refining the definition of qiyas regarding poetic syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why poetic syllogism is excluded from the definition of qiyas when 'qadaya' means actual propositions, and identify the two main answers regarding its mention in definitions.

Core Idea
Poetic syllogism is excluded because its premises are imaginative, not assents.

This lesson addresses a critical refinement in the definition of qiyas (syllogism). The definition states that a syllogism is 'a statement composed of propositions' (mu'allaf min qadaya). But what counts as a proposition? The author of the hashia examines whether poetic syllogism, which uses imaginative premises (mukhayyilat), fits this definition. Understanding this nuance helps you see how precise the logical definition must be.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations of 'qadaya'
The author considers whether 'propositions' means actual propositions (qadaya bi al-fi'l) or potential propositions (qadaya bi al-quwwa). The immediate understanding is actual propositions.
2
Apply each interpretation to poetic syllogism
If actual, poetic syllogism is excluded because it uses imaginative premises (mukhayyilat), which are not assents (tasdiq). If potential, then conditional propositions would be included when considered with their converses.
3
Examine the two answers provided
The first answer says the mention of poetic and sophistical syllogisms in definitions is by way of digression (istitrad), as they are not part of the proof (hujja). The second answer says the second interpretation is meant, but conditional propositions are excluded because 'luzum' in definitions means acquisition (iktisab), not mere logical consequence.
4
Determine the implications for the definition of qiyas
The definition must be precise enough to include only proper syllogisms. The digression explanation allows the definition to remain simple while excluding pseudo-arguments.

Source Text

(۵) قوله : و يقوله مؤلف من قضاياء...»: المراد منه مافوق قضية واحدة ليتناول القياس المؤلف من قضيتين. ثم ان كان المراد بها ما هى قضية بالفعل كما هو المتبادر خرج القياس الشعري عن التعريف اذ انه مركب من المخيلات وقد عرفت انها ليست بتصديق و ان كان المراد بها ما هو قضية بالقوة سیجی دخلت القضية الشرطية بالنسبة الى عكسها اذ يصدق عليها أنها قضاياء بالقوة يلزمها قول أخر. هذا اذا كان التعريف القياس المعقول واما اذا كان للقياس الملفوظ ، فيمكن ان يقال ان المراد هو الشق الأول و القياس الشعرى وان لم يكن مركباً مما هو قضية بالفعل بحسب الحقيقة، الا انه مركب منها بحسب ما يفهم السامع من كلام القائل. و يمكن الجواب: بان المراد هو الشق الأول وذكر الشعرى و كذلك المغالطة و امثالها من باب الاستطراد لأنها ليست داخلة فى الحجة التى هى من التصديقات وانما الداخل فيها هو القياس المركب من مقدمات يقينية او ظنية كالبرهان و الخطابة مثلاً. ويمكن الجواب ايضاً بان المراد هو الشق الثاني ولا يدخل القضاياء الشرطية، لان المراد باللزوم في التعريفات ما هو بطريق الاكتساب (عبدالرحيم). (۶) بالرفع خبر «انّ» ای فلان المتبادر من القضاياء القضاياء الصريحة فهو من قبيل قولهم : مؤمن خير من كافر» (محمد على). (۷) قوله: (والجزء الثانى من المركبة ليس كذلك اى ليس قضية صريحة» اعترض عليه بانالوجعلنا بدل اللادوام مفهومه الصريح ، صدق عليه الحد ايضاً مع انه ليس من افراد المحدود وهكذا كل قضية ركبت مع اخرى كيف كانتا اعم من ان تكون من المركبات المصطلحة وان لا تكون؟ واجيب بان قيد الحيثية معتبر في التعريف اى القياس قول مؤلف يلزمه لذاته قول اخر من حيث انه مؤلف و ظاهر ان لزوم العكس المستوى والعكس النقيض للقضية ليس من حيث انه مؤلف والى هذا يشير بعض المحققين من شراح المتن حيث يقول: ان المراد من الاستلزام هيهنا، الاستلزام بطريق النظر واستلزام القضية لعكسها لايكون بطريق النظر، هذا. : و قد اجيب ايضاً بانه يخرج بقولنا: قول أخر حيث تجعل التنوين للوحدة، فان اللازم في عكس القضية المركبة قولان لا قول واحد. وفيه ان ذلك لايتم بالنسبة الى بعض القضاياء المركبة اعنى ما يكون عكسه قولاً واحداً كالوقتيتين والوجوديتين الموجبتين على ما ذكر سابقا. (ميرزا محمد علی)
(5) His saying: 'and it is a statement composed of propositions...' means what is above one proposition so that it includes the syllogism composed of two propositions. Then, if what is meant by them is what is an actual proposition, as is the immediate understanding, the poetic syllogism exits the definition because it is composed of imaginative premises (mukhayyilāt) and you have known that they are not tasdiq (assent). And if what is meant by them is what is a proposition in potentiality, then the conditional proposition enters with respect to its converse, because it is true that they are propositions in potentiality from which another statement follows. This is if the definition is for the intelligible syllogism. But if it is for the uttered syllogism, then it can be said that what is meant is the first aspect, and the poetic syllogism, even if it is not composed of what is an actual proposition in reality, yet it is composed of them according to what the listener understands from the speaker's speech. And it is possible to answer: that what is meant is the first aspect, and the mention of the poetic and likewise the sophistical and their likes is by way of digression (istitrād) because they are not included in the proof which is from among the assents; rather what is included in it is the syllogism composed of certain or probable premises like demonstration and oration for example. And it is also possible to answer: that what is meant is the second aspect, and conditional propositions do not enter because what is meant by necessity (luzūm) in definitions is what is by way of acquisition (iktiṣāb) (Abd al-Rahim). (6) In the nominative case, the predicate of 'inna', i.e., the immediate understanding from 'propositions' is explicit propositions, so it is of the type of their saying: 'A believer is better than a disbeliever' (Muhammad Ali). (7) His saying: 'And the second part of the compound is not like that', i.e., it is not an explicit proposition. An objection was raised against it: If we make instead of 'non-permanence' its explicit meaning, the definition would apply to it as well, while it is not among the individuals of the defined. And likewise every proposition composed with another, however they are, whether they are from the technical compounds or not. And it was answered: that the condition of aspect (al-ḥaythiyya) is considered in the definition, i.e., the syllogism is a statement composed that by itself entails another statement from the aspect that it is composed. And it is apparent that the entailment of the simple converse and the contrapositive of a proposition is not from the aspect that it is composed. And to this alludes some of the verifying commentators of the text when he says: what is meant by entailment here is entailment by way of consideration (naẓar), and the entailment of a proposition for its converse is not by way of consideration. This. And it was also answered that it exits by our saying: another statement (qawl ākhar) where the tanwīn is made for singularity, for what is entailed in the converse of a compound proposition is two statements, not one statement. But there is in it that this does not hold for some compound propositions, namely those whose converse is a single statement, like the two temporal (waqtiyyatayn) and the two existential (wujūdiyyatayn) affirmative propositions as mentioned previously. (Mirza Muhammad Ali).
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage continues the critical refinement of the definition of qiyas. The author (in the hāshiya) comments on the phrase 'mu'allaf min qadaya' (composed of propositions) from the main text. He first clarifies that 'mu'allaf min qadaya' means more than one proposition, so that a syllogism with two premises is included. Then he discusses whether 'qadaya' means actual propositions or potential propositions. If actual, then poetic syllogism (qiyās shi'rī) is excluded because it is composed of imaginative premises (mukhayyilāt), which are not assent (tasdiq). If potential, then conditional propositions in relation to their converse would enter, because they are potentially propositions that entail another statement. This dilemma applies if the definition is for the intelligible syllogism. For the uttered syllogism, one might say that even poetic syllogism, though not composed of actual propositions in reality, is understood by the listener as such, so it could be included. But the author provides two main answers. The first: the first interpretation (actual propositions) is meant, and the mention of poetic and sophistical syllogisms in definitions is by way of digression (istitrād), because they are not part of the proof (ḥujja) which consists of assents. The second answer: the second interpretation (potential propositions) is meant, and conditional propositions are excluded because 'luzūm' (necessity) in definitions means acquisition (iktiṣāb) not mere logical consequence. Then a marginal note clarifies that the immediate understanding of 'qadaya' is explicit propositions, like the saying 'A believer is better than a disbeliever'. Next, an objection is raised: if the second part of a compound (like a conditional) is not an explicit proposition, then the definition might apply to things like 'inverse of permanence' which are not syllogisms. The answer invokes the condition of 'ḥaythiyya' (aspect): the entailment must be from the aspect that it is composed, not from any other aspect. For example, the converse of a proposition is not entailed from the aspect of composition. Another answer uses the singular form 'qawl ākhar' to exclude cases where the entailment yields two statements (like in the converse of a compound conditional). However, this fails for some compounds whose converse is a single statement, like affirmative temporal and existential propositions.

Key Terms
مخيلات
الاستطراد
الاكتساب

D) Because it does not lead to a conclusion.

Key Takeaway
Poetic syllogism is excluded because its premises are imaginative (mukhayyilat), not assent (tasdiq), and thus not real propositions.

Refining the Definition: The Condition of Aspect (al-Haythiyya) and the Singular Conclusion
Refining the definition by introducing haythiyya and counterexample to singular conclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of 'al-haythiyya' to determine whether a given entailment qualifies as a syllogism, and critique the 'singular conclusion' answer using examples from temporal and existential propositions.

Core Idea
The entailment must be from the aspect of composition, not from any other aspect.

The definition of syllogism requires that the entailment is by itself (lidhatihi). But how do we exclude cases where a single proposition entails its converse? The hashia introduces the condition of 'al-haythiyya' (aspect): the entailment must be from the aspect that the statement is composed, not from any other aspect. Additionally, some scholars use the singular form 'another statement' (qawl akhar) to exclude cases where two statements follow. However, this fails for certain propositions whose converse is a single statement. In this lesson, you will learn to apply these refinements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem: defining out the converse of a single proposition
A single proposition (e.g., 'Every A is B') entails its converse ('Some B is A') by immediate inference. This should not be considered a syllogism because it is not a compound.
2
Understand the first solution: the condition of aspect (haythiyya)
The entailment must be from the aspect that the statement is composed (min haythu huwa mu'allaf). The converse's entailment is not from the aspect of composition; it is from the proposition's nature.
3
Understand the second solution: singular tanwin in 'qawl akhar'
Making 'another statement' indefinite (tanwin) for singularity implies that the entailment yields exactly one statement. The converse of a compound proposition (e.g., a conditional) usually yields two statements, so it is excluded.
4
Critique the second solution using temporal and existential propositions
For affirmative temporal and existential propositions, the converse is a single statement. For example, the affirmative temporal proposition 'Zayd existed at time t' has a converse 'Something existed at time t' which is one statement. Thus the tanwin solution fails for these.
The passage mentions 'al-waqtiyyatayn wa al-wujūdiyyatayn al-mūjibatayn' as counterexamples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۵) قوله : و يقوله مؤلف من قضاياء...»: المراد منه مافوق قضية واحدة ليتناول القياس المؤلف من قضيتين. ثم ان كان المراد بها ما هى قضية بالفعل كما هو المتبادر خرج القياس الشعري عن التعريف اذ انه مركب من المخيلات وقد عرفت انها ليست بتصديق و ان كان المراد بها ما هو قضية بالقوة سیجی دخلت القضية الشرطية بالنسبة الى عكسها اذ يصدق عليها أنها قضاياء بالقوة يلزمها قول أخر. هذا اذا كان التعريف القياس المعقول واما اذا كان للقياس الملفوظ ، فيمكن ان يقال ان المراد هو الشق الأول و القياس الشعرى وان لم يكن مركباً مما هو قضية بالفعل بحسب الحقيقة، الا انه مركب منها بحسب ما يفهم السامع من كلام القائل. و يمكن الجواب: بان المراد هو الشق الأول وذكر الشعرى و كذلك المغالطة و امثالها من باب الاستطراد لأنها ليست داخلة فى الحجة التى هى من التصديقات وانما الداخل فيها هو القياس المركب من مقدمات يقينية او ظنية كالبرهان و الخطابة مثلاً. ويمكن الجواب ايضاً بان المراد هو الشق الثاني ولا يدخل القضاياء الشرطية، لان المراد باللزوم في التعريفات ما هو بطريق الاكتساب (عبدالرحيم). (۶) بالرفع خبر «انّ» ای فلان المتبادر من القضاياء القضاياء الصريحة فهو من قبيل قولهم : مؤمن خير من كافر» (محمد على). (۷) قوله: (والجزء الثانى من المركبة ليس كذلك اى ليس قضية صريحة» اعترض عليه بانالوجعلنا بدل اللادوام مفهومه الصريح ، صدق عليه الحد ايضاً مع انه ليس من افراد المحدود وهكذا كل قضية ركبت مع اخرى كيف كانتا اعم من ان تكون من المركبات المصطلحة وان لا تكون؟ واجيب بان قيد الحيثية معتبر في التعريف اى القياس قول مؤلف يلزمه لذاته قول اخر من حيث انه مؤلف و ظاهر ان لزوم العكس المستوى والعكس النقيض للقضية ليس من حيث انه مؤلف والى هذا يشير بعض المحققين من شراح المتن حيث يقول: ان المراد من الاستلزام هيهنا، الاستلزام بطريق النظر واستلزام القضية لعكسها لايكون بطريق النظر، هذا. : و قد اجيب ايضاً بانه يخرج بقولنا: قول أخر حيث تجعل التنوين للوحدة، فان اللازم في عكس القضية المركبة قولان لا قول واحد. وفيه ان ذلك لايتم بالنسبة الى بعض القضاياء المركبة اعنى ما يكون عكسه قولاً واحداً كالوقتيتين والوجوديتين الموجبتين على ما ذكر سابقا. (ميرزا محمد علی)
(5) His saying: 'and it is a statement composed of propositions...' means what is above one proposition so that it includes the syllogism composed of two propositions. Then, if what is meant by them is what is an actual proposition, as is the immediate understanding, the poetic syllogism exits the definition because it is composed of imaginative premises (mukhayyilāt) and you have known that they are not tasdiq (assent). And if what is meant by them is what is a proposition in potentiality, then the conditional proposition enters with respect to its converse, because it is true that they are propositions in potentiality from which another statement follows. This is if the definition is for the intelligible syllogism. But if it is for the uttered syllogism, then it can be said that what is meant is the first aspect, and the poetic syllogism, even if it is not composed of what is an actual proposition in reality, yet it is composed of them according to what the listener understands from the speaker's speech. And it is possible to answer: that what is meant is the first aspect, and the mention of the poetic and likewise the sophistical and their likes is by way of digression (istitrād) because they are not included in the proof which is from among the assents; rather what is included in it is the syllogism composed of certain or probable premises like demonstration and oration for example. And it is also possible to answer: that what is meant is the second aspect, and conditional propositions do not enter because what is meant by necessity (luzūm) in definitions is what is by way of acquisition (iktiṣāb) (Abd al-Rahim). (6) In the nominative case, the predicate of 'inna', i.e., the immediate understanding from 'propositions' is explicit propositions, so it is of the type of their saying: 'A believer is better than a disbeliever' (Muhammad Ali). (7) His saying: 'And the second part of the compound is not like that', i.e., it is not an explicit proposition. An objection was raised against it: If we make instead of 'non-permanence' its explicit meaning, the definition would apply to it as well, while it is not among the individuals of the defined. And likewise every proposition composed with another, however they are, whether they are from the technical compounds or not. And it was answered: that the condition of aspect (al-ḥaythiyya) is considered in the definition, i.e., the syllogism is a statement composed that by itself entails another statement from the aspect that it is composed. And it is apparent that the entailment of the simple converse and the contrapositive of a proposition is not from the aspect that it is composed. And to this alludes some of the verifying commentators of the text when he says: what is meant by entailment here is entailment by way of consideration (naẓar), and the entailment of a proposition for its converse is not by way of consideration. This. And it was also answered that it exits by our saying: another statement (qawl ākhar) where the tanwīn is made for singularity, for what is entailed in the converse of a compound proposition is two statements, not one statement. But there is in it that this does not hold for some compound propositions, namely those whose converse is a single statement, like the two temporal (waqtiyyatayn) and the two existential (wujūdiyyatayn) affirmative propositions as mentioned previously. (Mirza Muhammad Ali).
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage continues the critical refinement of the definition of qiyas. The author (in the hāshiya) comments on the phrase 'mu'allaf min qadaya' (composed of propositions) from the main text. He first clarifies that 'mu'allaf min qadaya' means more than one proposition, so that a syllogism with two premises is included. Then he discusses whether 'qadaya' means actual propositions or potential propositions. If actual, then poetic syllogism (qiyās shi'rī) is excluded because it is composed of imaginative premises (mukhayyilāt), which are not assent (tasdiq). If potential, then conditional propositions in relation to their converse would enter, because they are potentially propositions that entail another statement. This dilemma applies if the definition is for the intelligible syllogism. For the uttered syllogism, one might say that even poetic syllogism, though not composed of actual propositions in reality, is understood by the listener as such, so it could be included. But the author provides two main answers. The first: the first interpretation (actual propositions) is meant, and the mention of poetic and sophistical syllogisms in definitions is by way of digression (istitrād), because they are not part of the proof (ḥujja) which consists of assents. The second answer: the second interpretation (potential propositions) is meant, and conditional propositions are excluded because 'luzūm' (necessity) in definitions means acquisition (iktiṣāb) not mere logical consequence. Then a marginal note clarifies that the immediate understanding of 'qadaya' is explicit propositions, like the saying 'A believer is better than a disbeliever'. Next, an objection is raised: if the second part of a compound (like a conditional) is not an explicit proposition, then the definition might apply to things like 'inverse of permanence' which are not syllogisms. The answer invokes the condition of 'ḥaythiyya' (aspect): the entailment must be from the aspect that it is composed, not from any other aspect. For example, the converse of a proposition is not entailed from the aspect of composition. Another answer uses the singular form 'qawl ākhar' to exclude cases where the entailment yields two statements (like in the converse of a compound conditional). However, this fails for some compounds whose converse is a single statement, like affirmative temporal and existential propositions.

Key Terms
الحيثية
الوقتيتين والوجوديتين
الاستلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Why does the 'singular tanwin' solution fail for affirmative temporal propositions?
A) Because they are not compound propositions.
B) Because their converse yields two statements.
C) Because their converse yields a single statement.
D) Because they are not considered in the definition.

Key Takeaway
The condition of haythiyya (aspect) refines the definition of qiyas, and the singular conclusion solution is insufficient for some compound propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition 'Every prayer is obligatory.' Does the entailment of its converse 'Some obligatory is prayer' satisfy the condition of haythiyya? Why or why not?
?
Take the compound proposition 'If the moon is eclipsed, then it is dark.' Its converse (by contraposition) is 'If it is not dark, then the moon is not eclipsed.' Does the singular tanwin solution exclude this? What about an affirmative temporal proposition like 'Zayd existed at the time of prayer'? Check its converse.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Excluding Induction, Analogy, and Material Consequences from Qiyas
Argues that the phrase 'yalzamu' excludes induction and analogy, and addresses the luzum condition for non-demonstrative syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify arguments that are formally valid versus those true only due to particular matter, and explain why induction and analogy are not considered syllogisms in classical logic.

Core Idea
Induction and analogy lack formal entailment; they are material, not syllogistic.

The definition of qiyas (syllogism) includes the condition that the conclusion must necessarily follow from the premises. This condition excludes reasoning forms that do not guarantee necessity. In this lesson, we explore how induction, analogy, and material consequences are excluded by this condition, using concrete examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exclusion clause
The phrase 'yalzamuhu' (it entails) is used to exclude induction and analogy. Why? Because they do not produce a necessary logical consequence.
Induction moves from particulars to a universal, but does not guarantee the conclusion necessarily.
2
Examine material consequence examples
The author gives two syllogistic forms that happen to be true but only due to the specific content (particular matter). Change the content and the conclusion fails. This shows that the necessity must be formal, not material.
Example: 'No horse is a speaker; every speaker is human; therefore no horse is human.' The conclusion is true only because of the material relation between horse and human.
3
Distinguish material from formal
Formal entailment holds regardless of content; material truth depends on the specific terms. The syllogism is defined by formal necessity.
If you replace the major premise with 'Every speaker is an animal', the conclusion 'No horse is an animal' does not follow, showing the argument's dependence on material.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(8) His saying: 'And by his saying "yalzamuhu" (it entails) induction and analogy are excluded.' And their interpretation will come after this, God willing. Then, just as they both are excluded by it, also excluded is what is true of the other statement by virtue of particular matter (khāṣṣiyyat al-māddah), like our saying: 'No horse is a speaker, and every speaker is human,' for it is true that 'No horse is human.' And like our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and some animals are speakers,' for it is true that 'Every human is a speaker.' For their truth in them is not necessary for them but is due to the particular matter. Do you not see that if we change the major premise in the first example to our saying: 'Every speaker is an animal,' it is not true that 'No horse is an animal'? And in the second example, if we change it to our saying: 'Some animals are whinnying,' it is not true that 'Some humans are whinnying'? There remains here something: that by taking the condition of necessary consequence in the definition, the rhetorical, dialectical, poetic, and sophistical syllogisms are excluded, while they are individuals of the defined. For none of them entails something else due to their not producing certainty, as will come at the end of the book. Therefore, it is better to restrict necessary consequence by our saying: 'when they are conceded (mata sullimat)' so as to be safe from this, as did a group of predecessors (salaf) when they said: 'The syllogism is a statement composed of propositions which, when they are conceded, a statement follows from them.' And some verifying commentators of the text have pointed out the refutation of this objection when he said: 'What is meant by entailment is that the premises are such that when knowledge of them is obtained, knowledge of the result is obtained. So there is no justification for considering concession in the premises as occurred in the phrase of the predecessors.' But there is in it that this meaning is not understood from the word when used absolutely, so one should not pay attention to its likes in the position of definition. So it is better to cling, in the refutation, to the consideration of the condition of aspect (al-ḥaythiyya) in the definition, i.e., it entails from the aspect that it is itself (min haythu huwa huwa), meaning from
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage addresses the exclusion clause in the definition of qiyas. The author (in the hāshiya) comments on the phrase 'yalzamuhu' (it entails) from the main text, which is meant to exclude induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthīl). He explains that similarly, it excludes cases where the conclusion follows due to the particular matter (khāṣṣiyyat al-māddah) rather than the form. He gives two examples: the first, from premises 'No horse is a speaker' and 'Every speaker is human', it follows that 'No horse is human' – but only because of the material fact that 'horse' and 'human' are related, not due to syllogistic form. If we change the major premise to 'Every speaker is an animal', the conclusion 'No horse is an animal' does not follow. Similarly, from 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some animals are speakers', it follows that 'Every human is a speaker' only due to the material connection. This shows that the condition of necessary consequence (luzūm) is meant to exclude such material entailments, focusing on formal entailment. However, this condition also excludes rhetorical, dialectical, poetic, and sophistical syllogisms because they do not provide certainty (yaqīn). Yet these are supposed to be included as types of qiyas. To solve this, some earlier scholars (salaf) added the condition 'mata sullimat' (when they are conceded), meaning the conclusion follows on the assumption that the premises are conceded. But a verifying commentator objects that the term 'istilzām' (entailment) in definitions means that if the premises are known, the conclusion is known, not that they need to be conceded. However, this meaning is not evident from the word alone. Therefore, the author recommends adhering to the condition of 'ḥaythiyya' (aspect): the entailment is from the aspect that the premises are composed, not from any other aspect. This preserves the inclusion of non-demonstrative syllogisms because even though they do not produce certainty by themselves, they entail a conclusion from the aspect of composition, just not with certainty. The text ends abruptly, but the idea is that the condition of aspect works better.

Key Terms
الاستقراء (al-istiqrā'): Induction; excluded from the definition of qiyas because it does not entail a conclusion necessarily. [Note: Standard term; no shift.]
التمثيل (al-tamthīl): Analogical reasoning; also excluded from qiyas proper in this context. [Note: Often used for analogy; here distinct from syllogism.]
خصوص المادة (khuṣūṣ al-māddah): Particular matter; as opposed to form, the specific content of premises that yields truth but not formal necessity. [Note: Standard in logic; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author exclude induction from the definition of qiyas?
A) It does not produce certainty.
B) It does not entail a conclusion necessarily.
C) It is not composed of propositions.
D) It uses particular matter.

Key Takeaway
Induction and analogy are excluded from qiyas because they do not guarantee formal necessary consequence, only material or probable truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; this drink is an intoxicant; therefore it is forbidden.' Is this a formal syllogism or a material one? Explain based on the condition of necessary consequence.
?
Identify whether the following is a formal syllogism: 'Most scholars are competent; Zaid is a scholar; therefore Zaid is competent.' Is this valid? Why or why not?

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The Problem of Necessary Consequence and Non-Demonstrative Syllogisms
Argues that the phrase 'yalzamu' excludes induction and analogy, and addresses the luzum condition for non-demonstrative syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the two proposed refinements to the definition of qiyas (mata sullimat vs. haythiyya) and evaluate their adequacy in including non-demonstrative syllogisms.

Core Idea
The luzum condition protects form over matter but risks excluding valid argument types; the 'aspect' condition preserves all qiyas.

The condition that the conclusion must be a necessary consequence of the premises works well to exclude induction and analogy, but it also excludes rhetorical, dialectical, poetic, and sophistical syllogisms because they don't produce certainty. This created a problem: these are still considered types of qiyas. Two solutions were proposed: adding 'when they are conceded' or emphasizing the aspect of composition. This lesson examines these solutions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The luzum condition in the definition of qiyas implies that the premises must yield certain knowledge of the conclusion. But rhetorical and dialectical syllogisms do not yield certainty, so they are excluded, which is undesirable because they are still considered qiyas.
These types are mentioned as 'individuals of the defined' (afrād al-mahdūd), so they should be included.
2
Examine the first solution: 'mata sullimat'
Some scholars added 'when they are conceded' to the definition, meaning the conclusion follows under the assumption that the premises are granted. This includes non-demonstrative syllogisms because they still produce a conclusion given concession.
This is the approach of the salaf (predecessors). However, it is criticized because the meaning of 'istilzām' is about knowledge, not concession.
3
Examine the second solution: al-ḥaythiyya
The commentator argues that the entailment should be understood 'from the aspect that the composition is such', not from the aspect of material truth or knowledge. This better captures formal necessity without requiring actual knowledge or certainty.
The author prefers this solution because it does not rely on the added condition of concession, which is not evident from the word.

Similarly, from 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some animals are speakers', it follows that 'Every human is a speaker' only due to the material connection. This shows that the condition of necessary consequence (luzūm) is meant to exclude such material entailments, focusing on formal entailment. However, this condition also excludes rhetorical, dialectical, poetic, and sophistical syllogisms because they do not provide certainty (yaqīn). Yet these are supposed to be included as types of qiyas. To solve this, some earlier scholars (salaf) added the condition 'mata sullimat' (when they are conceded), meaning the conclusion follows on the assumption that the premises are conceded. But a verifying commentator objects that the term 'istilzām' (entailment) in definitions means that if the premises are known, the conclusion is known, not that they need to be conceded. However, this meaning is not evident from the word alone. Therefore, the author recommends adhering to the condition of 'ḥaythiyya' (aspect): the entailment is from the aspect that the premises are composed, not from any other aspect. This preserves the inclusion of non-demonstrative syllogisms because even though they do not produce certainty by themselves, they entail a conclusion from the aspect of composition, just not with certainty. The text ends abruptly, but the idea is that the condition of aspect works better.

Key Terms
اللزوم (al-luzūm): Necessary consequence; in the definition, it must be formal, not material. [Note: Used in many contexts; here specifically logical necessity.]
متى سلمت (mata sullimat): When they are conceded; a proposed restriction to include non-demonstrative syllogisms. [Note: Found in earlier definitions (salaf); later criticized as not capturing the intended meaning.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did some scholars add 'mata sullimat' to the definition of qiyas?
A) To include induction.
B) To include material consequences.
C) To include rhetorical and dialectical syllogisms.
D) To exclude analogy.

Key Takeaway
The definition of qiyas must be broad enough to include non-demonstrative syllogisms; the 'aspect' condition successfully achieves this without introducing the problem of concession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a dialectical syllogism on the issue of the world's creation (e.g., 'If the world is temporally originated, it has a creator; the world is temporally originated; therefore it has a creator'). Explain why it is a valid qiyas under the haythiyya condition even if the opponent does not accept the premises.
?
Compare the two solutions: Would the 'mata sullimat' condition also include the above syllogism? Why might the haythiyya condition be preferred?

Defending the Definition of Qiyas: Intrinsic Entailment in All Figures
Author defends the definition of qiyas against the objection that other figures require reduction to first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a syllogism in the second, third, or fourth figure still has entailment 'li dhatihi' despite requiring reduction to the first figure, and distinguish intrinsic entailment from self-evident entailment.

Core Idea
A syllogism's conclusion follows from its premises intrinsically, even if that entailment isn't obvious without conversion.

In defining qiyas, the author requires the conclusion to follow 'for itself' (li dhatihi). This raises an objection: what about figures other than the first, since they need reduction? The author clarifies that intrinsic entailment does not require self-evidence; reduction is only for knowing, not for being. Let's examine this critical refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The definition using 'li dhatihi' seems to exclude second, third, and fourth figures because their entailment appears to require reduction to the first figure, so it is not 'for itself'.
2
Pinpoint the response
The author says 'luzum' is general, including self-evident and non-self-evident. In these figures, the entailment is intrinsic (min haythu al-dhat) in all materials, even if not self-evident.
3
Contrast with qiyas al-musawat
Qiyas al-musawat (syllogism of equality) lacks intrinsic entailment entirely because it sometimes fails in certain materials, proving that its entailment is not li dhatihi.
4
Grasp the nuance
Reduction to the first figure is needed only to acquire knowledge of the entailment, not for the entailment itself, which is already present.
5
Understand 'extraneous premise'
An extraneous premise is one not entailed by the original premises and with different terms; intrinsic entailment means no such premise is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله بقوله لذاته خرج ما يلزم منه قول أخر ...»: ربما يتوهم انه يخرج به القياس المركب على هيئة الشكل الثانى والثالث والرابع فان استلزامه النتيجة فيها ليس لذاته بل تحتاج في انتاجه الى رده الى الشكل الاول على ما سيأتي فيكون لزوم النتيجة للقياس في هذه الاشكال بواسطة الشكل الاول لانذاته فيلزم خروج بعض افراد المعرف عن التعريف فلا يكون منعكساً. و الجواب: ان المراد من اللزوم اعم من البين وغير البين و لزوم القول الآخر للقياس في هيئة تلك الاشكال من قبيل الثانى و هذا بخلاف قياس المساواة فان اللزوم فيه ليس لذاته مطلقا بدليل التخلف في بعض المواد كما سيأتي بخلاف هذه الاشكال فان اللزوم ثابت فيها من حيث حيث الذات في جميع المواد غاية الامر انه ليس ببين وهذا لا يخرجه عن ان يكون لزومه له لذاته كما هو ظاهر لارباب الدراية وبعبارة اخرى اخصر من ذلك انه لا احتياج فى انتاج القياس فيها الى شيء من الرد الى الشكل الاول وغيره مما سيأتي. و انما يحتاج اليه لتحصيل العلم بالانتاج لالنفس الانتاج فانه حاصل لذاته كما هو ظاهر ولو سلم فالمراد من اللزوم لذاته ان لا يكون بواسطة مقدمة غريبة. والمراد من المقدمة الغريبة ما لا يكون لازمة للمقدمتين او لازمة لاحد هما لكن طرفاها مغايران الحدود تلك المقدمة فافهم . (محمد على)
His statement: 'by his statement 'for itself' excludes what entails another statement...' – It might be mistakenly thought that it excludes the syllogism composed in the form of the second, third, and fourth figures, because the entailment of the conclusion in them is not for itself (li dhatihi), but they need to be reduced to the first figure in order to produce the conclusion, as will come. Thus the entailment of the conclusion for the syllogism in these figures is through the first figure, not for itself, so some individuals of the definiendum would be excluded from the definition, so it would not be convertible. And the answer: What is meant by 'entailment' is general, including the self-evident and the non-self-evident. The entailment of another statement for the syllogism in the form of those figures is of the second type. This is contrary to the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawat), for the entailment in it is not for itself absolutely, evidenced by its failure in some materials, as will come. Contrary to these figures, the entailment is established in them from the perspective of essence (min haythu al-dhat) in all materials, the only issue being that it is not self-evident. And this does not remove it from being an entailment for itself, as is apparent to people of acumen. In other words, more concise: there is no need in the production of the syllogism in them for any reduction to the first figure or anything else that will come. It is only needed for acquiring knowledge of the entailment, not for the entailment itself, for it is obtained for itself, as is apparent. And if it is granted, then what is meant by 'entailment for itself' is that it is not through an extraneous premise. And what is meant by 'extraneous premise' is that it is not entailed by the two premises, nor by one of them, and its two extremes are different from the limits of that premise, so understand. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the definition of qiyas, addressing a potential objection. The objection is that if the definition requires the conclusion to follow 'for itself' (li dhatihi), then syllogisms in the second, third, and fourth figures would be excluded, because they require reduction to the first figure to make the conclusion evident. The author responds by clarifying that 'entailment' (luzum) includes both self-evident (bayyin) and non-self-evident (ghayr bayyin). In the second, third, and fourth figures, the entailment is intrinsic but not self-evident; it exists 'from the perspective of essence' (min haythu al-dhat) in all valid moods, even though one may need to convert or reduce to see it. The author contrasts this with qiyas al-musawat (the syllogism of equality), where entailment is not intrinsic at all, as it sometimes fails. He then offers a more concise explanation: reduction to the first figure is needed only to obtain knowledge of the entailment, not for the entailment itself, which is already present. Finally, he defines 'extraneous premise' (muqaddima ghariba) as a premise that is not entailed by the original premises or by one of them, and whose terms differ from the terms of those premises. This is an important technical nuance distinguishing intrinsic logical consequence from extrinsic aid.

Key Terms
اللزوم لذاته
المقدمة الغريبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the entailment in the second figure is self-evident.
B) Because reduction to the first figure is part of the syllogism itself.
C) Because the entailment is intrinsic, though not self-evident.
D) Because the definition was only intended for the first figure.

Key Takeaway
Intrinsic entailment (luzum li dhatihi) means the conclusion follows from the premises in themselves, even if one needs conversion or reduction to see it.

Qiyas al-Musawat: Why Equality Syllogisms Need an Extra Premise
Author explains why qiyas al-musawat requires an external premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawat) requires an external premise and explain why the conclusion does not follow intrinsically.

Core Idea
Equality syllogisms don't prove their own conclusions; they need an external bridge premise.

In this passage, the author challenges the assumption that qiyas al-musawat (syllogism based on equality or transitive relations) is a self-contained valid argument. Through concrete examples, he shows that the desired conclusion does not follow from the premises alone. Instead, the argument only works when an extra premise is supplied from outside. This distinction is crucial for understanding what makes a syllogism truly 'intrinsic' (li-dhatihi).

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under scrutiny
The author is examining the claim that qiyas al-musawat entails its conclusion intrinsically (li-dhatihi). Find the base text statement that he quotes: 'But not for itself, rather through an external premise.'
2
Examine the counterexamples
The author gives two examples: (1) 'Two is half of four, four is half of eight' does not prove 'Two is half of eight' because half of half is a quarter, not half. (2) 'Man is incompatible with horse, horse is incompatible with human' does not prove 'Man is incompatible with human' because incompatibility is not transitive. Notice why the conclusion fails to follow.
These show that without an external premise, the inference is invalid.
3
Recognize the intrinsic consequence
The author explains that if the syllogism were truly intrinsic, its necessary conclusion would be a different statement: e.g., 'A is equal to that which is equal to C' instead of 'A equals C'. This is the literal logical consequence of the premises, not the desired relational statement.
4
Understand the role of the external premise
To get the desired conclusion (e.g., 'A equals C'), an external premise is needed, such as 'Whatever is equal to the equal of C is equal to C'. This premise is not part of the original two premises and must be supplied from outside.
Thus the syllogism is not self-sufficient.
5
Conclude the distinction
Qiyas al-musawat is not a valid syllogism on its own; it is only valid with the addition of an external premise. This distinguishes it from standard categorical syllogisms where the conclusion follows intrinsically from the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١١) قوله: «لكن لا لذاته بل بواسطة مقدمة خارجية»: الاترى انه لا يصح ان يقال في قولنا الاثنان نصف الأربعة والاربعة نصف الثمانية : الاثنان نصف الثمانية ولا في قولنا: الانسان مباين للفرس والفرس مباين للبشر ان يقال: الانسان مباين للبشر»؟ لعدم وجود المقدمة الخارجية المسلمة فيها اذ لا يكون نصف نصف الشيء نصفاً لذلك الشيء ولا يلزم ان يكون مباين المباين مبايناً. فعلم ان صدق القول الآخر في مسألة المساواة واللزوم وغيرهما انما هو بواسطة مقدمة خارجية مسلمة ولو لا هذه لكان اللازم لهذا القياس لذاته فى الاولى الف مساو المساوى ج و في الثانية الف ملزوم الملزوم ج و هكذا في مسألة الظرفية الف مظروف المظروف ج وهو ليس بمقصود. والحاصل ان قياس المساواة اذا كان له مقدمة خارجية مسلمة يلزمه القول الآخر لكن لا لذاته بل بواسطة تلك المقدمة الخارجية و ان لم تكن له مقدمة خارجية لا يلزمه شيء كما عرفت.
(11) His statement: 'But not for itself, rather through an external premise': Do you not see that it is not correct to say in our statement 'Two is half of four, and four is half of eight: two is half of eight,' nor in our statement 'Man is incompatible with horse, and horse is incompatible with human: man is incompatible with human'? Because the external premise is not available here, since half of half of a thing is not half of that thing, and the incompatible of an incompatible is not necessarily incompatible. So it is known that the truth of the other statement in the matter of equality, entailment, and others is only through an accepted external premise. And if not for this, the necessary of this syllogism for itself would be, in the first example: A is equal to that which is equal to C, and in the second: A is entailed by that which is entailed by C, and likewise in the matter of containment: A is contained in that which is contained in C, which is not what is intended. The sum is that qiyas al-musawat, if it has an accepted external premise, entails the other statement, but not for itself, rather through that external premise. And if it does not have an external premise, it entails nothing, as you know.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage explains why qiyas al-musawat (syllogism of equality) is not an intrinsic (li dhatihi) syllogism. The author provides counterexamples: from 'Two is half of four' and 'Four is half of eight', it does not follow directly that 'Two is half of eight' because half of half is not generally half (e.g., half of half of a cake is a quarter, not half). Similarly, from 'Man is incompatible (mubayin) with horse' and 'Horse is incompatible with human', it does not follow that 'Man is incompatible with human' because incompatibility is not transitive. The correct intrinsic consequence for the equality case would be 'A is equal to that which is equal to C' (i.e., A equals the equal of C), which is not the desired conclusion 'A equals C'. To get the desired conclusion, one needs an external premise such as 'Whatever is equal to the equal of C is equal to C'. This premise is not inherent in the original two premises. Thus qiyas al-musawat is not a valid syllogism on its own; it requires an external premise. This distinguishes it from the second, third, and fourth figures, where the entailment is intrinsic even if not self-evident.

Key Terms
قياس المساواة (qiyas al-musawat)
مقدمة خارجية (muqaddima kharjiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that qiyas al-musawat does not entail its conclusion 'li dhatihi'?
A) Because its conclusion is always false.
B) Because it requires an external premise that is not part of the original premises.
C) Because it belongs to the second figure.
D) Because the conclusion is self-evident and needs no proof.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-musawat is not a self-sufficient syllogism; it only yields the desired conclusion when supplemented by an external premise.

Equality Syllogism Requires an External Premise
Author argues that equality syllogism needs an external premise, with objection and response about tawaqquf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an equality syllogism requires an external premise and evaluate whether the conclusion follows.

Core Idea
An equality syllogism alone does not guarantee transitivity; it needs a separate premise to bridge the terms.

In this passage, the commentator explains a critical nuance about the equality syllogism: it does not produce the intended conclusion by itself. He illustrates with examples from numbers and disparateness, and then defends his view against an objection using the concept of dependency. By working through this passage, you will learn to differentiate between a self-sufficient syllogism and one that relies on an external premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Premises
Find the two premises in the first example: 'Two is half of four' and 'Four is half of eight'. Note the relationship: both use 'half of' as the predicate.
2
Compare Intended and Actual Conclusion
The intended conclusion is 'Two is half of eight'. The author says this does not follow. Without an external premise, the syllogism yields 'Two is half of the half of eight' (i.e., two is half of four), which is not the same.
3
Locate the External Premise
The external premise that would make the syllogism valid is something like 'The half of a half of something is half of that something.' In the passage, this is implied as a 'conceded external premise'.
4
Examine the Objection
The objector gives an example about divorce dependency: divorce depends on marriage, marriage depends on consent, but divorce does not depend on consent. The objector claims this shows the external premise rule fails.
5
Analyze the Response
The commentator refines the meaning of 'consent': it refers to consent at the time of marriage, not divorce. Thus, the dependency chain holds: divorce depends on marriage, marriage depends on (that) consent, so divorce depends on that consent. The analogy of roof-wall-foundation confirms the transitivity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «لكن لا لذاته بل بواسطة مقدمة خارجية»: الاترى انه لا يصح ان يقال في قولنا الاثنان نصف الأربعة والاربعة نصف الثمانية : الاثنان نصف الثمانية ولا في قولنا: الانسان مباين للفرس والفرس مباين للبشر ان يقال: الانسان مباين للبشر»؟ لعدم وجود المقدمة الخارجية المسلمة فيها اذ لا يكون نصف نصف الشيء نصفاً لذلك الشيء ولا يلزم ان يكون مباين المباين مبايناً. فعلم ان صدق القول الآخر في مسألة المساواة واللزوم وغيرهما انما هو بواسطة مقدمة خارجية مسلمة ولو لا هذه لكان اللازم لهذا القياس لذاته فى الاولى الف مساو المساوى ج و في الثانية الف ملزوم الملزوم ج و هكذا في مسألة الظرفية الف مظروف المظروف ج وهو ليس بمقصود. والحاصل ان قياس المساواة اذا كان له مقدمة خارجية مسلمة يلزمه القول الآخر لكن لا لذاته بل بواسطة تلك المقدمة الخارجية و ان لم تكن له مقدمة خارجية لا يلزمه شيء كما عرفت. فان قيل : انا لانسلم الكلية فى الأولى فان للتوقف مقدمة خارجية صحيحة مسلمة وهي : إن الموقوف على الموقوف على الشيء موقوف على ذلك الشيء، مع انه لا يصح ان يقال في قولنا الطلاق موقوف على النكاح و النكاح موقوف على رضاء الطرفين، الطلاق موقوف على رضاء الطرفين، الجوازه مع عدم الرضاء عن جانب الزوجة بالاتفاق. قلنا: لانسلم عدم الصحة ، فان اللزوم و التوقف حاصل فيه ايضاً فان المراد من رضاء الطرفين رضاؤهما حين النكاح لارضاء هما حين الطلاق و ظاهر ان الطلاق موقوف على هذا الرضاء ليقع النكاح. ثم الطلاق فانه ازالة قيد النكاح فهذه القضية نظير قولك : السقف موقوف على الحائط و الحائط موقوف على الاساس فالسقف موقوف على الاساس (میرزا محمد علی)
His saying: 'But not essentially, rather by means of an external premise': Do you not see that it is not correct to say in our statement 'Two is half of four and four is half of eight' that 'Two is half of eight', nor in our statement 'Man is disparate from horse and horse is disparate from donkey' that 'Man is disparate from donkey'? Because the external premise that is conceded is absent in them, since the half of a half of a thing is not a half of that thing, nor is it necessary that the disparate of a disparate be disparate. So it is known that the truth of the other statement in the matter of equality, concomitance, and others is only by means of an external premise that is conceded. Without this, the necessary conclusion of this syllogism by itself would be, in the first example, 'A is equal to the equal of C', and in the second, 'A is the concomitant of the concomitant of C', and likewise in the matter of containment, 'A is the container of the container of C', and that is not intended. The upshot: that the equality syllogism, if it has a conceded external premise, yields the other statement, but not essentially, rather by that external premise; and if it does not have an external premise, it yields nothing, as you have seen. So if it is said: We do not concede the universality in the first [example], for indeed for tawaqquf (dependency) there is a correct conceded external premise, namely: 'That which is dependent on what is dependent on a thing is dependent on that thing.' Yet it is not correct to say in our statement 'Divorce is dependent on marriage and marriage is dependent on the consent of the two parties' that 'Divorce is dependent on the consent of the two parties', because of its permissibility with lack of consent on the wife's side by consensus. We say: We do not concede the incorrectness, for indeed the concomitance and dependency occur in it as well. For what is meant by 'consent of the two parties' is their consent at the time of marriage, not their consent at the time of divorce. It is evident that divorce is dependent on this consent so that marriage occurs, then divorce [occurs], for it is the removal of the tie of marriage. So this proposition is analogous to your saying: 'The roof is dependent on the wall and the wall is dependent on the foundation, so the roof is dependent on the foundation.' (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a statement about the equality syllogism (qiyas al-musawat). The commentator explains that this syllogism does not produce the desired conclusion by itself; it requires an external premise (muqaddima kharijiyya). He provides examples: from numerical half-relations and from disparity, showing that without the external premise, the syllogism yields a different conclusion (like 'A is equal to the equal of C') which is not the intended one. He then faces an objection: someone might give an example of tawaqquf (dependency) where the external premise seems present but the conclusion does not hold—divorce depends on marriage, marriage depends on consent, but divorce does not depend on consent because divorce can occur even without the wife's consent. The commentator responds by clarifying the meaning of consent: consent at the time of marriage, not at divorce. Since divorce requires a valid marriage, and marriage requires consent, the chain holds. He draws an analogy: the roof depends on the wall, the wall on the foundation, so the roof depends on the foundation. Thus the objection is refuted. The commentator uses a dialectical method common in Islamic logic: stating a claim, providing examples, anticipating an objection, and answering it.

Key Terms
قياس المساواة
مقدمة خارجية
الموقوف على الموقوف على الشيء موقوف على ذلك الشيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that from 'Two is half of four' and 'Four is half of eight' we cannot conclude 'Two is half of eight' without an external premise?
A) Because the syllogism is invalid in form.
B) Because the half of a half is not a half.
C) Because the middle term is not distributed.
D) Because the external premise is missing.

Key Takeaway
The equality syllogism depends on an external premise to establish transitivity; without it, the conclusion does not follow.

Reduction of Qiyas al-Musawat to Two Syllogisms
Author reduces qiyas al-musawat to two syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decompose a qiyas al-musawat argument into two categorical syllogisms and identify the intermediate conclusion.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-musawat hides a two-step categorical structure.

At first glance, the equality syllogism seems to directly conclude equality from two equality premises. However, as al-Yazdi explains, it actually requires two separate syllogisms. The first produces an intermediate result, and the second uses an external premise to reach the desired conclusion. Understanding this reduction clarifies why all syllogisms are ultimately categorical.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms and premises
In the example, we have three terms: A, B, C. The premises are: 'A is equal to B' and 'B is equal to C'. Note that the external premise is not initially given.
2
Run the first syllogism
From the two premises alone, the first syllogism produces an intermediate conclusion: 'A is equal to the equal of C'. This is a categorical syllogism: All A is equal to B, all B is equal to C, therefore all A is equal to the equal of C.
This intermediate conclusion is not the final aim.
3
Identify the intermediate conclusion as minor premise
The intermediate conclusion becomes the minor premise of a second syllogism. The major premise is an external axiom: 'Everything equal to the equal of C is equal to C'.
4
Combine to obtain the final conclusion
The second syllogism takes the minor premise 'A is equal to the equal of C' and the major premise 'Everything equal to the equal of C is equal to C' to deduce the final conclusion 'A is equal to C'.
Thus, qiyas al-musawat is not a primitive syllogism but a compound of two categoricals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «يرجع الى قياسين»: بيانه انه ينتج لذاته نتيجة فيجعل هذه النتيجة صغرى و المقدمة الخارجية كبرى فينتج النتيجة المطلوبة، مثلاً قولنا الف مساو لـ «ب» و ب مساول «ج» ينتج لذاته اى من غير احتياج الى المقدمة الخارجية ان الف مساو المساوى ج فيضم هذه النتيجة الى المقدمة الخارجية هكذا الف مساو لمساو لـ «ج» و كل مساو لمساو لـ «ج» مساو له خالف مساو ل «ج». (عبد الرحيم)
His saying: 'It reduces to two syllogisms': Its explanation: It produces by itself a result, then that result is taken as the minor premise and the external premise as the major, thereby producing the desired result. For example, our statement 'A is equal to B and B is equal to C' produces by itself, i.e., without need for the external premise, that A is equal to the equal of C. Then one combines this result with the external premise thus: A is equal to the equal of C, and everything equal to the equal of C is equal to it, therefore A is equal to C. (Abd al-Rahim)
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a short explanatory note on the reduction of qiyas al-musawat to two syllogisms. The commentator clarifies that the equality syllogism, when taken alone, only yields an intermediate result: 'A is equal to the equal of C'. This result then becomes the minor premise of a second syllogism, with the external premise 'Everything equal to the equal of something is equal to that thing' as the major, finally producing the intended conclusion 'A is equal to C'. Thus the original syllogism is not an independent form but a combination of two categorical syllogisms. The example uses the logical variables A, B, C to illustrate the process.
